The Perfume of Administration

Chapter 1

CHAPTER 1

Godfrey’s heart, a stubborn and inconvenient organ, continued to beat despite his best efforts to drink it still. He considered this a personal failing, like his inability to finish the cathedral’s gargoyle or to properly court the widow whose lands adjoined his own.

This was the central, unspoken tension at the high table. Sir Bors of the Much-Mourned Liver, a slab of a man whose face resembled a ham that had taken a few knocks, leaned in to spear another slice of the glistening beast. As he did, his elbow nudged the silver chalice before his liege lord, Count Guibert, known to his tax collectors as The Tally. The Count did not flinch at the sloshing of good wine. His gaze, pale and liquid as whey, remained fixed on the third guest of honour, propped upright in a chair they’d brought down from the solar.

The late Countess Isolde.

She was, by any objective measure, having a worse evening than anyone else in the hall. Three days dead of a sudden fever, she had been dressed in her finest azure gown, her hands folded over a jewelled psalter, a wimple artfully arranged to conceal the worst of the greying skin at her neck. The embalmer, a nervous little man from the town who smelled of cloves and regret, had done his best. But a late spring warmth had settled over the castle, a thick, woolly blanket of air that carried the scent of damp stone, woodsmoke, and now, beneath the perfume of rosemary strewn on the rushes and the tang of the roasting meat, a faint, sweet, insistent note of spoilage.

It was this note that Sir Bors, mid-chew, finally identified. He paused, his jowls quivering. He looked from the pig, to the Countess, and back again. A profound philosophical quandary furrowed his brow. He swallowed.

“The rosemary,” he announced to the table at large, his voice a gravelly baritone. “Strong this year. Pungent.”

A murmur of agreement rippled down the tables where the lesser knights and household officials ate. They had been casting sidelong glances at the high table all evening, their mirth and chatter dampened by the presence of mortality in a dinner chair. The Count had insisted. “She presided over this hall for twenty years,” he’d said, his tone brooking no argument. “She will preside over her own requiem feast.” So there she sat, a silent, sagging witness.

The Count’s son, Amaury, watched it all from his seat to his father’s left. At nineteen, he possessed the kind of delicate, almost pretty features that made armourers sigh and his father’s battle-hardened retainers offer him figs instead of daggers. His inheritance was a simmering anxiety. He saw what others refused to: the fly, bold as a papal legate, that had landed on the Countess’s gilded headband and was now cleaning its legs with fastidious glee.

“Father,” Amaury whispered, leaning in. The scent of his mother—or what was left of it, layered over the new, awful sweetness—filled his nostrils. He fought a gag. “Perhaps… perhaps the kitchen has prepared a subtle dish. A fish course. Something that doesn’t… compete.”

Count Guibert turned his whey-eyes on his son. He spoke softly, a dry rustle of parchment. “The pig is a symbol of earthly pleasure, Amaury. Transient. Like the flesh. Your mother understood the juxtaposition.” He took a slow sip of wine, his eyes drifting back to his wife. “She looks thoughtful, don’t you think?”

What she looked was dead. But Amaury had learned the geometry of his father’s grief, a strange, angular thing with no soft corners. “Very contemplative,” he managed.

The feast was supposed to be a celebration of her passage to a better world. The Bishop, a man whose girth testified to a profound belief in the spiritual merit of capons, had said so at length that afternoon in the chapel, his Latin rolling like a tun of ale down a staircase. Now, sweating in his vestments at the high table, he was focusing on the temporal, using a thick slice of manchet bread to mop up the pig’s juices on his plate with sacramental intensity.

The conversation, what there was of it, was a stilted minuet. They spoke of the unseasonable warmth, of the promising green haze on the distant vineyards, of the rumour that the Duke’s favourite hound had bitten a tax collector. It was all foam on a dark ale. Underneath, the uninvited guest made its presence known.

Amaury’s eyes kept straying to the lower hall, to a figure seated near the screen passage. Brother Aloysius, a travelling Franciscan who had arrived that morning seeking alms and been conscripted into the day’s piety. He was a scarecrow in a brown habit, all sharp angles and watchful quiet. Unlike the others, he did not seem perturbed by the Countess. He ate his bread and pottage with a detached efficiency, his gaze occasionally sweeping the room as if taking inventory of souls, and perhaps, Amaury fancied, finding the ledger wanting.

The evening limped on. Tapers guttered in their sconces, casting leaping shadows that made the Countess’s folded hands seem to twitch. A minstrel, hired from the town, launched into a interminable lay about a knight, a lake, and a swan of dubious symbolic value. His voice was a nasal drone, fighting a losing battle against the mounting olfactory evidence.

It was during a particularly wrenching high note that the inevitable occurred.

The embalmer’s art, challenged by the warmth and, perhaps, the Countess’s own lifelong disdain for half-measures, finally surrendered. A soft, sighing sound, incongruously human, escaped her. It was followed by a subtle but definite settling, a slump forward of the torso that caused the jewelled psalter to slide from her grasp and land with a thump on the oak table.

The minstrel faltered. The harp string twanged into silence.

All eyes locked onto the high table. The Countess now leaned at a polite, conversational angle towards Sir Bors, as if about to inquire after the quality of the crackling.

Sir Bors froze, a piece of pork halfway to his open mouth. He stared, cross-eyed, at the grey profile now inches from his own. The sweet smell intensified, becoming a palpable presence.

Count Guibert laid down his knife with precise finality. “It would seem,” he said, his voice filling the crystalline quiet, “my lady wife has finally offered an opinion on the entertainment.”

A stifled snort came from the lower tables, quickly choked into a cough.

“The journey to God’s embrace,” the Bishop boomed, seizing the theological initiative, “is often marked by such… final exhalations. A sign of the soul’s release!” He beamed, as if he had personally arranged the effect.

But the spell of dignified mourning was broken. The reality in the chair could no longer be framed by symbolism. Amaury felt a hysterical laugh bubble in his chest. He saw it all: the grease on his father’s chin, the fly now exploring the Countess’s ear, the sheer, ridiculous theatre of it. We are just meat, he thought, with better tailoring and worse smells.

His father stood. “Brother Aloysius,” he called, his voice cutting through the murmur. The scarecrow friar looked up, his face impassive. “You are a man of simple needs and, I perceive, unflinching nerve. You will accompany my son and Sir Bors. Take my lady wife to the chapel. Prepare her for her vigil. The rest of you,” he swept the hall with his pale gaze, “continue. She would have hated waste.”

The feast resumed, the chatter now louder, frantic with relief. The three men became an island of silence. Sir Bors, with the sigh of a man ordered to storm a particularly malodorous hell-mouth, wiped his hands on his tunic. Amaury rose, his legs unsteady. Brother Aloysius approached the high table, his movements economical. He did not look at the Countess’s face. He simply took one arm, nodding to Bors to take the other.

“Lift from the chair, sirs,” Aloysius said, his voice a dry whisper, like reeds in a breeze. “Not by the limbs. The chair.”

Together, they wrestled the unsettlingly yielding weight of the late Countess from her seat. Amaury took her feet, the embroidered slippers feeling absurdly small. She was heavier than he expected, a sack of settled grain. The sweet-rot smell enveloped them. Bors grunted, turning his head to the side to breathe.

They formed a clumsy, six-legged procession, shuffling away from the light and noise of the hall, into the dark, cooler passage that led to the chapel. The sounds of the feast faded, replaced by the scuff of their boots on stone and their own ragged breathing.

“A unique tribute,” Brother Aloysius observed mildly, as they navigated a corner, the Countess’s head lolling against his shoulder.

“My father’s notions of tribute are… fixed,” Amaury panted.

“He sees the poetry in it,” Bors grumbled from the front, his voice muffled. “I see the need for a stronger embalmer. Or a colder season.”

They reached the heavy oak door of the chapel. Aloysius shouldered it open. The air inside was chill, smelling of old incense, cold stone, and the faint, clean scent of the beeswax candles burning on the altar. In the soft, dancing light, the stark crucifix loomed.

They laid her on the trestle table before the altar that had been prepared earlier, draped in black linen. She settled with a finality that made Amaury wince. In the holy quiet, her state seemed more profound, more insulting. The wimple had slipped, revealing the full, darkening truth of her neck.

Sir Bors crossed himself with gusto. “God rest her. I’ll rest my nose.” He turned and left without ceremony, his footsteps echoing away towards the comparative sanctity of the wine cellar.

Amaury was left alone with the friar and his mother. The silence stretched. He expected prayers, a rite, something to sand the rough edge of the horror.

Brother Aloysius did not pray. He walked to the Countess’s side and looked down at her with the detached curiosity of a gardener inspecting a blighted root. “Three days in this warmth,” he mused. “The organs will be a symphony of putrefaction. The skin will slip like a glove.”

Amaury stared at him, revolted. “Is that your comfort, Brother? A lesson in corruption?”

Aloysius turned his reed-like gaze on the young man. “Comfort is a lie we tell the living. This,” he gestured to the body, “is the truth. The only truth that awaits every soul at that feast, from the Count to the scullion. The pig and the peer, in the end, share more than a table.” He picked up the jar of unguent and linen strips left by the embalmer. “Your father wishes a vigil. You are to keep it?”

“I am.”

“Then watch. Not with your heart, for it will sicken you. Watch with your eyes. See the process. It is the most honest sermon you will ever hear.”

He began to work, not with reverence, but with a brutal, practical efficiency. He repositioned the limbs, tightened the wimple, his thin fingers quick and unflinching. Amaury forced himself to look. He saw the blotches on her hands, the way her mouth had fallen open a little, revealing the dark cave within. This was not his mother, the woman who had sung off-key in the garden, who had fretted over the accounts, who had once slapped a bishop’s nephew for pinching a serving girl. This was a thing. A broken device.

“You speak very freely for a friar,” Amaury said, his voice tight.

“I am a minor brother. My order values poverty. I have extended that poverty to my opinions. They have little value, so I spend them freely.” Aloysius finished his adjustments and stepped back. The Countess looked more arranged, no less dead. “There. She will hold for the night. Perhaps.”

He moved to a shadowed corner of the chapel, sat on a stool, and pulled a small, worn book from his habit. He seemed to recede into the darkness, leaving Amaury alone in the pool of candlelight with the truth.

Amaury knelt on the cold stone, not because he felt pious, but because his legs would no longer hold him. The laughter was gone, replaced by a hollow, ringing terror. The friar’s words echoed. The pig and the peer. He thought of Sir Bors’s greasy jowls, his father’s calculating eyes, the Bishop’s self-satisfied smirk. He thought of his own soft hands, unmarked by sword or plough.

They were all just temporary arrangements of warm meat, waiting for the warmth to fade and the smell to begin. And they spent their brief, feverish interval dressing it up, propping it in chairs, singing to it, arguing over it, loving and hating it, while all the while, underneath the silks and the sentiments, the process was already underway.

He looked at his mother’s face, now composed into a parody of peace. The fly had found her again, tracing a path along her hairline.

Amaury did not pray. He watched the fly. And for the first time in his life, he understood something with a clarity that felt like a blade between the ribs.

Everything was a requiem feast. And the corpse was always at the table.

Chapter 2

CHAPTER 2

Dawn, when it came, was a grey thief, stealing the stark shadows from the chapel and leaving a flat, cheerless light in their place. Amaury’s knees were two points of grinding pain, his spine a column of rust. He had not slept. He had watched the candles burn down to stubs, their light shrinking the circle of revelation until it held only his mother’s face and the relentless, busy fly. The friar in the corner had been a still, dark shape, occasionally turning a page with a soft, dry whisper that was the loudest sound in the world.

The door creaked open. Amaury flinched, the noise a physical blow. It was the embalmer, the clove-scented man, his face pale with lack of sleep and professional anxiety. He scurried in, bearing a fresh basket of linen and herbs. He took one look at the Countess, still holding Brother Aloysius’s brutal composition, and his shoulders slumped in relief.

“God be praised, she’s… settled,” he whispered, as if loud noise might disturb her progress towards liquefaction.

Brother Aloysius unfolded from his corner like a praying mantis. “She has settled into the inevitability of physics,” he said. “Your praise is misdirected.” He nodded to Amaury. “Your vigil is done, young lord. Go. Breathe air that does not taste of the grave.”

Amaury stood, his joints protesting. The world outside his body felt distant, muffled. He walked past the embalmer, who was already fussing with a new jar of unguent, and pushed out into the corridor.

The castle was stirring. Servants moved like ghosts through the gloom, hauling water, banking fires in the great hall to burn off the miasma of last night’s feast. They glanced at him with pity and a faint distaste, as if his night-long communion with death might be contagious. He needed to be away from stone, from smell, from the hushed tones.

He found his way to the inner bailey. The sky was a soiled wool blanket, but the air was fresh, carrying the scent of damp earth, horses, and the distant midden. He filled his lungs until they ached, trying to scour the chapel from his insides.

“You look like a man who’s seen his own entrails.”

The voice came from the stable doorway. Sir Bors stood there, gnawing on a hunk of bread and watching a groom try to coax a bad-tempered destrier into accepting a saddle. Bors’s face was puffy, but his eyes were clear. He seemed to have processed the previous night’s events by digesting them literally, with the aid of several more flagons.

“I feel hollowed out,” Amaury admitted, leaning against the cool stone of the wall.

“First real death, is it? Not a grandparent in a bed, or a knight in a field, all clean and heroic?” Bors took a savage bite. “Mother. In a chair. At dinner. That’s a messy one. Sticks to the ribs.”

“Is that your wisdom? It sticks to the ribs?”

“My wisdom is this: everything does. The good wine, the bad pork, the sight of your mother turning. It’s all just… matter. In here.” He thumped his substantial chest. “The trick is not to let the bad matter poison the good. Your father, now.” Bors chewed thoughtfully. “He’s trying to turn your mother into a symbol. Symbols don’t smell. It’s a neat trick, if you can manage it. I can’t. I’m a simple man. I see a corpse, I think: soon, me. So I have another drink.”

Amaury watched the destrier, a mountain of muscle and malice, stamp its iron-shod hoof. “Brother Aloysius said something similar. That we’re all just meat.”

“The friar’s a morbid little stick, but he’s not wrong. Difference is, he thinks that’s a reason to wear scratchy wool and eat turnips. I think it’s a reason to wear good wool and eat pig.” Bors finished his bread and brushed the crumbs from his tunic. “Your father wants you in the solar. After you’ve… cleaned up.”

The solar was the Count’s sanctuary, a room that smelled of parchment, sealing wax, and the faint, almond scent of the poison he kept for rats. Count Guibert stood at a narrow window, his back to the room, looking out over his lands. The Tally was at work. Amaury could almost hear the calculations: the yield from the demesne fields, the rents from the burghers in the town, the tithe to the Duke, the cost of the impending funeral, the dowry that would now, presumably, not be needed for a daughter.

“Amaury.” His father did not turn. “You kept the vigil.”

“I did.”

“And?”

What was the correct answer? That he had seen the void? That he now understood the frantic, silly dance of every living thing was just a distraction from the inevitable slump? “It was… instructive,” he said, landing on the friar’s word.

Finally, his father turned. His face was pale, but composed, the skin taut over the bones. His grief had been internalized, converted into administrative energy. “Your mother was a practical woman. She would have appreciated the efficiency of last night. A feast that served two purposes: honour and household morale. Waste not.” He moved to the table, where a map was unfurled, held down by a dagger and a ledger. “Her death, while a personal loss, creates certain… realignments.”

Amaury’s hollow feeling intensified. “Realignments.”

“The marriage alliance with Sir Roget’s house. It was to be sealed with your sister, Marie. With your mother gone, the connection weakens. Sir Roget is sentimental. He valued your mother’s counsel. We must reinforce the bond.” The Count’s pale eyes found his son’s. “You will marry his daughter, Lucette.”

The words hung in the almond-scented air. Amaury felt a jolt, not of surprise, but of a profound, weary inevitability. Of course. One piece of flesh removed; another slotted into its place. The great, grinding machine of lineage and land required its fuel. “I have never met her.”

“You have. At the Michaelmas tournament two years past. A small girl with a lisp and a fondness for marzipan.”

A memory surfaced: a child of perhaps fourteen, her face round as a pastry, watching the jousts with wide, anxious eyes, powdered sugar on her chin. A child.

“She is a woman now, or near enough,” the Count continued, as if reading his thoughts. “The contracts are being reviewed. The funeral will be in three days. A week of mourning. Then we will proceed.”

“And if I find I cannot… proceed?”

The Count’s expression did not change, but the temperature in the room seemed to drop. “Cannot? Amaury, you are not a troubadour. You are a heir. Your wants are as relevant as the wants of the castle cat. Less so; the cat catches rats. Your function is to continue the line, secure the holdings, and die at a time that is, God willing, inconvenient for your enemies. The ‘how’ of it is your only domain of choice. You can be a diligent lord, or a negligent one. A beloved husband, or a tolerated one. But a husband and a lord you will be.” He tapped the ledger. “The numbers demand it. Mortality demands it. We fill the gaps, or the world fills them for us with thistles and wolves.”

It was the same lesson as the chapel, just dressed in different clothes. The pig and the peer. The heir and the marriage contract. All matter, being rearranged.

“I understand,” Amaury said, the words ash in his mouth.

“Good. Use this time. Consider your new responsibilities. Avoid the friar. His perspective is… unhelpfully eternal. We must live in the temporal world, with its taxes and its treaties.”

Amaury was dismissed. He wandered the castle, a ghost among the living. He saw two kitchen boys squabbling over a chicken leg with a ferocity that suggested it was the last act on earth. He saw a maid smiling at a guardsman, a flash of real, warm feeling in the grey morning. He saw his sister, Marie, her eyes red-rimmed, carefully stitching a black ribbon onto a sleeve. The machine, grinding on.

He found himself at the chapel door again. He didn’t go in. He stood in the passage, listening to the muffled sounds of the embalmer’s work. The clink of a jar, the snip of scissors, a low, muttered prayer that sounded more like a plea for professional success than spiritual mercy.

“He is using a mixture of arsenic and meadow-sweet. A preservative and a mask. A futile alchemy.”

Brother Aloysius was beside him, having appeared as silently as a shadow lengthening. He held a piece of hard, dark bread, which he offered to Amaury. Amaury shook his head.

“My father is arranging a marriage for me. To fill the gap.”

“Ah. The other alchemy. The mixing of flesh and land to produce more flesh and more land. Also futile, in the long view, but it passes the time more pleasantly than arsenic.” Aloysius took a small, precise bite of his bread. “You are troubled.”

“I am told my wants are irrelevant.”

“They are. So are mine. So are your father’s, though he would burn me for saying so. The only want that matters is the worm’s. And it is patient.” The friar chewed, his sharp Adam’s apple bobbing. “But within that irrelevance, there is a kind of freedom.”

“Freedom to do what?”

“To see. To choose how you meet the inevitable. You can rage, like that horse in the yard. You can numb yourself, like your father, with ledgers. You can distract yourself, like Sir Bors, with a trough. Or you can simply… see it. The whole ridiculous, beautiful, stinking parade. It is the only unique act a man can perform. How he looks upon the mess.”

Amaury studied the friar’s gaunt, calm face. “And how do you look upon it?”

Aloysius almost smiled. It was a thin, crack in his austerity. “As a comedy of the highest order. A play where every actor believes fervently in his part—the hero, the villain, the lover—and none have read the final act, where the stage is left empty and the costumes are eaten by moths. The humour is exquisite, if you have the stomach for it.”

From within the chapel, a sudden, sharp curse from the embalmer, followed by the sound of something heavy and wet hitting the rushes.

Aloysius cocked his head. “Ah. A bladder, I suspect. The comedy turns broad.”

Amaury didn’t laugh. But the cold, sharp edge of his terror began to blunt, replaced by a strange, dizzying detachment. He saw it: his father at the window, tallying. Sir Bors in the stable, chewing. The embalmer fighting a losing battle against fluids. Himself, being married off to a girl who liked marzipan. The fly, tracing its path.

A parade. A ridiculous, desperate, smelly parade, marching towards a cliff they all knew was there.

“What do I do?” he asked, the question not for the friar, not for his father, but for the empty air of the passage.

Aloysius finished his bread, brushing the crumbs from his hands. “For now? You go and meet your bride. Observe the comedy. Play your part. And remember the punchline.”

He turned and walked away, his sandals whispering on the stone, leaving Amaury alone with the sound of the embalmer’s frantic mopping and the first, faint, undeniable chuckle rising in his own chest.

It was a terrible sound. But it was his.

Chapter 3

CHAPTER 3

The town of Montguerre lay in a sulk at the foot of the castle’s hill, a tangle of thatch and timber that smelled, depending on the wind, of baking bread, tanning hides, or the collective effluvia of its eight hundred souls. It was here, three days after the Countess’s decidedly eventful vigil, that Amaury found himself on a mission of morbid commerce.

His father’s efficiency was boundless. The funeral was planned, the bishop re-engaged (for a supplemental fee to address the “unusual atmospheric conditions” of the first service), and now the final touch: a tomb effigy. “Stone does not spoil,” the Count had said. “It is the only reliable legacy. Go to Maître Gilles. He has the contract. Oversee the final measurements. Ensure he captures her… dignity.”

And so Amaury rode through the town’s muddy main street, accompanied by a single, bored man-at-arms who was more interested in the baker’s daughter than in protecting his lord’s heir from the perils of chicken runs and overflowing gutters. The people bowed their heads as he passed, their faces a mixture of genuine sympathy for the loss of a decent enough chatelaine and keen interest in the spectacle of death at the big house.

Maitre Gilles’s workshop was a cavern of dust and half-born saints. Chips of limestone littered the floor like stale cheese rinds. A headless John the Baptist shared a corner with a smug-looking angel whose wing had snapped off. In the centre, dominating the space, was a block of pale Caen stone, and beside it, perched on a stool like a dusty owl, was the sculptor himself.

Gilles was a small man with eyes perpetually squinted from judging planes and curves. His hands, resting on his knees, were a map of white scars. He did not rise for Amaury, merely nodded at the block.

“Young lord. The stone is good. It will hold her for a thousand years, or until the next heresy when they smash it for rubble. Such is fate.”

“My father wishes you to confirm the likeness,” Amaury said, dismounting from the awkwardness of the situation as much as from his horse.

“Likeness.” Gilles spat the word, along with a gob of dust-phlegm. “I work from a death mask. Taken after the fever broke, but before the… settling. It is a face at peace. Whether it resembles the living woman is a matter for philosophers and drunkards. I carve the peace.” He gestured to a cloth-draped object on a bench. “The mask. You wish to see?”

Amaury did not wish to. He felt he had seen the full spectrum of his mother’s transition. But duty was a goad. “Show me.”

Gilles whisked the cloth away. There, in pale, hardened plaster, was his mother’s face. It was eerily smooth, the fever’s lines and the death’s slackness somehow blended into an expression of profound, vacant serenity. It was a face that had never worried over accounts, never laughed at a bawdy joke, never wrinkled in disgust at a bad smell. It was the idea of a face.

“It’s… tranquil,” Amaury managed.

“Tranquil is what people buy,” Gilles said, covering it again. “They don’t pay for a smirk, or a worry-line, or the look a woman gets when she’s just told the cook the pork is under-salted. They pay for eternal peace. It’s my best-selling expression.” He picked up a pointed chisel. “Now, the measurements from the embalmer. The length is set. But the hands. The effigy’s hands will be folded. Your mother’s hands, in life… were they plump? Thin? Did she have a favoured ring? A scar?”

Amaury blinked. He tried to recall his mother’s living hands. They were always in motion: smoothing a tapestry, counting coins, adjusting a veil. He remembered their warmth, once, on his forehead when he was ill. But their specifics? The knuckles, the shape of the nails? They were gone, replaced by the blotched, waxy things he’d seen in the chapel. “I… I don’t know. She had a ring. A ruby.”

“A ruby is a detail. I can carve a bump. I need the truth of the hand. Is it the hand of a woman who spun, or one that only pointed?” Gilles sighed at Amaury’s blank look. “No matter. I shall give her the hands of a virtuous queen. Long, elegant, useless. That satisfies most sons and husbands.”

He turned to the stone and gave it a soft tap with his hammer. A pure, high note rang in the dusty air. “Hear that? Good stone. It will outlast your name, my lord. Outlast this town. Your father is wise. Flesh fails. Stone endures. Or, at least, it rots more slowly.”

Amaury was about to leave, his duty to “oversee” clearly fulfilled, when a commotion erupted outside. Raised voices, the nervous whinny of his horse. He and Gilles stepped to the workshop’s open front.

In the street, his man-at-arms was holding the bridles of their horses, looking bemused. Before him stood a girl of about sixteen, her face flushed with fury and travel dust. She was dressed in a riding habit that had once been fine but was now spattered with mud. Beside her, a harried-looking older knight with the boar’s head crest of Sir Roget on his surcoat was trying to calm her.

“I will not wait at the inn like a parcel!” the girl was saying, her voice clear and sharp, though it did indeed carry the faintest of lisps. “He can see me now, as I am! Road-dust and all! Or is the great Count Guibert’s son too fine to look upon his intended before she is scrubbed and perfumed for his inspection?”

It was Lucette. The marzipan child had grown into a young woman with eyes the colour of a storm sky and a chin that suggested a stubbornness not even sugar could soften.

The older knight saw Amaury and blanched. “My lady, please—”

Lucette followed his gaze. Her eyes locked onto Amaury. She took him in: his fine, unmarked tunic, his clean hands, the doubtless stupid look of shock on his face. She did not curtsy. She planted her hands on her hips, a gesture utterly at odds with the elegant, useless hands Maître Gilles was even now planning to carve.

“So,” she said, the ‘s’ softening to a faint ‘th’. “You are the one. Do you make a habit of lurking in stone-yards, or is this a special outing to commemorate your mother?”

The knight groaned. “My lord Amaury, forgive her. The journey was long, the news of the Countess’s passing… she is overwrought.”

“I am not overwrought! I am informed,” Lucette shot back, not taking her eyes off Amaury. “My father has sold me to your family to shore up an alliance, because your mother had the poor timing to die. And now I am to be dragged up there,” she jerked her head towards the castle on the hill, “to simper and mourn a woman I met once, and marry a boy I do not know. So forgive me if I lack the patience for courtly delays.”

Amaury found his voice. It sounded thin in the dusty air. “My lady. This is… unexpected.”

“Death usually is. For the deceased. For the rest of us, it’s just a tedious rearrangement of furniture.” She parroted the very cynicism that had been festering in his own soul, but hearing it from her, in her clear, lisping anger, made it sound fresh and terrifying.

Maitre Gilles, behind him, chuckled softly. “The comedy writes itself,” he muttered to his stone.

Amaury ignored him. He stepped forward, into the muddy street. He saw the fatigue under her anger, the fear under her defiance. She was a piece of flesh being rearranged, just like him. But she was fighting it. Raging, like the destrier. It was the most alive thing he’d seen in days.

“The castle is in mourning,” he said, trying for some semblance of control. “It is no place for…”

“For a living, breathing reminder of the future?” she finished. “Yes. I imagine it’s terribly inconvenient. Shall I wait here with the headless Baptist until you’re ready?”

The older knight looked ready to faint. Amaury’s own man-at-arms was grinning openly now.

Amaury did something then that he did not plan. He laughed. It was not the hollow, terrible chuckle from the chapel passage. It was a short, surprised burst of air. “Maître Gilles is carving my mother’s effigy. With the hands of a virtuous queen. Would you care to see? It might prepare you for the general atmosphere of… composed unreality.”

Lucette stared at him. The fight seemed to seep out of her, replaced by a wary curiosity. She looked from his face to the dusty workshop, to the terrified knight. “Virtuous queens have boring hands,” she said finally. “My mother’s hands are covered in needle-pricks and are stronger than our smith’s. She says it’s from wringing my father’s neck in her mind.”

“A more honest effigy,” Gilles called out. “But no one would buy it.”

Amaury offered his arm, a gesture that felt absurdly formal in the muddy street. “My lady. Welcome to Montguerre. The comedy, I am told, is exquisite.”

She hesitated for a long moment, then took his arm, her grip firm and unqueenly. “I prefer tragedies,” she said, but she let him lead her towards the workshop, leaving the two soldiers to gape in the street.

As they stepped into the dust-choked world of half-made saints, the first major complication of Amaury’s new life was no longer a theoretical contract or a distant, sugary memory. It was a living, breathing, furious girl with road-dust on her face and a gaze that saw right through the stone, right through the mourning, right to the ridiculous, beating heart of the parade.

And for the first time, the thought of his own inevitable slump did not fill him with dread. It filled him with a strange, urgent need to see what this particular piece of temporary, furious flesh would do next.

Chapter 4

CHAPTER 4

The stone dust tasted of forgotten prayers and poor ventilation. It was the first thing Amaury noticed as he led Lucette into the workshop’s dim belly. The second was that Maître Gilles had not been exaggerating about the hands. The effigy of his mother, half-emerged from a block of creamy Caen limestone, held her own left hand before her face. The fingers were long, elegant, and utterly inert, like a set of expensive spoons arranged for a banquet no one would attend.

“You see?” Amaury said, releasing Lucette’s arm. The formal gesture dissolved in the grit. “The hands of a queen who has never lifted anything heavier than a pious thought.”

Lucette did not approach the statue. She stood just inside the doorway, a slash of grey daylight and road-stained wool against the perpetual twilight of the workshop. Her eyes moved from the effigy to the ranks of other figures—a headless John the Baptist weeping artful tears on a side bench, a sorrowing angel with a chisel-stroke for a mouth, a stack of bland, beatific faces that looked as interchangeable as coins.

“She looks bored,” Lucette said. Her voice was quieter in here, absorbed by the dust and the stone. “My mother says all noblewomen in stone look bored. It’s because they’re not allowed to scratch their noses.”

From behind a shrouded block, Gilles gave a wet snort of laughter. “The girl has the eye. It is the curse of the literal-minded. They see the itch beneath the piety.”

The older knight, Sir Geraint, had crept in after them, his hand still on his pommel as if the Baptist might reattach his head and demand alms. Amaury’s own man, Guilhem, leaned against the doorframe, his grin replaced by a look of plain confusion. This was not how a lord greeted, or failed to greet, his betrothed. This was theatre of a peculiar and unsettling kind.

Amaury felt the peculiarity settle around his shoulders, a mantle more familiar than ermine. He addressed Geraint. “Sir. Convey my… profound apologies to the Lady Eglantine for the state of the welcome. And for the state of the welcomer. The household is in disarray. She will be housed in the east solar, with her daughter, and afforded every comfort.”

“The east solar,” Geraint repeated, his voice thin. “It is… prepared?”

“It is empty,” Amaury said. “Which is a form of preparation. It has a roof. I have been assured of this. See to it.”

It was a dismissal, blunt as a trowel. Geraint’s jaw worked, but he bowed, a stiff hinge of a movement, and backed out, pulling Guilhem with him. The door thudded shut, leaving the three of them in the sepulchral quiet.

“The comedy,” Lucette stated, turning her gaze from the bored stone countess to the living, breathing son. “It is, as advertised, exquisite. Do you always conduct your affairs in charnel houses?”

“Only the familial ones,” Amaury said. “This is the cleanest room in Montguerre. Here, the dead are polite and stay where you put them.”

Gilles emerged, wiping his hands on a rag that seemed to be made of older, dirtier rags. He was a small man, all knuckles and keen eyes, his face a topography of stone-pox scars and amused lines. He looked Lucette up and down, not with a courtier’s appraisal, but with a carver’s assessment of grain and density. “You’ll want a different nose,” he said to Amaury, as if continuing a conversation. “The mother’s is too soft. This one’s is better. It has a claim.”

Lucette’s hand went self-consciously to her face. It was not a noble nose. It was a functional one, straight and definite, with a faint dusting of freckles the road had not washed away.

“The contract,” Amaury said, steering them all firmly from the subject of noses, “specifies my mother’s likeness. Not an improvement upon it.”

“Contracts are for moneylenders and virgins,” Gilles sighed. “Art is for the rest of us. But fine. She will have her spoon-hands and her soft nose, and future generations will look upon her and feel a profound urge to take a nap.” He shuffled back to his block. “You may use the stool. It has three legs. It builds character.”

The stool in question sat before a rough worktable littered with wax models, small maquettes of grief. Amaury did not sit. He was acutely aware of Lucette’s presence, a live wire in the room of grounded stone. She was examining a clay model of a weeping cherub, her fingertip tracing the curve of a fat, tragic cheek.

“You are not what I expected,” she said, not looking up.

“Few corpses are,” Amaury replied. He leaned against the table, the stone cool through his tunic. “What was the expectation? A gallant in a black doublet, sighing over a miniature? A brooding tyrant sharpening a dagger?”

“Something with more volume,” she said. “You are all… edges and quiet. And you laugh at the wrong moments. My mother prepared me for a puppet of protocol. She did not prepare me for a man who keeps company with headless saints and offers tours of his mother’s unfinished death-mask.”

“A failing in her otherwise no doubt exhaustive curriculum.” He watched her. The fury that had animated her in the street had banked to embers, but the heat was still there, a latent energy that made the dust motes seem to dance differently around her. “And you are not what I expected.”

“Which was?”

“A transaction. A sealed letter with hair and teeth.”

She put the cherub down. “I have hair. And teeth. The letter, I fear, is inside me, and it is mostly a list of complaints.”

“Welcome to the ledger,” he said, and the corner of his mouth twitched. “Montguerre runs on them. The Tally’s greatest legacy is that everyone here counts everything. Sheep, sins, soup-ladles. You will be counted, weighed, and found temporarily acceptable.”

“And you?” she asked, finally looking directly at him. Her eyes were the colour of a forest floor, all damp earth and hidden moss. “How are you counted?”

It was the question beneath all questions. He held her gaze. “I am the interim sum. The figure written in pencil, awaiting the final, permanent ink. I am learning the rules of subtraction.”

For a long moment, she said nothing. The only sounds were the distant tap of Gilles’s point chisel and the sigh of the draft under the door. Then she nodded, once, a sharp, accepting dip of her chin. “My mother said you were a poet. She meant it as an insult.”

“The highest compliment my condition allows,” Amaury said. He pushed off from the table. “Come. I will show you to your solar. The one with the confirmed roof. The comedy requires you be housed before the next act.”

“Which is?”

“Dinner,” he said, leading her back to the door. “Where you will meet the rest of the cast. They are, without exception, terrible.”

The east solar did, indeed, have a roof. It also had a vast, soot-streaked fireplace that looked capable of consuming an entire oak, and a tapestry depicting the martyrdom of Saint Sebastian so vigorously that the arrows appeared to be fleeing his body in terror. The room smelled of damp rushes and old incense.

Lucette’s mother, the Lady Eglantine, was discovered within, a thin, sharp woman in travel-stained velvet, directing two terrified-looking housemaids with the precision of a siege engineer. She turned as they entered, and her eyes—a paler, harder version of her daughter’s—took in Amaury in a single, comprehensive sweep that seemed to measure his worth, his hygiene, and his proximity to death all at once.

“My lord,” she said, her voice a dry rustle of parchment. She did not curtsy. It was a magnificent omission. “You keep country hours. We anticipated a greeting at the gate, not a scavenger hunt through a… a mason’s midden.”

“My apologies, lady,” Amaury said, executing a bow that was just deep enough to be correct and just shallow enough to be a commentary. “The business of mortality is time-consuming. I trust the solar is to your satisfaction?”

“It is a room,” Eglantine said, her gaze flicking to the agonized Sebastian. “It has walls. One cultivates low expectations in such times.” Her eyes landed on her daughter. “You are dusty.”

“I have been inspecting art,” Lucette said, her voice flat. “It is a dusty business.”

Amaury felt the strange urge to laugh again. He suppressed it. “Rest. The bell for the evening meal will sound soon. I shall send a guide.” He turned to go.

“A moment, my lord.” Eglantine’s voice stopped him. She took a step closer, and he caught the scent of lavender and a sharper, metallic note—fear, or perhaps just the iron of relentless will. “My daughter is here as a promise against a future. A promise you appear to be treating as a… a philosophical abstraction. I would know your intentions.”

He met her flinty gaze. “My intentions, lady, are to survive the week. Beyond that, the landscape grows foggy. Your daughter is a fact in my new reality. I am still learning the grammar of it. For now, my intention is to not be a bore, and to ensure she is not poisoned at dinner. Shall we start there?”

A flicker of something—not approval, but perhaps a recognition of an opponent not entirely feeble—passed over her face. “A modest ambition. We shall see if it is sufficient.”

Back in the corridor, the stone silence of Montguerre closed around him. It was a different silence from the workshop. Colder, heavier, freighted with the breath of generations who had counted and been counted. He made his way not to his own chambers, but to the Count’s—his—study.

The room was a monument to The Tally’s mind. Parchment scrolls filled cubbyholes, each tagged with wax seals and cryptic notations. Ledgers bound in mould-spotted leather lay in stacks. A massive abacus, its beads yellowed with age, dominated one trestle table. This was the engine room of the estate, and it was here that Amaury faced his first true test of the new world.

He was not alone. A man waited by the hearth, warming his hands. He was perhaps fifty, with the patient, worn face of a donkey and clothes of good, dull wool. He turned and bowed. “My lord. I am Fulk, the steward. I served your father in this capacity for twenty years.”

“Fulk.” Amaury moved to the heavy chair behind the writing desk. It groaned under his weight, a protest from the past. “Report.”

It was not a request for a report. It was an audition.

Fulk did not flinch. He launched into a stream of numbers—tithes from the southern vineyards delayed by rot, the need for a new roof on the granary, the disputed boundary with the de Coursan family that had flared up again (“They’ve moved the marker stone, my lord. Again. It now sits in our best pasture, claiming it has always enjoyed the view.”), the dwindling salt stores, the mood in the kitchen after the cook’s nephew was caught pilfering saffron.

Amaury listened. This was the grammar. Not of love or mortality, but of grain and stone and petty theft. It was numbing. It was essential. He asked questions, short and precise. About the de Coursans. About the cook’s nephew. About the cost of slate versus thatch.

Fulk answered without hesitation. His knowledge was deep, granular, and utterly devoid of poetry. He was a living ledger. After an hour, Amaury held up a hand. “The de Coursans. What do they want?”

“The pasture, my lord. Or the fight. It is hard to tell with them. Old Bernard de Coursan’s pride is a large and hungry beast. It needs feeding.”

“And the cook’s nephew?”

“A fool. But the cook is a genius with a capon. A balance must be struck.”

Amaury leaned back. The chair groaned again. “And what do you want, Fulk?”

The steward’s donkey-face showed its first flicker of surprise. Then it settled into something like cautious respect. “I want the sums to close, my lord. I want the roof fixed before the rains. I want the marker stone put back where it belongs, by cover of night if necessary. I want a functioning house for you to… to inhabit.”

For as long as you are able, hung unspoken in the air.

“See to the roof and the salt,” Amaury said. “Leave the marker stone. And the nephew. For now. We will need a full inventory of the plate and linens by week’s end. The Lady Eglantine will ask.”

Fulk bowed, deeper this time. “At once, my lord.”

As the steward left, Amaury felt a small, hard satisfaction. He had parsed a page of the new grammar. He had made a decision that was not about dying, but about slate and saffron. It was a pitifully small victory, but it was his. He was still staring at the abacus, its rows a stark representation of a life reduced to columns, when the bell for dinner clanged, a dull, bronchial sound that seemed to originate in the castle’s stones.

The high hall of Montguerre was determined to be impressive. Vaulted ceilings arched overhead, lost in shadow. Tattered banners hung from the beams, their heraldic beasts faded to ghosts. The long trestle tables were scarred by generations of knives and tankards. At the high table, on a dais, the chair of the Count stood empty. Amaury took the seat to its left. The Count’s chair remained a void, a monument to The Tally’s enduring presence.

The hall filled with the muted rustle of Montguerre’s lesser nobility and household officials. They were a drab flock, their colours subdued, their conversations a low murmur. They watched, not openly, but from the corners of their eyes, as Lucette and her mother were led to the high table and seated to Amaury’s right.

Lucette had changed into a gown of deep green wool, simple but well-made. It did not suit her. It made her look like a sapling forced into a pot. Her hair, brushed and loosely bound, still resisted order, with strands escaping to frame her face. She surveyed the hall with the same detached curiosity she had shown the weeping cherub.

Then the doors opened again, and the atmosphere changed. The murmur didn’t grow louder; it grew tighter, like a tensing muscle.

Three men entered. They did not walk with the shuffling deference of the household. They strode. The leader was broad and bull-necked, his tunic straining over a barrel chest, his beard a briar patch of grey and black. This, Amaury knew without introduction, was Bernard de Coursan. His two sons flanked him—younger, leaner copies, with the same small, hot eyes.

They made straight for the high table, their boots loud on the flagstones. Bernard stopped before the dais, his gaze sweeping over Amaury, Lucette, Eglantine, and coming to rest on the empty Count’s chair. He did not bow.

“So,” he boomed, his voice filling the hollows of the hall. “The new sum in the column arrives. And brings his… factors.” His eyes raked over Lucette, dismissive and crude.

Amaury took a slow sip of wine. He set the cup down with a soft click that echoed in the sudden quiet. “Bernard de Coursan. Your timing is impeccable. The soup is about to be served. Do you come to dine, or to audit the menu?”

A few stifled gasps from the lower tables. De Coursan’s face darkened. “I come to see the boy who presumes to sit in his father’s shadow. And to ask when the matter of my pasture will be settled. Your steward is a stone himself. Unmoving.”

“The stone in question seems to be the one with legs,” Amaury said, his voice mild. “The one that finds its way into my best grazing land. Tell me, do you feed it? Perhaps sing to it at night?”

One of the sons, the taller one, took a half-step forward. “You mock us?”

“I observe,” Amaury said, shifting his pale, whey-like gaze to the young man. “A habit I inherited. My father observed that a man who steals land acre by acre, stone by stone, is a man who fears a straight fight. Was he wrong?”

The silence now was absolute. Even the kitchen sounds from the screens passage had ceased. Bernard de Coursan’s hands clenched into fists. Here was the clear enemy, not shrouded in mystery, but clad in brute ambition and local grievance. He was the first antagonistic force, naked and direct.

“Your father,” de Coursan spat, “knew the value of a strong neighbour. Knew when to make a bargain.”

“My father,” Amaury said, “is currently providing a banquet for worms. His bargaining days are over. You will remove your stone. You will keep your sheep on your own side of the boundary that has stood for a hundred years. If you do not, I will not send a steward. I will send men with hammers, and they will not stop at the marker stone.”

It was a bluff. He had no men he could trust with hammers, save perhaps Guilhem, who was probably grinning somewhere in the shadows. But the words hung in the air, cold and sharp. He had learned the first rule of this new world: show no subtraction. Not even of spine.

De Coursan stared at him, his chest heaving. He looked at the empty Count’s chair, then back at Amaury, as if calculating the true source of the threat. The moment stretched, taut as a bowstring.

Then, from beside Amaury, a clear, dry voice spoke.

“The soup is getting cold.”

Everyone turned. Lucette was looking not at de Coursan, but at the empty bowl before her, as if this were the only matter of consequence in the hall. “And it seems a pity to waste good mutton broth on a conversation about rocks. Surely, Sir Bernard, your dispute can keep until the morning? Even stones sleep.”

Her intervention was so absurd, so utterly domestic, that it cut the tension like a knife through over-stretched parchment. De Coursan blinked, thrown off his rhythm. He looked from the furious, dying boy to the practical, impatient girl, and something in the calculation broke. With a final, inarticulate grunt, he turned on his heel and stalked to a lower table, his sons following like storm-clouds.

The hall exhaled. Servants scurried in with tureens. The murmur resumed, higher-pitched now, buzzing with the news.

Amaury did not look at Lucette. He kept his eyes on his soup bowl, watching the greasy broth shimmer. “That,” he said quietly, “was either the most foolish or the most brilliant thing I have ever witnessed.”

“It was neither,” she said, picking up her spoon. “It was hungry. And I have heard enough about rocks today to last a lifetime.” She took a sip. “The soup is adequate. The company, less so.”

For the second time that day, Amaury felt the laugh rise, and this time he let it out—a soft, breathy sound lost in the clatter of resumed dining. He looked at her then, this furious, hungry fact in his reality. She had, with a single sentence, identified the enemy and then dismissed him as a nuisance. She had not fought his battle. She had simply refused to acknowledge its primacy over dinner.

It was a weapon he had never considered.

As the meal progressed—a parade of overcooked meats and sodden vegetables—Amaury watched the room. He saw Fulk, nodding slowly from a corner table. He saw the sullen glower of de Coursan and his brood. He saw the careful, neutral faces of the others, waiting to see which way the wind would blow. And he saw Lucette, engaging her mother in a hissed debate about the proper way to darn a tapestry, a debate that seemed to contain volumes of unspoken history and strategy.

He had faced the petty challenge of administration. He had met a potential ally in the stoic Fulk. He had clashed with a clear enemy. And now, as the bland pudding was served, he acquired his crucial tool.

It was not a sword, or a secret, or a map.

It was a perspective.

His own mortality had been a lens focused on a single, inevitable point. It made everything else a blur. Lucette’s perspective was different. She saw the immediate itch, the cold soup, the ridiculous stone. She saw life as a series of tangible, manageable irritations and hungers, not a grand procession towards a marble effigy. In her presence, the grand tragedy of his condition shrank, not to a comedy, but to a problem. One of many. And problems could be met, if not solved, then at least faced with something other than dread.

When the final platters were cleared, he rose. The hall quieted.

“Thank you,” he said, his voice carrying without effort. “The household will assemble in the yard at dawn. There is work to be done.”

It was a nonspecific order, but it felt right. He offered his arm to Eglantine, then to Lucette. As he led them from the dais, past the staring eyes and the still-glowering de Coursans, he felt the strange urgency return. Not to see his own end, but to see the next move in this sudden, complicated game.

He left them at the foot of the stair to the east solar. Eglantine swept up without a word. Lucette paused.

“You threatened a man with hammers,” she said.

“I did.”

“Do you have hammers?”

“Not yet.”

She considered this. “A poet’s threat, then. All imagery, no substance.” A faint, almost-smile touched her lips. “It was still better than the pudding.” She turned to go, then glanced back. “Dawn, you said?”

“Dawn.”

“I shall be there. To observe the… work.”

She disappeared up the stairs. Amaury stood in the torch-lit gloom, the sounds of the hall fading behind him. The key lesson was learned. Mortality was a fact, like a nose or a boundary stone. But life was a series of actions taken in spite of it. The tool he had acquired was the will to act, not as a doomed poet, but as a man with a leaking roof, a thieving cook, and a neighbour who needed to be shown where the stones belonged.

And he had, for the first time, someone to witness the actions. Not to mourn them, but to judge their adequacy. It was a slender thread, but it was taut, and it pulled him forward into the dark corridor, towards a dawn he now, peculiarly, wished to see.

The hook was set. Not in the grand plot of inheritance or death, but in the simple, urgent question: what would they build, or break, when the dawn came?

Chapter 5

CHAPTER 5

The dawn arrived not with a fanfare of light, but with a slow, grey seepage, as if the sky were a sodden wool blanket being wrung out over the land. It found Amaury in the inner bailey, standing on a patch of gravel that was neither mud nor flagstone, a fitting place for a man who was neither poet nor lord. The air smelled of damp stone, woodsmoke, and the faint, persistent sweetness of the midden on the far side of the wall. The smell of administration.

He had expected to be alone with this smell. Instead, the yard was a murmuring hive.

Lucette stood by the well-head, a grey cloak wrapped tightly over her dark gown, watching the assembling household with the detached interest of a naturalist observing an ant colony. She had, as promised, come to observe the work.

The work, however, had preceded him. Brother Benoit, his robe hitched up and tucked into his belt, was directing a trio of sullen-looking stable hands in the stacking of firewood that had been left to rot against the granary wall. His tonsured head bobbed, a pale moon in the gloom, as he issued instructions in a patient, relentless monotone. “Not there, there. The dry ones to the kitchen, the wet to the side for the mortar-makers. Think of it as penance with a practical application.”

Near the gate, Eglantine was a still, dark column. She was speaking quietly with a grizzled man Amaury recognized as Guilhem, the senior huntsman, who was nodding slowly, his eyes on the ground. As Amaury watched, Eglantine passed him a small, heavy-looking pouch from her sleeve. Guilhem’s hand closed over it, and he melted back towards the kennels without a word.

Amaury felt the peculiar urgency of the night before crystallize into a sharp, cold point in his chest. They had not waited for his dawn. They had begun their own.

He cleared his throat. The sound was swallowed by the damp air. He tried again, pitching his voice to carry as it had in the hall. “The household will assemble.”

It was largely already assembled. They turned towards him—kitchen scullions with raw hands, men-at-arms in worn leather jacks, washerwomen with sleeves rolled to the elbow. Their faces were a palette of exhaustion, curiosity, and profound indifference. He saw Perrette, the cook, her arms folded over a vast, flour-dusted apron, her expression one of theatrical resignation. He saw the two de Coursan men-at-arms, leaning against the gatehouse wall, their presence a silent, glowering footnote.

He had planned a speech. Something about shared purpose, the strength of the stone, the honour of the house. The words turned to ash on his tongue. They were not a household. They were a collection of rented loyalties, old resentments, and people simply waiting for the next meal. The only thing they shared was the certainty of their own ends, and the rain.

So he abandoned the poetry.

“The roof of the east solar leaks,” he said. His voice was flat, carrying. “The mortar in the south curtain wall is crumbling. The stores in the granary are weeviled. The de Coursans’ sheep are on our pasture.” He pointed, a series of sharp, unadorned gestures. “You, you, and you—with Brother Benoit. Salvage what wood is sound. You, find the mason’s tools in the undercroft. You, with the steward, take an inventory of the grain, sack by sack. Separate the ruined from the salvageable.”

He turned to Perrette. “From today, meals for all who labour will be drawn from the common kitchen. No separate pots.”

Her jowls trembled. “My lord, the traditions of the—”

“Are currently eating the walls from the inside out. You will comply, or you will explain your substitutions to the Count’s justice.” He had no idea what the Count’s justice entailed, but the phrase had a satisfying, stony heft to it.

He finally looked at the de Coursan men. “You will return to your lord. You will tell him his sheep are to be removed from the west pasture by noon. If they are not, they will be impounded. He may collect them upon payment of a trespass fee. The rate is one silver penny per head.”

One of the men spat into the mud. “He won’t like that.”

“I am not in the business of providing things he will like,” Amaury said. “I am in the business of mending walls. Go.”

They went, their glowers now tinged with a shade of uncertainty. The dismissal had been too clean, too devoid of bluster. A poet’s threat was one thing. A list of tasks and a trespass fee was another.

The crowd dispersed into clots of activity, the morning’s silence replaced by the grudging music of labour: the scrape of wood, the clank of a pail, the low mutter of complaint. Amaury was left standing on his island of gravel. Lucette approached, her steps soundless on the wet earth.

“No hammers,” she observed.

“Not yet. But I have identified several problems that may require them.”

“You sound like a man reading a ledger aloud.”

“I feel like one. Is it adequate?”

She considered the yard. Brother Benoit had one of the stable hands by the shoulder, pointing earnestly at the grain of a warped beam. Eglantine was now walking slowly along the inner curtain wall, her fingers brushing the stone as if reading its flaws. “It is a beginning,” Lucette said. “Adequacy is a moving target. It was, however, better than a speech.”

She moved to join Eglantine. Amaury was about to follow when a small, damp shape detached itself from the shadow of the well. It was the boy from the kitchen, the one who had stared at the dead Countess. His face was pinched with cold.

“M’lord?”

“What is it?”

“The mason’s tools,” the boy whispered, as if sharing a treason. “Old Thibault, he sold ‘em. Last winter. For ale.”

Amaury looked at the group he’d sent to the undercroft. They would return empty-handed. Another problem. “What is your name?”

“Colin, m’lord.”

“Well, Colin. Do you know where Thibault is?”

“Dead, m’lord. Drank himself frozen in a ditch after he sold the trowels.”

So. No tools. Amaury felt the cold point in his chest press deeper. Mortality was not just a fact for poets and countesses. It was a thief who stole your trowels and died in a ditch, leaving you with crumbling walls. “Find Guilhem the huntsman,” he said. “Tell him I need two men with strong backs and weak noses for the midden. We’ll look for old iron.”

The boy’s eyes widened, then a flicker of something—not excitement, but a kind of grim recognition—passed over his face. He nodded and scurried off.

This was the heart of it, Amaury realized. Not the grand confrontation, but this endless, granular negotiation with decay. It was a different kind of verse, written in mud and missing tools. He went to join the women at the wall.

Eglantine’s fingers paused on a stone the colour of a rotten tooth. “Here,” she said. “The heart of it. The mortar is not just crumbling. It’s gone. Washed out by a leak from the parapet walk above, channeled down through this crack.” She looked at Amaury. “It is a simple fix, if you have the lime, the sand, and the will. But it is also a symptom. The wall is telling you a story of neglect. A story written in water.”

“I need a mason,” Amaury said. “And tools.”

“You have a mason,” Lucette said quietly.

They both looked at her. She was gazing up at the crenellations, her profile sharp against the grey sky. “I spent seven years in the household of the Duke of Aquitaine. The first three, I was a companion to his daughter. The last four, I was a prisoner in a pleasant tower. To pass the time, I read. Not just psalters and romances. The Duke had a fine, practical library. Vitruvius. Texts on geometry, on fortification.” She brought her gaze down to meet Amaury’s. “I understand the theory of walls. The practice may differ.”

It was the most she had ever offered of herself. Amaury saw it not as a boast, but as a tool laid on a bench, available for use. A translation of wasted years into useful knowledge. It was the alchemy they all now practiced: grief into watchfulness, poetry into orders, captivity into engineering.

“Can you direct men?” he asked.

“I can direct you. You can direct the men.”

Eglantine’s lips thinned, but she gave a curt nod. “It is better than watching it fall. We will need to inspect the entire circuit. To see how many other stories the stone has to tell.”

The three of them walked the walls. It became a grim procession of diagnosis. Lucette named the flaws: the bulging ventre of a section weakened from behind, the larmier of a gutter stone cracked and spilling its tears down the face, the piètre foundation of the old gatehouse. Her voice was cool, precise. Eglantine translated the ailments into consequences: damp in the storerooms, weakness before a sapper’s pick, a collapsed arch in a generation. Amaury translated those consequences into costs: in silver, in labour, in security.

It was, he thought, a new and terrible form of poetry. A terza rima of impending ruin.

By mid-morning, they had a list. It was not a list of tasks; it was a confession of failure, written in stone and rot. Colin found them as they stood in the shadow of the gatehouse, the boy’s hands black with muck. “We found some iron, m’lord,” he reported. “A broken axe-head. Some nails. No hammers. But Guilhem says he knows a man. A smith in the village who drinks less than Thibault. He could make what’s needed, if he has the iron and the coin.”

“Coin,” Amaury echoed. The word hung in the damp air, another fundamental element, like mortar.

“The purse you gave Guilhem this morning,” he said to Eglantine. “For what?”

“For eyes and ears in the village,” she said, unflinching. “And for a pledge of good faith with the smith. We will need more than his tools. We will need his silence, and his speed.”

“You used my coin for spies.”

“I used our coin for intelligence. The de Coursans will not wait for your mortar to set. They will know of your… ledger-reading… by now. Guilhem’s man will tell us what they say in the tavern. The smith will tell us if they come to buy his loyalty, or his iron.”

Amaury looked from her flinty eyes to Lucette’s calm, assessing gaze. They were not merely witnessing. They were acting. They were building a conspiracy of repair around him. The realization did not warm him; it applied a new, more complex pressure. Their fates were now mortared to his own, stone to stone. Their mortality, and his, were now a shared project.

“We need to see it,” he said.

“See what?” Eglantine asked.

“The source of the coin. The heart of the danger. The tally.” He meant the Count’s treasury, the strongroom. The physical manifestation of the power he was supposed to wield and the decay he was supposed to stem. “I have seen the symptoms on the walls. I need to see the disease in the chest.”

Eglantine exchanged a glance with Lucette. It was the first time Amaury had seen them communicate so directly, a silent, swift transaction of understanding.

“That,” Eglantine said, “is a different kind of inmost cave.”

The strongroom was not in the keep, but beneath the floor of the chapel.

This, Eglantine explained as she led him to the small, dark-paneled chamber behind the altar, was both practical and theological. Practical, because no one would think to look for wealth under the feet of God. Theological, because it reminded the Count that his wealth was, in the end, dust, and only a prayer away from being reclaimed by a higher authority.

“It did not stop him from acquiring quite a lot of dust,” she said dryly. She knelt before a carved wooden panel depicting the martyrdom of Saint Sebastian. The saint’s expression of pious agony seemed particularly apt. She pressed her thumb into the saint’s left knee. There was a click, and a section of the floor, indistinguishable from the others, swung upward on a silent iron pivot.

A smell rose from the darkness: cold stone, old metal, and the faint, sweet-tang of rot that comes from leather left in the damp.

Lucette lit a lantern she had fetched from the sacristy. The flame guttered, then steadied, painting their faces in stark relief. “The steps are steep,” she said, and went down first.

Amaury followed. The stairs were narrow, cut deep into the foundation rock. The air grew colder with each step. At the bottom was a low, vaulted space, no larger than a peasant’s hut. It was not lined with chests spilling gold, as in a minstrel’s tale. There were three iron-bound coffers, a stack of ledger books on a stone shelf, and a series of wooden pegs on the wall from which hung a few sealed parchment bags and two empty sword scabbards.

The central tension of the high table—the corpse versus the pig—was here translated into its financial equivalent. The pressing, immediate need for coin for tools, for lime, for smiths, versus the silent, looming obligation represented by those ledgers and bags.

Eglantine went to the largest coffer. From a chain around her neck, she produced a heavy key. The lock turned with a scream that echoed in the confined space. She lifted the lid.

The lantern light fell on a bed of silver pennies, a layer perhaps two hands deep. Beneath that, Amaury saw the dull gleam of a few silver bars. There was no gold. No jewels. It was a working hoard, the kind meant to pay soldiers and buy grain, not to dazzle or ransom kings.

“The Tally was not a hoarder,” Eglantine said. “He was a circulator. Coin was to move, to compel, to purchase advantage. This is what remains in circulation after his final journey.” She pointed to the ledgers. “Those are the promises. Debts owed to him. Debts he owes. Mostly to the Crown. The bags on the wall are the sealed tallies from the last three years’ rents.”

Amaury walked to the shelf. He opened the top ledger. The script was a tight, spidery hand, every line crammed with figures and notations. He saw the de Coursan name, next to a disputed sum regarding the west pasture, marked with a symbol that looked like a frowning face. He saw entries for the sale of “mason’s tools (set)” to one “T. Vaillant” for a sum that would have bought a lake of ale. He saw regular, draining payments to a factor in Bordeaux for wine, to a mercenary captain for the seasonal guard, to the papal nuncio for indulgences.

It was the biography of the crumbling walls, written in numbers.

“How much is immediately available?” he asked, his voice hushed by the cold and the weight of the numbers.

“Enough to buy your hammers and pay your smith,” Eglantine said. “Perhaps enough to lime and sand for the worst of the wall. Not enough to re-roof the solar. Not enough to settle the larger debts if they are called in. And certainly not enough to raise a force to oppose de Coursan if he chooses to press his claim with more than sheep.”

“So we have enough to begin mending,” Amaury summarized, “but not enough to finish. And our neighbour has enough to wait for us to fail.”

“Yes.”

Lucette had been examining the stonework of the vault itself. “This room is sound,” she said. “The mortar is good. It is the one place he did not neglect.” She turned, her eyes catching the lantern light. “It is a fitting metaphor. The heart of his power is preserved in stone. The limbs of it, the walls that protect it, are left to rot. He cared for the treasure, not the thing that made the treasure possible.”

Amaury closed the ledger. The dust from its pages coated his fingers. This was the ordeal. Not a dragon, not a dark wizard. It was arithmetic. It was the slow, certain erosion of means by necessity. It was mortality expressed in ledger columns, where the final sum was always zero.

“We take what we need for the smith and the mortar,” he said. “We hide the rest. We move the coffers.”

“Hide it where?” Eglantine asked. “This is the hiding place.”

“It is the known hiding place. De Coursan will know of it. If he comes, he will come here first. We need a second. A third.” He looked at the empty scabbards on the wall. “We need to think like a poet again. Not of beauty, but of misdirection.”

A strange light came into Eglantine’s eyes. It might have been respect. “The chapel is too obvious. The solar is leaking. The kitchens are busy.”

“The midden,” Lucette said.

They both looked at her.

“You sent Colin to dig in it for iron. No one looks for silver in a midden. It is the last place a thief or a rival would search. Bury it deep, in a sealed cask, below the active layer. The smell is its own guard.”

It was a brutal, brilliant practicality. It acknowledged the world as it was: foul, layered, and hiding its value in waste. Amaury almost smiled. “A poet’s threat is imagery without substance. This is substance without imagery. I prefer it.”

Eglantine was already calculating. “We would need a night. A small, trusted group. Guilhem. Brother Benoit, perhaps. His penance for the weeviled grain can be the guarding of treasure in shit.”

The plan formed in the cold, dead air. It was not a noble plan. It was not a heroic plan. It was a plan of grim salvage, of hiding the last kernels of grain from the rats. It was, Amaury understood, the only kind of plan that ever truly worked. Love did not conquer all. Mortality did not inspire great art. They simply dictated the terms of engagement, reducing every grand design to a series of filthy, necessary tasks.

“Tonight, then,” he said. “After the household sleeps. We move the heart to the midden. Tomorrow, we begin to mend the limbs.”

He was giving an order again. But this one felt different. It was not a performance for a hall. It was a conspiracy in a vault. He was no longer just a man reading a ledger. He was a man rewriting one, in secret, by lantern light.

The final obstacle was not a portcullis or a guardian beast. It was Brother Benoit’s conscience.

They stood in the sacristy after vespers, the four of them: Amaury, Eglantine, Lucette, and the monk. The plan had been laid out. Brother Benoit’s round face was pale, his hands tucked into his sleeves, clutching at his own wrists.

“To hide the Lord’s tithes and a lord’s treasury in… in filth,” he whispered. “It is a desecration. Of the coin. Of the purpose.”

“The purpose,” Eglantine said, her voice like a blade pared to its essential edge, “is to keep it from becoming de Coursan’s coin, which he will use to buy the loyalty of the priest who replaces you and to fund the raid that burns the chapel to the ground. Is that a holier outcome?”

“It is a deception.”

“It is stewardship,” Amaury said. He found the right words, not poetic, but solid. “The Count charged me with preserving his legacy. Stone and silver. The stone is failing. If we lose the silver, the stone is lost forever. This is not theft. It is burial. Like a seed in dung. It is ugly, but it is how things grow.”

Brother Benoit’s eyes moved from face to face. He saw no piety in Eglantine’s, only fierce pragmatism. He saw no horror in Lucette’s, only calm analysis. He saw, in Amaury’s, not a lord’s command, but a shared burden. The will to act, even in the muck.

“A seed in dung,” the monk repeated slowly. He let out a long, shuddering breath. “Very well. I will stand watch. And I will pray for forgiveness. For all of us.”

That settled, the practical fears emerged. Guilhem, summoned and sworn to silence on his hunting knife, worried about the watch. The de Coursans might have left men in the village to watch the roads. The moon was new, which was good for secrecy, but bad for seeing an ambush.

Lucette addressed this. “We do not use the road. We go through the breach in the orchard wall, cross the lower stream, and approach the midden from the woodland side. The path is used by foxes and poachers. It will be muddy, but quiet.”

“You know it?” Amaury asked.

“I walked it. The day after I arrived. To know my boundaries.”

She had been mapping her prison. Now, she was charting their escape route. Her captivity had yielded another tool.

The last fear was voiced by no one, but it hung in the air, thicker than the incense from the chapel. What if they were wrong? What if this desperate, filthy gambit was the last, foolish act of a doomed household? What if the true ordeal was not the moving of the treasure, but the moment after, when they stood empty-handed in a reeking pit, with nothing left to do but wait for the end?

They did not speak it. To give it voice would be to make it a player in the game. So they let it hang, a silent fourth in their conspiracy.

As the castle settled into the deep, watchful silence of the night, they gathered again in the chapel. The coffers were lighter, their immediate needs removed and hidden in a sack in Amaury’s chamber. The remaining silver was divided into three heavy, oilcloth-wrapped bundles. They were a sober, grim group. Guilhem and a one-eyed poacher named Pons he vouched for carried the bundles. Brother Benoit carried the lantern, its shutter mostly closed. Eglantine led, Lucette guided from the rear.

They slipped from the chapel door into the curtain wall’s shadow, a procession of ghosts. The orchard wall had a slump in it, disguised by a thicket of bramble. They squeezed through, one by one, the thorns catching at cloaks like pleading fingers. The stream was icy, swift with spring rain, and Pons lost his footing on the slippery stones, going in to his knee with a choked curse. The bundle was kept dry.

Then into the woods. Lucette moved without sound, a shadow among shadows. The path was a faint suggestion, a memory of passage in the mulch. The smell of the castle midden found them before they saw it—a complex, overwhelming perfume of decay that made the eyes water.

The midden was a vast, layered mound at the far corner of the outer bailey wall, where the prevailing wind carried the stench away from the living quarters. In the near-total dark, it was a black mound against a slightly less black sky.

This was the entrance. The inmost cave. Not a dragon’s lair, but the collective gut of the household, the place where everything ended up. The final obstacle was their own revulsion.

Guilhem had brought two spades, hidden that afternoon. He and Pons began to dig, at a spot Lucette indicated, well away from the fresh deposits, where the older, compacted waste would provide a secure bed. The sound of the spades biting into the dense, fibrous mass was wet, thick, obscene. Each shovelful released a fresh wave of putrid gas.

Amaury stood guard, his back to the diggers, his eyes straining into the wood. He heard Eglantine’s steady breathing beside him. Heard Brother Benoit’s whispered, frantic psalms. Heard the grunt of effort, the squelch of corruption.

He thought of the dead Countess at the high table. This was her true counterpart. Not the elevated, dressed corpse, but this anonymous, communal decay. This was where all feasts ended. This was the ultimate tally. The poetry of it was almost too perfect, too cruel. The great wheel of being: from silver coin to purchased pig, from roast meat to consumed feast, from excrement to midden, where the coin was buried to begin the cycle again.

Love was not here. Mortality was the only principle, the great composter of all things.

After an eternity of digging, Guilhem hissed, “Deep enough.”

The bundles were lowered into the earth’s foul stomach. The soil and waste were packed back over them. Pons scattered leaf litter and a broken basket over the spot. It was done.

They retreated the way they came, each of them carrying the stench deep in their woolens, in their hair, a badge of their secret. In the chapel, they stood looking at each other in the flickering light of a single candle. They were no longer a poet, a widow, a prisoner, and a monk. They were co-conspirators in a crime against entropy. Their faces were pale, smudged with dirt and something else.

“We have hidden the heart,” Eglantine said, her voice raw. “Now we mend the body.”

Amaury nodded. He felt empty. Cleansed of poetry, of pretense, and now of the last illusion of security. The tool he had acquired was no longer just the will to act. It was the knowledge of what action cost. It was the smell of the midden on your hands, the cold of the stream in your bones, the weight of a secret in your chest.

He left them there. He walked out into the main bailey. The castle around him was a silhouette of lack, of missing mortar, of leaking roofs. But he saw it differently now. It was not just a problem. It was a testament. It was what stood between the hidden, filthy seed and the waiting world.

He looked east. The sky was not yet light, but the blackness had a thin, porous quality. Dawn was again a distant rumour. He had wished to see it before. Now, he needed to see it. He needed its grey, indifferent light to fall on the walls, so he could see clearly what was to be done.

The hook was no longer a question of what they would build. It was a colder, sharper thing. They had buried their hope in shit. Would it rot there, or would it, against all reason, take root?

Chapter 6

CHAPTER 6

The dawn, when it finally came, was the colour of a bruise left by a mailed fist. It did not break so much as it seeped, a slow, greyish-purple stain spreading across the underbelly of the sky. Amaury stood in the inner bailey, the cold stone of the keep at his back, and watched it bleed.
