The smell of the midden was a ghost in his nostrils, a persistent whisper beneath the sharper scents of wet stone and woodsmoke. It was the perfume of his new estate. He was no longer a man who contemplated lack; he was a man who buried hope in excrement and waited to see what would grow.

The castle, in this bruised light, was a catalogue of wounds. The gap in the curtain wall was a missing tooth in a beggar’s grin. The thatch on the barracks was a sodden, rotting scab. But Amaury saw the sinew beneath the sores, the stubborn architecture of defiance. It was a testament. And like all testaments, it demanded a witness who understood the cost of the ink.

He found Guilhem by the well, drawing water for the garrison’s thin morning gruel. The sergeant’s movements were efficient, brutal, the rope burning through his calloused palms without complaint. He did not look up as Amaury approached.

“The men are talking,” Guilhem said, his voice a low grind of stone on stone. “They say the ghost of the Countess walks the upper gallery. That she weeps for her stolen jewels.”

“Let them talk,” Amaury said. “A weeping ghost is less trouble than a thirsty soldier.”

Guilhem straightened, his eyes, small and dark as river pebbles, fixing on Amaury. “They also say the new master walks at night with a shovel. That he consorts with the widow and the mad monk in the chapel. That they are brewing something unholy.”

Amaury met his gaze. The fear here was not of phantoms. It was a simpler, more terrestrial fear: the disruption of a fragile order. “We are brewing survival, Sergeant. It is often an unholy business.”

“Survival has a shape,” Guilhem countered. “It is walls, and spears, and full granaries. Not secrets in the dark.”

“What we buried last night is a seed,” Amaury said, the metaphor rising to his lips, tarnished but necessary. “A seed does its work in the dark. In secret. It is the original conspirator.”

Guilhem spat into the dust by the well-coping. “Seeds need good soil. Not… what you used.”

“It was the soil we had.” Amaury’s voice was flat. “The question is not the quality of the earth, but the ferocity of the sprout. We will need men to guard the wall today. The real one. Not the phantom in the gallery.”

He left Guilhem to his water and his suspicions. They were both tools, of a sort. Distrust could be a whetstone.

The hall was a cavern of lingering night. The hearth held only grey ash, the great table was bare save for the ghost of last week’s feasts—a greasy smear, a forgotten crust hard as mortar. At the high table, sitting alone as if awaiting a tribunal, was Eglantine. She was still in the same gown from the night’s digging, the wool at the knees stained a dark, earthy brown. In her hands she turned a small, sharp knife—a bodkin for stitching leather, or perhaps, Amaury thought, a heart.

“He will not hold,” she said, without preamble. Her voice was stripped of its earlier rawness, now cold and clinical as a surgeon’s blade. “Pons. The silence is a weight on him. He bends beneath it. He mutudes to the air, to the walls. He asked a patch of lichen on the chapel stone for forgiveness.”

Amaury took a seat beside her, not at the head of the table. That chair, Count Guibert’s chair, remained empty. A monument to a different kind of absence. “What does he say?”

“He speaks of betrayal. Of the body as a temple defiled. Of the soul’s anchor being cut loose.” She laid the bodkin flat on the wood. “He is not wrong. But his truth is a lit candle in a room full of straw. It will burn us down.”

“We need him,” Amaury said. “He knows the body. He has… access.”

“He is a crack in our vessel,” Eglantine said. “And we are sailing into a storm.”

“What storm?”

She finally looked at him. The grief that usually pooled in her eyes had been boiled away, leaving behind a crystalline hardness. “The Tally is not a fool. He is a man who believes in balances. His wife is dead. A political asset, removed from the ledger. He will seek a credit. He will come, or he will send his eyes. And his eyes will see a crumbling wall, a fearful garrison, a muttering monk, and a widow who does not mourn correctly.” She tapped the bodkin’s point against the table. Tap. Tap. Tap. “We have hidden the heart. But the body we must mend is not just hers. It is this.” She gestured to the hall, the castle, the entirety of their crumbling, stinking world. “And a body under inspection cannot be seen to be… adulterated.”

The mortal threat was not a sword, not yet. It was a gaze. The cold, whey-pale gaze of The Tally, settling on their works and finding them wanting. It was the fear of exposure, which was, in its way, a kind of death.

“Then we give him a show,” Amaury said, the idea forming like a cyst, painful and necessary. “A pageant of correct mourning. A spectacle of orderly decay.”

Eglantine’s smile was a thin, bloodless line. “A feast for the eyes, to distract from the taste of the meat.”

“We have the corpse,” Amaury said, the words foul in his mouth. “We have the grieving court. We have the rites. We will stage a funeral so convincing, so dripping with pious sorrow, that it will be a wall he cannot see past.”

“And Pons?” she asked. “He must preside. He must anoint, and chant, and lower her into the consecrated ground. Can he do that? Knowing what lies in the midden?”

“He must,” Amaury said. “It is the final stitch. The suture that closes the wound for good.”

He went to find the monk, the weight of the coming performance already settling on his shoulders, a role he had not sought: director of a requiem for a hollow god.

He found Pons in the herb garden, a forlorn square of wilted greenery behind the chapel. The monk was on his knees, but he was not praying. He was digging, his fingers clawing into the damp soil around the roots of a sage bush, his breath coming in ragged, sobbing gasps.

“It’s not here,” Pons whispered to the earth. “It’s not here, it’s not here, it’s not…”

“Brother Pons,” Amaury said, his voice quiet.

Pons flinched as if struck. He scrambled back, sitting in the dirt, his hands black with loam. His face was a landscape of torment, his eyes wide wells of panic. “You. You made me a grave-robber. A body-thief. I have taken the pith from the vessel. I have left a shell for the worms, while the fruit… the fruit…” He could not say it. His gaze darted towards the castle’s lower bailey, towards the midden.

“We saved it from the fire,” Amaury said, crouching down to his level. The smell of turned earth and crushed sage was clean, but it could not mask the deeper stench on them both. “From the utter annihilation your Church promises to the sinful flesh. Is that not a mercy?”

“Mercy?” Pons barked a laugh that was mostly a choke. “You speak like the serpent. It is a perversion. The body is a sacred whole. To rend it… it is an offence against God’s architecture. We have built a heresy in the dark!”

“We have built a secret,” Amaury corrected, his patience a thin wire. “A small, hidden truth. The world is made of them. Is the Trinity not a mystery? Is the Eucharist not a transformation you cannot see? This is no different. It is faith in a different shape.”

“Do not theologize to me, poet,” Pons snarled, the word an accusation. “You trade in pretty lies. This is a foul, physical truth. I can feel it… there.” He clutched his own chest. “A coldness. A missing weight. As if my own soul is unmoored.”

This was the confrontation, not with steel, but with a cracking mind. Amaury saw it clearly. Pons’s fear was the mortal threat. It was the loose thread that, pulled, would unravel their entire shroud.

“Then you must perform the rite,” Amaury said, his voice dropping, becoming conspiratorial, intimate. “You must make the shell whole again in the eyes of God and man. You must give her the blessing, the holy water, the committed earth. You must finish it. Only then will the architecture be restored. Only then will the vessel be properly sealed, its contents… elsewhere… sanctified by the beauty of the ceremony. You are not a thief, Brother. You are a translator. Moving a sacred text from a vulnerable scroll to a hidden, safer codex.”

He was weaving a poem for a madman, a metaphor to cage his guilt. Pons stared at him, his breathing slowing. The idea was a lifeline, and the monk, drowning in literalism, clutched at it.

“A translation?” he whispered.

“The finest of your career,” Amaury said. “A funeral mass for a queen. You will chant the Dies Irae until the vaults shake. You will anoint her with oils so precious the air will thicken with their scent. You will make her death a masterpiece. And in doing so, you will bless what we have done. You will make it… liturgically sound.”

He saw the struggle in Pons’s face—the rigid dogma warring with the desperate need for absolution. The need won. It was a small, silent death. The death of the monk’s uncompromised conscience. He nodded, once, a brittle, mechanical motion.

“The mass,” Pons said, his voice hollow. “It must be flawless.”

“It will be,” Amaury promised.

He left Pons in the garden, already composing the liturgy in his head, a man now building a beautiful, gilded cage for his own complicity. The first scene was set. The players were in place. The stage was a chapel, the prop a corpse, the audience a world that craved a satisfying ending.

The funeral of Countess Isolde was a masterpiece of grim theatre.

They dressed her in the finest they could muster—a gown of faded blue samite, a mantle trimmed with fox fur that was mottled with moth-bites. They placed her on a bier in the chapel, her hands folded over a wooden crucifix, her face veiled in yellowing lace. Candles, every one they could find, were lit. Their light did not warm; it revealed. It glinted on the silver thread of her gown, on the polished wood of the coffin, on the waxy, sunken topography of her cheeks beneath the lace.

The castle’s entire population was pressed into the pews. The men-at-arms, smelling of sweat and rust. The scullions and kitchen drudges, wide-eyed. The few remaining courtiers, like faded, startled birds. Guilhem stood at the back, his face a helmet of stone. Eglantine sat in the front row, a black veil over her face, her posture a perfect sculpture of bereavement.

Amaury stood beside her, playing the role of the new, somber castellan. He watched Pons.

The monk was transformed. Or perhaps, Amaury thought, he had finally snapped into the version of himself the role required. He moved before the altar in his vestments, which were clean but threadbare, like banners from a long-forgotten war. His voice, when he began the Latin, was not the muttering, panicked thing from the garden. It was a clear, cold bell, ringing in the cramped stone space. He chanted the psalms of lamentation with a terrifying intensity, each syllable a hammer blow on the nail of her coffin. He swung the censer until the air was a visible, blue-grey fog, the smell of frankincense so thick it became a taste, a cloying sweetness that fought a losing battle against the deeper, colder scent of mortality that seeped from the bier.

It was during the Dies Irae that the performance reached its crescendo. Day of wrath, that day will dissolve the world in ashes… Pons’s voice cracked not with weakness, but with a fervor that bordered on fury. He was not just describing the terror of judgment; he was prosecuting it. His pale eyes swept the congregation, and each man and woman flinched as if accused. He was holding a mirror to their own rot, their own secret middens, and in that moment, the carefully staged funeral became something real, something terrifying. The corpse was no longer a prop; it was an indictment.

Amaury felt a cold admiration. Pons was using his breaking mind as a tool, channelling his guilt into a performance of such seismic piety that it would brook no questioning. This was the battle, and the monk was fighting it with the only weapons he had left: fire, smoke, and doom.

Then, as the final notes of the chant faded into the smoky air, a new sound intruded.

The slow, rhythmic clop of hooves on the cobbles of the outer bailey. Not the ragged noise of a single rider, but the ordered, heavy tread of many. A metallic jingle of harness and mail.

Guilhem, at the back, turned his head slightly. Eglantine’s veiled form went perfectly still. The congregation, already primed by Pons’s fire and brimstone, stirred like a field of grass before a storm.

The great oak door of the chapel, left ajar for the somber procession to follow, was pushed fully open.

Against the flat grey light of the afternoon stood a figure on horseback. He was clad in a brigandine of boiled leather studded with iron, a plain, unadorned sword at his hip. His face was narrow, sharp, bisected by a blade of a nose. His eyes were the colour of old ice. He did not enter. He simply sat his horse in the doorway, a silhouette blocking the exit, and surveyed the scene.

Count Guibert’s seneschal. The Tally’s most trusted eye. His name was Renaut, but the peasants called him The Ledger.

The funeral, the beautiful, terrifying pageant, froze. The mortal threat had arrived, not as a question, but as a silent, observing presence. The ‘death’ was not a blade in the gut. It was the cold scrutiny of the real world, intruding upon their fragile fiction. Pons, at the altar, faltered. The Latin died on his lips. His eyes, wide with spent fervor, locked on the intruder. The spell was broken. The crack in their vessel yawned wide, and the cold light of accounting poured in.

For a long moment, the only sound was the drip of wax from a hundred candles and the restless shift of Renaut’s horse.

Then, Amaury moved.

He did not stand. He uncoiled, rising from the pew with a slow, deliberate grace that belied the frantic hammering of his heart. He turned to face the doorway, placing his body between Renaut and the bier, between the seneschal’s gaze and Eglantine’s veiled face.

“You interrupt the office of the dead, sir,” Amaury said, his voice not loud, but carrying in the stunned silence. It was layered with a poet’s precision, a touch of affront, a veneer of authority thinner than the gold leaf on a reliquary.

Renaut’s ice-chip eyes slid from the corpse to Amaury. He did not dismount. “My lord Count Guibert sends his condolences. And his inquiry. The Lady Isolde’s passing was… sudden. He wishes assurance that all rites are observed. And that her possessions are duly inventoried.”

The inventory. The true purpose. The Tally was already balancing his books.

“The rites are being observed with all devotion,” Amaury said, gesturing towards Pons, who stood paralysed at the altar like a saint in a niche. “As for her possessions, they await the Count’s instruction in her solar. Untouched.” This was a gamble. They had touched everything. They had searched for the heart, for clues, for anything of value or meaning.

Renaut’s gaze swept the chapel again, a slow, calculating arc. It took in the shabbiness of the congregation, the moth-eaten finery of the corpse, the desperate, candle-lit piety. It was the gaze of a man assessing livestock. Or collateral.

“My men will require quarters,” Renaut stated. It was not a request. “And fodder. We will attend the interment. And then, we will review the inventory.”

Defeat was a cold stone in Amaury’s belly. They were to be overseen. Watched as they buried their empty shell. Their secret, so recently interred in filth, was now to be interred in earth under the eyes of their enemy. The fiction would hold only if every step was perfect. And Pons was a shattered glass, held together by will and incense smoke.

“The castle is at your disposal,” Aglantine said, her voice emerging from behind her veil, calm and drained. The grieving widow, accepting the inevitable. “My husband’s hospitality, though he is absent, extends to his lord’s servants.”

Renaut gave a curt nod, finally swinging down from his saddle. His boots, good leather, scraped on the stone flags. The sound was the closing of a trap.

The procession to the burial ground in the small plot outside the walls was an agony. Pons led, chanting again, but his voice was a reedy, broken thing. The pallbearers—Guilhem and three of his most stolid men—carried the coffin. Renaut and six of his own men, clean and hard-looking, walked directly behind, a phalanx of silent auditors. Amaury and Eglantine followed, the bereaved. The rest of the castle trailed like a ragged, mournful shadow.

The earth by the chapel wall was already turned, a neat, rectangular mouth. Guilhem had seen to it. As the coffin was lowered, Amaury watched Pons. The monk held the aspergillum, the holy water sprinkler. His hand trembled so violently the water fell not in a gentle rain, but in frantic, scattered drops, like the tears of a panicked child. He began the final prayer. “In paradisum deducant te Angeli…”

His voice hitched. He stared into the grave, at the box containing their lie. His lips moved, but no sound came out. The silence stretched. Renaut’s men exchanged glances. The crack was widening, becoming a chasm. Pons was going to betray them all with his silence, to die his second death—the death of his nerve—here at the graveside.

Then, Eglantine stepped forward.

She moved to the very edge of the grave. She tore the veil from her face. Her features were pale, stark, beautiful in their utter desolation. She was not performing now. She was channeling something older, something raw. She looked at Pons, and then she began to sing.

It was not a Latin hymn. It was a lament in the old tongue, a peasant’s dirge, its melody a winding, aching thing that spoke of fallow fields and empty cradles. Her voice, unadorned and clear, cut through the cold air. It was the sound of love, not for the woman in the box, but for the idea of loss itself. It was mortality, given a melody.

The effect on Pons was instantaneous. The human sound, the shared, ancient grief, broke his paralysis. He blinked, as if waking. He looked at Eglantine, and something in him settled. He raised the aspergillum again, his hand steadier, and joined his Latin prayer to her vernacular song. The two voices, the sacred and the profane, the liturgical and the visceral, wove together over the grave. They did not harmonize. They coexisted, two truths laid over one deception, making it stronger, more real.

Renaut watched, his expression unreadable. But his men were no longer glancing at each other. They were watching the widow, some with pity, others with a kind of grim respect. The spectacle had transcended the staged. It had become genuinely moving. It was the clever trick, the aid from an ally—not with a weapon, but with a song. Eglantine had snatched their victory from the jaws of defeat by exposing a deeper, truer heart.

The final dirt was thrown. The grave was sealed. Renaut turned away, his audit of the burial complete. “The solar,” he said to Amaury, his tone leaving no room for mourning. “Now.”

The solar was Isolde’s ghost, preserved in dust and faded fabric. Renaut moved through it like a tax assessor. He opened chests, noted tapestries, counted silver spoons with a dispassionate eye. A scribe with him noted everything on a wax tablet, the stylus scratching like a rodent’s teeth.

Amaury stood by the cold hearth, waiting for the question. It came as Renaut lifted a small, inlaid jewellery box. It was empty.

“Her personal jewels?” Renaut asked, not looking up.

“She was not wearing them at her passing,” Eglantine said, her voice still hoarse from the song. She had followed them, the widow insisting on her duty. “They are not here. Perhaps she gave them to the Church. Or to a servant. She was… generous in her piety.” The lie was smooth, layered with plausible virtue.

Renaut’s ice-eyes flicked to her. “Generosity is a debit. It must be accounted for.” He closed the empty box. “We will interview the household.”

This was the danger. Pons, under pressure, might break. A servant, hoping for favour, might whisper of night-time shovel work.

“Of course,” Amaury said. “But the sergeant, Guilhem, can attest to the discipline of the men. And Brother Pons… as you saw, the Lady’s death has unmoored him. His mind is on higher things. Earthly inventories pain him greatly.”

He was directing Renaut’s suspicion, shaping it. Painting Pons as a holy fool, not a conspirator. Renaut studied him for a long moment. The seneschal was a man who believed in what he could count, what he could see. He had seen a convincing funeral. He had seen a distraught monk and a beautifully grieving widow. He had seen a crumbling castle, but one that was observing the proper forms. The mystery of the missing jewels was a small, messy entry in the ledger, but the overall balance—the appearance of order—seemed to hold.

Finally, Renaut nodded. “We will stay the night. Ensure your granary is secure. My lord the Count will send further instructions regarding the disposition of this holding.” His gaze settled on Amaury. “You are the castellan. The order, and the lack of it, is yours. See that the columns remain straight.”

It was not approval. It was a provisional entry. They had passed the inspection, but they were now on the books. The reward was not safety, but a stay of execution. It was the knowledge that their secret was still buried. And it was the object of Renaut’s thinly-veiled threat: their continued tenure, their fragile authority, earned by surviving the ordeal of his gaze.

As Renaut and his men left the solar to take their imposed quarters, Amaury let out a breath he felt he’d been holding for hours. He looked at Eglantine. The stark grief had left her face, replaced by a weary, granite determination.

“We have mended the body,” she said softly. “For now.”

“He will be back,” Amaury said. “Or The Tally himself will come. With a full audit.”

“Then we must make the columns very straight indeed,” she replied. “We must make this ruin profitable. Or at least, convincingly potential.”

They had survived the confrontation, the near-defeat, and snatched a victory. They had their reward: another day, another chance to build the walls higher. But as Amaury looked out the solar window, he saw Renaut’s men in the bailey, their efficient movements a foreign, hostile organism in the castle’s sluggish blood. The hook was no longer the abstract question of growth from filth. It was a concrete, pressing calculus.

They had hidden the heart. They had mended the body. Now, they had to animate the corpse of Isolde’s Hold with a performance of vitality so convincing it would generate its own truth. They had to make the barren field appear fertile before the landlord returned to claim his rent.

Love was not here. Mortality was the only principle. But survival, Amaury was learning, was a kind of love—a fierce, desperate love for the next breath, for the next sunrise, for the hidden, filthy seed against all reason pushing its pale shoot towards the indifferent light.

Chapter 7

CHAPTER 7

The elixir was a ledger.

It lay on the solar’s scarred table between them, its leather cover the colour of a dried bruise. It was not the grimoire Amaury had half-feared, nor a chest of coin he had half-hoped for. It was Countess Isolde’s private accounts, the one document Renaut’s men had not found because it had been bricked into the alcove behind the loose stone Eglantine had shown him, her fingers tracing the mortar like a blind woman reading a face. The reward for surviving the inspection was not gold or power, but knowledge. The knowledge of how a corpse had once breathed.

Amaury opened it. The vellum pages crackled with a sound like distant fire. The script was a sharp, disciplined hand, each column ruled with geometric severity, each entry a stark notation of loss.

To Brother Martin, for masses for the soul of my father, Humbert: 12 silver pennies. To the farrier, for the shoeing of the palfrey ‘Grey-Song’, deceased of a burst heart, Michaelmas last: 8 pennies. For seed corn, purchased from the merchants of Lagny, spoiled by the autumn rains: 3 shillings, 4 pennies. A loss. To the mason, Regnaud, for the repair of the south curtain wall where the mortar wept. Paid in ale and salt pork, the coin being short.

It went on, page after page. A chronicle of entropy. Every entry was a tally of expenditure against a diminishing return, a investment in a sinking ship. The Countess had not been a poor steward; she had been a meticulous chronicler of her own decline. Here was the cost of masses for the dead, the maintenance of walls that crumbled faster than they could be repaired, the purchase of seed that rotted in the granary. It was the autopsy of a holding.

“This is our treasure?” Amaury’s voice was flat. “A list of failures?”

Eglantine’s finger, calloused and blunt, tapped an entry near the back. “Look at the totals. The quarterly renders to The Tally.”

Amaury looked. The sums sent to Count Guibert were precise, unvarying. They did not fluctuate with the harvest, or the murrain, or the death of a valuable horse. They were a constant drain, a leech attached to the neck of the estate, drinking its vitality regardless of the patient’s health.

“He took his due,” Eglantine said. “Always. The field could be barren, the herds thin, the walls dust. His tally was immutable. She sold plate, timber rights, her mother’s jewels… see here… to make the columns match. To keep the entry clean.”

Amaury understood. The ledger was not just a record. It was a map of pressure points. It showed where the body had been bled. And in its margins, in tiny, frantic script, were notes that were not numbers.

Lupins, sown in the west field where the wheat failed. They say the roots break the hardpan. A desperate prayer. The boy Luc found morels in the rotten oak by the stream. A small grace. Added to the pot. Spoke to the widow Clothilde about her geese. She will part with six goslings in spring, for a promise of protection. Protection from what? From me? From the wind?

Here was the other ledger. The ledger of small, stubborn rebellions against the tally. The lupins, the morels, the goslings bought on credit of a hollow promise. This was the seed. This was the growth from filth. Not a grand strategy, but a series of desperate, organic adjustments, a fungal network of survival beneath the visible rot.

“We don’t need to invent vitality,” Amaury said slowly, the idea forming like a cold, hard knot in his gut. “We need to redirect it. The Tally’s column must be filled. But not from the heart. From the extremities we can afford to lose.”

Eglantine nodded, a grim physician agreeing to a course of amputation. “We show Renaut a flush of health. A sudden, inexplicable fertility in one visible plot. He will report it. It will satisfy The Tally’s hunger for potential. And while his eye is on that ripe fruit, the rest of the organism… persists. In the shadows.”

The elixir, then, was not a potion. It was a formula for a controlled poison, a strategic sickness. They would feed the cancer to save the body.

They celebrated with a shared cup of the last good wine, a vintage from before the Countess’s final fever, drunk from the same chipped clay mug. It was not a celebration of joy, but of alignment. Two surgeons, having agreed on a brutal procedure, acknowledging the necessity with a toast to the patient’s unlikely constitution.

The solar was cold. The fire smoked sullenly. Outside, the grey afternoon was leaching the colour from the world. Renaut and his three men were somewhere in the keep, a silent, auditing presence, a clock ticking in the walls. Yet here, in this moment, there was a respite. The hook was set, but the line was slack.

“My father was a baker,” Eglantine said abruptly, her eyes on the embers. “A good one. His loaves were sought after. He believed in the purity of the process. Good grain, clean water, careful fire. Then the blight came. The grain was dusty, full of ergot. He baked with it. He said to do otherwise was to admit defeat. The bread was beautiful. It shone. It killed seven people in our parish. They called it Saint Anthony’s Fire. He drank himself to death on cheap cider, in a ditch. He loved the craft more than the people who ate it.”

Amaury said nothing. He waited for the point, which in Eglantine’s speech was always a buried root, needing to be dug for.

“I learned,” she said, taking the mug from him, “that survival is not purity. It is compromise. It is sometimes using the blighted grain, but knowing how much will sicken and not kill. It is knowing when the beautiful loaf is a weapon. This place… it is blighted grain. We must bake with it. And we must not poison our own.”

It was the closest thing to a confession of faith he had heard from her. Her love was not for the castle, nor for him, nor for the memory of Isolde. It was for the principle of continuation itself, a fierce, pragmatic love for the stubborn spark in the damp tinder. It was the love a gravedigger might have for the integrity of a well-dug hole, a defence against the chaos of the unburied dead.

He thought of the hidden heart in its box of salt. A love that had failed to save a life, now preserved as a tool, a relic to be deployed for audit and show. Was that not also a form of love? A love so strong it outlasted its object and became a utility? Mortality made relics of all loves, eventually. The only question was what use you could wring from the relic before it too turned to dust.

Their moment of respite ended with a sound like a falling sack of grain from the bailey below, followed by a sharp, truncated cry.

The consequence of their ordeal was named Odo, and he was currently trying to swallow his own fist to stop himself from sobbing.

He was one of the stable-lads, a spindly thing of about twelve years, with eyes too large for his grimy face. He lay in the churned mud by the well, his left leg bent at a wrong, sickening angle. Standing over him, his face a mask of cold fury, was Ser Renaut. One of his men, a stocky individual with forearms like cured hams, was brushing dirt from his own tunic.

“The boy was underfoot,” Renaut said, his voice cutting through the gathering murmur of the castle folk. “A hazard. I have corrected the hazard.”

Amaury felt the cold knot in his gut tighten into a spike. He pushed through the small crowd. Eglantine was already there, on her knees in the muck, her hands hovering over the grotesque angle of the boy’s shin. Her face was blank, but a muscle jumped in her jaw.

“He was running,” the stocky man said, his tone defensive. “He near knocked me into the midden. A little discipline is required here, Castellan. Sluggish, then unruly. It’s a poor reflection.”

Amaury looked from the weeping boy to Renaut’s impassive face. This was not about a boy underfoot. This was the first counter-thrust. The inspection had passed, but the occupation had begun. Renaut was testing the limits of their provisional entry, probing for softness. He was establishing the new tally: the price of disorder was broken bone.

“Discipline is mine to administer,” Amaury said, his voice quieter than he intended. He forced it to carry. “The boy is part of the Hold’s assets. You have damaged an asset.”

Renaut’s pale eyes flickered to him, a trace of interest there, like a scribe noting an unexpected but logical entry. “An asset that impedes efficiency is a liability. I have improved your balance sheet.” He glanced at Eglantine. “Can you mend him, or must we put him down?”

The question hung in the damp air. It was not a metaphor. Eglantine did not look up. Her hands, now gently feeling along the boy’s leg, were steady. “I can mend the bone. It will be a clean break, set true. He will walk. He will not run for a long time.”

“There,” Renaut said, as if settling a minor account. “The liability is restored to potential utility. A valuable lesson, at a reasonable cost.” He turned to his man. “Come. The inventory of the armoury remains incomplete.”

They walked away, their boots sucking at the mud. The crowd parted for them in silence. The fury in the air was palpable, a thick, sour smell of humiliation and impotent rage. But it had no focus. It was the rage of the field against the frost.

Amaury helped Eglantine carry Odo to the infirmary, a dank room off the kitchen that smelled of old herbs and despair. As she worked, splinting the leg with strips of wood and linen, her movements efficient and tender, Amaury saw the consequence of their victory unfold. Renaut’s world was one of pure, frictionless efficiency. Their world was one of stubborn, messy life. The two could not coexist. The audit was not over; it had merely changed form. It was now a live demonstration, and they were failing it in real time. The furious reaction was not a tantrum; it was the logical, applied pressure of a system encountering an anomaly. They were the anomaly.

Odo, his face slick with tears and snot, whispered, “I was only fetching water for the new mare, sir. She’s thirsty.”

“I know,” Amaury said. The hook was no longer abstract. It had a face, and a broken leg. The calculus was now immediate, bloody, and personal. They could not just appear vital. They had to become robust enough to withstand the casual violence of efficiency. They had to make the organism not just look alive, but be resistant to poison.

The decision to begin the return journey was not a decision to go anywhere. It was a decision to retreat to a defensible position within their own walls. The journey was inward, into the conspiracy.

They gathered that night in the undercroft, a low, vaulted space that stank of earth and old roots. With them were the key liabilities: the one-armed smith, Geraint; the widow Clothilde with the geese; old Brother Martin, who could read and write and had a perpetual smell of sacramental wine about him; and the cook, a vast, silent woman named Mathilde who controlled the castle’s stomach and, by extension, its morale. They were the fungal network.

By the guttering light of a single tallow dip, Amaury laid out the ledger. He showed them the immutable tally for The Tally. He showed them the bled-dry fields, the sold jewels. He did not need to mention Odo’s leg. It was in the room with them, a ghost in a splint.

“Renaut will be here for a fortnight,” Amaury said. “His report will determine if The Tally comes himself, with more men, to take direct possession. Our task is to generate a surplus. Not a real one. A visible one. A show crop.”

Geraint spat into the rushes. “The west field is dead. The east field is stones. The south field drinks the river and gets swamp-rot.”

“The west field,” Eglantine said, pointing to the Countess’s marginalia. “Where she sowed the lupins. A desperate prayer. The roots break hardpan. They are a green manure. They fix the air into the soil.”

“They are not wheat,” Clothilde said, her voice like the cluck of her own geese. “You cannot pay a tally with purple flowers.”

“We are not paying with flowers,” Amaury said. “We are paying with a story. The story is this: Isolde’s final, secret project. A new method, learned from a passing monk from the east. The lupins prepare the soil with miraculous speed. We will plough them in. We will then sow a fast barley in that one field. We will manure it with every scrap from the midden, guarded day and night. We will have the children pick every insect from the shoots. We will pour our effort into this one, single, visible plot.”

“And the other fields?” Brother Martin asked, his eyes shrewd.

“They will look… managed. Cleaned. But they are not the story. Renaut’s eye will be drawn to the miracle. He will see frantic, hopeful activity. He will see a gamble that promises future return. Potential. That is what The Tally buys. He is a speculator in futures. We are selling him a future.”

Mathilde the cook spoke for the first time, her voice a low rumble. “The effort will take food. Strength. The people are already thin.”

“They will be fed,” Amaury said, and here he played his foul card. “From my stores. From the salt meat, the grain I brought. It is an investment.”

There was a long silence. They were all complicit now. They were all surgeons, agreeing to feed the one healthy limb to the gangrenous one, to save the body. It was a brutal, loving calculus.

“And when the barley sprouts,” Geraint said slowly, “and Renaut sees it is, in truth, a pathetic little patch, not the golden sea of his report?”

“By then,” Amaury said, meeting each of their eyes in the gloom, “his report will be written. He will have seen what he needed to see: order, effort, innovation, potential. The story will be more real than the barley. He will leave. And we will have bought ourselves a season. Perhaps two. To make the story… true.”

It was a plan built on the understanding of their enemy’s nature. The Tally did not want wealth; he wanted the promise of wealth, neatly columned. Renaut did not want a thriving hold; he wanted a report that demonstrated the feasibility of thriving, a proof of concept. They would give him a theatre of vitality. A performance so earnest it would blur the line between actor and role.

The conspiracy was sealed without oaths. It was sealed with nods, with the grim set of a mouth, with the weary acceptance of those who have no better cards to play. They dispersed into the sleeping castle, a network of secret veins carrying a new, desperate purpose.

Amaury and Eglantine were the last in the undercroft. The tallow dip was drowning in its own grease.

“We are gambling the seed corn,” Eglantine said. “Literally. Your seed corn. If this fails, we starve this winter. A slower break than Odo’s, but just as final.”

“I know,” Amaury said. He thought of the hidden heart. A relic of love, used to mend a body for an audit. Now they would use their own sustenance, the love of future life, to fuel a fiction. It was the same principle. Mortality demanded you spend your love as currency. You could only hope the purchase was worth the cost.

“We have begun the return journey,” he said, though they were going nowhere. “We are returning to the surface. To the light where Renaut can see us. We are leaving the safety of the hidden heart, the secret filth. We are exposing the pale shoot to the frost.”

Eglantine pinched out the dying flame with her fingers, unflinching at the burn. “Then we must pray for a mild spring.”

The next dawn, the performance began.

The west field, a forlorn strip of packed earth and defeated weeds, became the stage. Every able body was upon it. Geraint, with his one good arm, guided a plough pulled by the two oldest oxen, their bones sharp under their hides. Children and elders followed, bending like penitents to gather stones and the tough, ancient roots of thistles. The lupins, a scant, spindly crop from Isolde’s last, desperate sowing, were turned under into the reluctant soil.

Renaut watched from the battlements. Amaury could feel the man’s gaze like a physical pressure, a cold spot on the back of his neck. He made himself visible, moving from group to group, pointing, gesturing to the field with the enthusiasm of a convert. He was selling the story with his body.

By the third day, the field was a brown scar. By the fifth, it had been dressed with a mountain of ripe, steaming midden, carried in baskets and barrows in a stinking, relentless procession. The castle reeked of effort and excrement. It was the smell of pretended life.

On the seventh day, as the first, frail green spears of barley pierced the dark soil in nervous clusters, the consequence of their return journey arrived.

It came not from Renaut, but from the north road. A single rider, his horse lathered, his colours those of The Tally. He carried no weapon, but a leather satchel crammed with scrolls. He was a clerk, a younger, leaner version of Renaut, with ink-stained fingers and the harried air of a man pursued by numbers.

He dismounted in the bailey, ignored Odo hobbling past on his crutch, and presented himself to Amaury. “Castellan. I am Julien, of the Count’s fiscal chamber. Ser Renaut’s preliminary report suggested a re-evaluation of certain assets. I am here to conduct the deeper audit.”

Amaury’s blood turned to well-water. This was the furious reaction. Renaut had not been fooled. Or he had been fooled just enough to be intrigued. The inspection of the body was over. Now they were going to assay the quality of the blood, the density of the bone. The clerk’s eyes were already scanning the bailey, the people, the walls, not with a soldier’s tactical assessment, but with an accountant’s cold appraisal. He was here to count the geese, the goats, the nails in the gates, the bushels of grain in the store. He was here to find the true tally, hidden beneath their theatre of lupins and barley.

The hook was no longer a calculation. It was a needle, poised over the thin parchment of their fiction. The return journey had led them directly into the assessor’s path. They had animated the corpse just in time for a more thorough autopsy.

Chapter 8

CHAPTER 8

The audit was a quieter, more intimate violence than the blade.

Julien the clerk moved through the castle like a chill, his presence a subtraction. He did not shout, or threaten, or even seem to notice the people whose lives he was inventorying. His attention was for the ledger, the granary, the sheepfold, the armoury. He was a man in love with absence, dedicated to finding the hollow places where value should have been and was not. His stylus, scratching on wax, was the only sound in the rooms he entered, a dry, insectile rasp that ate away at silence and substance alike.

Amaury followed him, a shadow to this sharper shadow. He was the host, the castellan, the explainer. Each explanation felt like a suture over a wound that was already festering.

“The sheep count is low,” Julien stated, not looking up from his tablet. They stood in the reeking fold. The animals huddled away from the man’s numerical aura.

“The spring rot,” Amaury said, the lie smooth and heavy as a river stone. “It took the lambs and the weaker ewes. A act of God.”

Julien’s stylus paused. “God’s arithmetic is notoriously imprecise. He does not file quarterly returns.” He made a note. The note was a verdict.

They moved to the granary. The great bins were not empty. Odo and the others had worked through the last nights, mixing the last of the good barley with chaff, with dried, ground lupin stalks, with anything that would bulk and would not immediately betray itself to the eye. It was a porridge of desperation. Julien plunged a brass sampler deep into a bin, withdrew it, and let the grains trickle into his palm. He rolled them, brought them to his nose. He did not smell them. He assayed them. He said nothing. He wrote.

This was the true consequence. Not a battle, but a balance sheet. Not a siege, but a summation. Renaut had seen the theatre in the field and had sent for a critic. The green barley spears were a punchline they had written for themselves; Julien was here to read the rest of the joke, which was in the footnotes.

In the bailey, Odo watched from his perch on a mounting block, his crutch across his knees. His face, usually a landscape of sour amusement, was flat. He saw what Amaury saw: the clerk was not just counting what was missing. He was calculating the energy of the lie. The cost of the midden, the labour of the false sowing, the sheer frantic industry of the past week—all of it was a negative number, a debt incurred to simulate a credit. They had spent their last strength to paint a picture of vitality, and the paint itself was proof of their bankruptcy.

“He’s weighing the air, that one,” Odo muttered as Amaury passed. “He’ll tax us for the dust motes.”

By the third day of the audit, the castle’s pretence of life had curdled into a jittery silence. People spoke in hushes, as if loud words might attract another column in Julien’s ledger. The clerk had taken a small table in the hall, away from the hearth, and there he compiled. Scrolls and tablets surrounded him, a fortress of figures. He requested the castle’s own accounts, the pitiful records Amaury kept. He compared them, his finger tracing down lines of numbers, his lips moving soundlessly. The difference between the official fiction and the tangible reality was the shape of their doom.

Amaury knew the moment was coming. It arrived in the late afternoon, as thin, grey light bled through the narrow windows. Julien looked up from his table, his eyes finding Amaury where he stood pretending to examine a rusting mail hauberk.

“Castellan. A discrepancy.”

Amaury walked over, his legs feeling like timber. “My lord clerk?”

“The people.” Julien tapped a final sheet. “I have tallied the able-bodied adults, cross-referenced with your labour rolls for the spring planting on the demesne. There is a deficit. Approximately twenty souls. Where are they?”

The hook was in. It had been in since the beginning. They had animated the corpse, and now the clerk was pointing to the missing pulse, the absent breath.

“The fever,” Amaury said, the words ash in his mouth. “The same that took the old Countess. It… it culled more than the sheep.”

Julien leaned back. His chair gave a single, dry creak, a sound of profound skepticism. “A fever that selectively targets tax-paying adults, leaving children and the elderly largely untouched? A most political contagion.” He steepled his ink-stained fingers. “Or perhaps they are not dead. Perhaps they have become… lupins.”

The word hung in the hall, precise and devastating.

“My lord?”

“A figure of speech. A non-productive asset. A thing that looks like a crop but is not.” Julien’s whey-pale eyes, so like his Count’s, held Amaury’s. “Ser Renaut reported a curious fervour here. A field sown out of season, with improbable speed. A performance. Performers require an audience, but they also require… extras. Where did your extras go, Castellan, once the curtain fell on your agricultural drama?”

They were discovered. The truth was out, not in a shout, but in a quiet, logistical deduction. The missing people were the unaccounted-for cost of the barley field. Julien had followed the money of their effort, and it had led him to a hole in the population.

“They fled,” Amaury said, abandoning the fever. The truth, now, was the only currency left, and it was devalued. “The levies. The hunger. They slipped away into the woods, over the winter. To fend, or to die. I did not report it. I thought… I hoped to win them back, with a good harvest.”

“By pretending to have already sown one.” Julien nodded, as if this was a satisfying, if pathetic, solution to an equation. “You understand this constitutes fraud against your liege lord. The theft of his labour, the wilful misrepresentation of his assets. The penalty is not a fine, Castellan. A fine implies there is something left to take.”

The punishment was the castle itself. Its lands. Their lives, remanded to the Count’s discretion. Amaury saw it all in the clerk’s impassive face. They were not a holding anymore. They were a column to be zeroed out.

“There is the matter of the dowry,” Julien continued, as if ticking off items on a list. “The late Countess Isolde’s personal estate. Jewels, plate, certain sums. It was to revert to the Count upon her death, pending the resolution of her final household expenses. I will need to inspect it.”

Amaury’s breath caught. The dowry. The final, tangible proof of the Countess’s passage. It was the one thing they had not touched, the one sacrosanct hoard, locked in a chest in the solar. It was also their last, desperate hope. A bribe, a bargaining chip, a seed for a different future. To give it to Julien was to give up the last solid thing.

“Of course,” Amaury heard himself say. “It is in the solar.”

He led the way, his mind a riot of silent calculation. Odo caught his eye from across the hall, a question in the old soldier’s grimace. Amaury gave a minute shake of his head. The game is over. They climbed the stairs, the clerk’s soft footsteps a relentless echo behind him.

The solar was cold, the hearth dead. The chest, iron-bound and formidable, sat beneath the narrow window. Amaury produced the key from the chain around his neck, a heavy, old thing. He knelt, the iron biting into his palm as he turned it. The lock clunked, a sound of finality.

He lifted the lid.

The chest was empty.

Not a glint of silver, not a gleam of gold. Not a single pearl or scrap of silk. Just the bare, aromatic wood of the interior, and the faint, ghostly scent of lavender and rue that had clung to Isolde’s things.

Julien peered over his shoulder. “A most thorough fever.”

Amaury stared into the void. This was not Julien’s work. The audit had not reached this room. This was an older theft. A silent, perfect subtraction. He thought of the missing people, the twenty souls. He thought of the desperate, hungry nights. Someone had known. Someone had made a calculation of their own. The dowry was the reward. And it was gone.

“It seems,” Julien said softly, “your deficits are even deeper than I projected. This is no longer mismanagement. This is embezzlement. On a rather grand scale.”

The hook was no longer a needle. It was a spike, driven through the heart of their fiction. They had nothing. No food, no people, no hope, no treasure. They were a castle of air and hunger, and the wind was rising.

“You will be confined to the hall, Castellan,” Julien said, his voice devoid of triumph. It was a simple statement of process. “I will complete my final tallies and dispatch a rider to the Count. He will decide on the disposition of yourself and your… remaining assets.” He looked at Amaury, not with malice, but with a kind of professional pity. “You should not have sown the barley. It was an emotional expenditure. Ledgers despise emotion.”

He turned and left Amaury kneeling before the empty chest, the key cold and useless in his hand. The return journey had ended here, in a hollow box, in a silent room. They had fled the inmost cave of the Count’s scrutiny only to arrive at the cold, hard floor of his justice. The reward was gone, taken by ghosts. And the vengeful force pursuing them was not a knight with a sword, but a clerk with a ledger, and his verdict was already written in columns of absolute, unforgiving black.

Chapter 9

CHAPTER 9

The emptiness had a texture. It was not the soft, forgiving emptiness of a forgotten cellar or a disused chapel. It was the polished, definitive emptiness of a plundered reliquary. Amaury knelt on the solar floor, the cold of the key seeping into his palm, and felt the shape of it. It was a mould, this void in the chest, and he was the hot wax being poured in, taking its form: hollow, scentless, worthless.

Julien’s footsteps receded down the spiral stair, a dry, precise punctuation to the sentence he had just pronounced. Confined to the hall. Disposition of assets. The words were administrative, bloodless. They were the true horror. A sword to the gut was a transaction between men. This was a deletion by ledger. He, Amaury, Castellan of this airy ruin, was a negative figure. A debt in boots.

He did not move for a long time. The scent of lavender and rue from the empty chest was the ghost of a ghost. It spoke of Isolde’s careful hands, her inventories of linens and dried flowers, her belief in order as a kind of incense against chaos. That belief had been as misplaced as his own. Someone else’s order had superseded it. A colder calculus. The twenty missing souls. The missing dowry. They were not two mysteries, but one equation, and the solution was zero.

A sound escaped him, then. Not a groan, not a curse. It was the low, rough noise of a foundation stone settling into mud. It was the sound of finality.

He stood. His knees cracked like dry kindling. He looked around the solar, this chamber of quiet theft. The tapestry of the boar hunt seemed ludicrous now—a testament to a vitality that had never existed here. The boar was not at bay; it had already been butchered, salted away by invisible hands. He was the one wearing its skin, prancing for the auditors.

He was not confined to the hall. Not yet. Julien would be in the counting-room, scratching his final, damning columns. The rider would be saddled at dawn. Between now and that galloping verdict lay only the thin, cold fabric of a single night.

Amaury descended the stairs. The great hall was a cavern of echoes and hunger. The few remaining souls—the cook, two ancient grooms, the half-wit scullion named Pym—were huddled near the hearth where no fire burned. They looked at him not with expectation, but with the dull recognition of fellow prisoners. He had sown barley for them. It had been, as Julien said, an emotional expenditure. Ledgers despised emotion. But men, he thought, staring at their ash-smeared faces, perhaps did not.

“Pym,” he said. His voice was rusted from disuse.

The scullion flinched, as if called to account for a broken dish.

“The small cellar. The one behind the broken wine rack. Is it empty?”

Pym’s eyes, wide and watery, blinked. He was a boy of maybe fifteen, whose mind was a room with most of the furniture missing. But he knew the castle’s hidden bruises. “The wet one, sir? With the bad smell?”

“That one.”

“Nothin’ in there but wet and smell, sir. And the stones what fell.”

Amaury nodded. “Fetch a lantern. And a mattock.”

It was an order devoid of hope, which made it pure. He was not digging for treasure. He was digging because it was the last verb available to him. To break ground. To turn something over. To prove that his hands could still make a change in the state of things, even if that change was only a hole.

They descended into the undercroft, a realm of perpetual chill and the breath of damp earth. The ‘bad smell’ was the ghost of a long-flooded cesspit, a sweet-rotten miasma that clung to the back of the throat. Pym held the lantern high, its light licking the sweating, bulging walls. The fallen stones were a slump of rubble in the far corner, where the wall had surrendered to the pressure of the hill behind it.

Amaury took the mattock. He did not think of the dowry. He did not think of the Count’s justice. He thought only of the arc of the tool, the bite of iron into impacted dirt and shale. He swung. The impact juddered up his arms. He swung again. A piece of mortar clattered away. He was not a castellan unravelling a mystery. He was a man making a noise in the dark.

He lost time in the rhythm of it. Strike, crack, grate. Strike, crack, grate. Pym watched, his mouth a slack circle, as Amaury’s world narrowed to the circle of lantern light and the growing pile of debris. Sweat stung his eyes, mixing with the grit in the air. His breath sawed in his lungs, each gasp flavoured with decay.

The mattock head struck something that did not grate. It thunked.

Amaury paused, chest heaving. He knelt, scraping at the earth with his hands. It wasn’t stone. It was wood, old and soft with rot. He cleared more away, Pym crouching to bring the lantern closer. It was a plank, part of a larger structure—a crate, or a chest, long buried by the collapse.

His heart, a sluggish thing he’d thought bankrupt of feeling, gave a single, hard knock against his ribs. Not hope. Something older: curiosity. The final vice.

He worked more carefully now, using his hands to pull away the damp earth. The wood crumbled at the edges. He found a rusted hasp. Then a corner of something that was not wood. He wiped the mud away.

It was cloth. Mouldering, slimy, but cloth. He pulled gently, and with a soft, tearing sound, it came free. A sack. Heavy. He dragged it out into the open space and untied the rotten cord at its neck.

Pym’s lantern shook, casting leaping shadows.

Inside were coins. Not silver. Not gold. Copper. Great, greasy, verdigris-caked discs of it, black as old blood. He scooped a handful. They were hopeless. The currency of beggars and petty tolls. Beneath them, his fingers touched metal of another sort. He drew out a chalice. It was tin, battered and bent. A pilgrim’s token. Then a wooden bowl. A rusted knife with a bone handle. A child’s silver rattle, tarnished black.

This was not a treasure hoard.

It was a midden of lost things. The final inventory of the vanished.

He sat back on his heels, the copper coins dripping from his fingers. This was the reward. Not Isolde’s dowry, but the pathetic, secreted leavings of the twenty souls who had slipped away into the forest or the fever or the earth. Their last, desperate act of selfhood: to hide their pitiful wealth from the Count’s tally. They had chosen this wet, dark, smelling hole as their castle, their treasury. And they had died guarding it.

The laugh that came out of him was short and sharp, like the bark of a fox. It was the pure, cleansing acid of truth. He had been searching for a grand theft, a villain with a ledger. But the villain was the air itself, the slow, sucking poverty that had driven people to bury their rattles and their tin cups. The embezzlement was life, stealing away from this place bit by bit, coin by copper coin.

He was not the guardian of a lost fortune. He was the gravedigger of a lost poverty.

And in that moment, purged of his last, grand illusion, Amaury saw the final test before him. It was not to find the treasure. It was to honour the truth. The truth was this sack of grief. The truth was the empty chest upstairs. The truth was Julien’s ledger, waiting for its final entry.

“Pym,” he said, his voice calm now, scraped clean. “Take the lantern. Go to the stables. Saddle my palfrey and the bay rounsey. Bring them to the postern gate. Quietly.”

The boy stared, understanding nothing but the tone of command. He scurried away, the light bobbing and then vanishing, leaving Amaury in a darkness so complete it was like being inside the empty chest.

He sat in the blackness with the sack of lost things. This was his ordeal’s end. Not a battle with a knight, but a surrender to the facts. The castle was a corpse. He was its attendant, washing it for burial. The darkness within him—the pride that had clung to the title of Castellan, the shame of his failure, the futile hope of redemption—drained away into the surrounding earth. There was nothing left but the work.

He stood, hefting the sack. It was surprisingly heavy. The weight of twenty small departures.

The hall was a tomb of shadows when he returned. Julien was there, seated at the high table with a single candle, his ledger open like an altar stone. He did not look up as Amaury entered, his boots leaving muddy prints on the rushes.

“You are in breach of your confinement, Castellan.”

“I am,” Amaury said. He walked to the hearth and dropped the sack. It landed with a dense, unmusical clatter.

Julien’s pen paused. His whey-pale eyes lifted, flicked to the sack, then back to Amaury’s face. “More emotional expenditure?”

“The final tally.” Amaury walked to the table. He placed the heavy iron key to Isolde’s chest beside the ledger. “The dowry is gone. Taken, I would guess, by the former steward, the one who ‘retired to a monastery’ last year. A man with access, and time, and a keen understanding of the Count’s distractions. He is likely in Bordeaux by now, living quietly on sapphires.”

Julien’s expression did not change. “A hypothesis. Unproven.”

“It is the only one that fits the empty box.” Amaury leaned on the table, his shadow swallowing the candlelight. “But that is not the deficit, Clerk. The true deficit is here.” He gestured to the walls, the vaulted darkness overhead. “It is in the stones. It is in the land. It is in the slow leak of people and purpose. You can tally it in your columns, but you cannot arrest it. It is mortality. The estate is dying of natural causes.”

“My lord the Count,” Julien said, with infinite patience, “does not believe in natural causes. Only in causes, and effects. The effect of your stewardship is ruin. The cause is your incompetence. The ledger is clear.”

“The ledger is a story,” Amaury said, and the words felt true as he spoke them. “A story written in numbers. You have written one where I am the villain. Let me propose another.”

He reached out and turned the ledger around to face him. He pointed to the columns of expenses, the lists of missing stores. “Read it differently. The castellan, seeing the decline, attempted radical preservation. He dispersed the remaining peasantry to neighbouring holdings before they could starve, fabricating tales of disappearance to protect them from the Count’s conscription orders. He sold the late Countess’s dowry piece by piece to buy grain from passing merchants, but was robbed on the road—a loss he could not admit. He has, in fact, been conducting a strategic retreat, sacrificing coin and pride to preserve human capital. A doomed, perhaps foolish, but fundamentally loyal action.”

Julien stared at him. For the first time, a crack appeared in his professional veneer. It was not admiration. It was the stunned recognition of a master craftsman shown a new, blasphemous use for his tools. “That… is not what happened.”

“Is it not?” Amaury’s smile was thin, a crack in mud. “Can you prove it? The people are gone. The dowry is gone. The only witness is the castellan. And this.” He kicked the sack. “The saved treasures of those who left. Hidden, so the Count’s men wouldn’t take their last copper as a departure tax. Evidence of a compassionate, if chaotic, administration.”

The silence that followed was profound. Julien looked from Amaury’s face to the sack, to the key, to his own perfect columns of black ink. He was a man who lived in the cathedral of Fact, and Amaury had just introduced the heresy of Narrative.

“You are suggesting,” Julien said slowly, “I falsify the final report.”

“I am suggesting you choose the final report,” Amaury corrected. “The facts are empty vessels. You can fill them with the wine of incompetence, or the vinegar of tragic loyalty. One gets me hanged and the estate seized. The other… might give it a chance. The Count is sentimental about loyalty. It is the one emotion his ledgers respect.”

“Why?” Julien asked, the single word containing a universe of professional outrage. “Why would I do this? My function is clarity.”

“Your function,” Amaury said, leaning closer, the candlelight carving deep gullies in his face, “is to serve your Count. A hanged castellan and a seized estate are a simple line item. A tragedy, a story of a faithful servant overwhelmed by circumstance… that is a saga. It reflects well on a lord to have such men. It costs him nothing but a dead estate he didn’t want anyway. And it gets you, the clear-eyed clerk who uncovered not villainy but tragedy, a reputation for perception. You become the man who reads between the columns.”

He saw it land. Julien was a creature of advancement as surely as a knight was a creature of battle. The clerk’s fingers touched the edge of the ledger. He was silent for a long minute, his gaze turned inward, running calculations of risk and reputation.

“The rider,” Julien said finally, not looking up. “He leaves at dawn. The report is sealed by then.”

“Then you have until dawn to write a better story.”

Amaury left him there, with his candle and his crisis of faith. He had purged his own darkness—the belief that truth was a single, solid thing. Now he offered that purgation to Julien. The final battle was not fought with mattocks, but with meanings. It was grander, and more cleansing, than any physical ordeal. It was the rebirth of a fact into a legend.

He collected the two horses from a trembling Pym at the postern gate. The night was vast and cold, the sky a tapestry of indifferent stars. He led the animals not towards the woods, but around to the front of the castle, to the barren field where the barley struggled in the moonlight.

He took the sack of lost things. One by one, he began to bury the items. The copper coins in a shallow, scattered trench. The tin chalice under a particular stone. The rattle at the base of the struggling green shoots. He did not hide them. He planted them.

This was the application of his hard-won wisdom. The treasure was not to be spent. It was to be seeded. Let the Count’s men, if they ever came to plough this field, find the pathetic hoard of the vanished. Let it tell its own story, of people who loved small things enough to hide them. It was a testament. It was the only victory possible over the ledger’s absolute zero: to leave a residue of mystery, a poetry of loss in the soil.

He was on his knees, patting earth over the last copper coin, when he heard the crunch of a footstep on the dry soil.

He did not turn. “Is it dawn?”

“Not yet,” Julien’s voice came from behind him. It sounded tired. “The report is written. The rider has his sealed packet.”

Amaury stood, brushing dirt from his hands. He faced the clerk. Julien looked older in the monochrome light, the candle-fatigue etched deep.

“Which story did you choose?”

Julien held out a second, smaller, unsealed parchment. “The true one. For you. The other… the saga… goes to the Count.” He looked at the disturbed earth, at the horses waiting patiently. “You are leaving.”

“The confinement was to the hall,” Amaury said. “I am in the field.”

A faint, ghost of a smile touched Julien’s thin lips. “A semantic expenditure. Ledgers despise semantics.” He extended the parchment. “Your name is clear. In my report, you are a loyal fool, not a criminal. You will not be pursued. But this place…”

“Is a corpse,” Amaury finished. He took the parchment, the proof of his transformed state. He was no longer Castellan Amaury, defender of a dying fiction. He was Amaury, the man who buried lost things and walked away. “And I am not its mourner. Not anymore.”

He swung onto the palfrey. The bay rounsey, its coat dull in the starlight, waited on a lead.

“Where will you go?” Julien asked. It was not a professional question.

Amaury looked south, towards the dark line of the forest, and beyond it, the roads that led to worlds not made of ledger lines and empty chests. “To find a story that isn’t about deficits.”

He nudged the horse forward. He did not look back at the castle, a stark silhouette of hunger against the sky. He had faced his ordeal in the dark cellar. He had fought his final battle with words and meanings. He had planted his wisdom in the barren field. The antagonist—the crushing, impersonal force of failure and judgment—was defeated not by a sword, but by a better tale.

He was reborn into the cool, open mouth of the night, a man of no account, with a clean name and a horse, and the smell of turned earth on his hands.

He rode until the castle was a mere thickening of the gloom behind him. The forest edge welcomed him with its deeper dark. He dismounted, leading the horses into the bracken, the need for silence a old habit. He would rest here until true dawn, then choose a road.

It was as he was tethering the horses that he saw it. A flicker of orange, deep in the trees. Not a will-o’-the-wisp. Too steady. A campfire.

He froze, his hand going to the hilt of his eating knife, his only weapon. Poachers? Outlaws? The twenty vanished souls, gathered in a woodland court?

Crouching low, he moved towards the light, a shadow among shadows. The fire was small, ringed with stones. A figure sat beside it, wrapped in a dark cloak. Not a peasant. The posture was wrong—too upright, too still. A pot hung from a tripod over the flames, and the smell that wafted towards him was not of game or gruel.

It was of lavender and rue.

The figure turned its head slightly, the firelight catching the line of a cheekbone, the curve of an ear. Amaury’s breath stopped in his chest.

It was a face he knew. Not from life. From a chair at a high table, propped upright in a gown of faded silk.

The late Countess Isolde was having a much better evening. She was, very objectively, alive. And she was looking directly at him, her eyes reflecting the flames, as she stirred the pot of her own ghostly perfume.

Chapter 10

CHAPTER 10

The corpse was, once again, beginning to compete with the roast pig.

This was the central, unspoken tension at the campfire. Amaury of the Much-Mourned Prospects, a man whose face felt like a map of roads he’d never intended to travel, stood frozen at the edge of the firelight. His hand, which had gone to the hilt of his eating knife, now fell limp at his side. The weapon was an absurdity here, a teaspoon against a tide. Before him, the late Countess Isolde stirred a small pot suspended over the flames. The scent of lavender and rue, that ghostly perfume from her vigil in the solar, was now threaded with woodsmoke and the damp, living breath of the forest floor. It was a smell that spoke not of embalming, but of simmering intention.

She was, by any objective measure, having a better evening than he was. Three days dead of a sudden fever, she had been dressed for a different sort of audience. The faded silk of her gown was now a practical wool, dark and travel-stained. The careful cosmetics of her death-mask were gone, replaced by the sharp, clean geometry of cheekbone and the watchful intelligence of eyes that were very much alive. They reflected the flames, not with the dull glaze of silver inlay, but with the liquid, whey-pale focus he remembered in her husband, The Tally. She looked at him, and the look was an invoice.

“You’re blocking the warmth,” she said. Her voice was not the whispery thing he’d imagined for a spectre. It was dry, precise, the sound of a ledger page being turned. “And you’ve brought two horses. One for me, I presume. Sit. You look like a man who has just seen his own ghost, which is tedious. I am not yours to be haunted by.”

Amaury did not sit. The ordinary world—the cool, open mouth of the night, the clean name, the smell of turned earth—had lasted precisely one hour. It had not been seen through the eyes of his changed self, for his changed self had not accounted for this. His ordeal in the cellar, his battle with words, his planting of wisdom in a barren field—all of it felt like a child’s game of mumblety-peg. The antagonist, it seemed, was not a single force of failure. It was a matryoshka doll of absurdities, and he had only opened the largest, most obvious shell.

“You were dead,” he said. The statement was a flat stone dropped into the silence between them.

“I was presented,” she corrected, not looking up from her pot. “There is a difference, one that costs approximately thirty silver marks in bribes to the physician, the washerwoman, and two guards with a taste for theatricality and a weak stomach for actual corpses. The pig, I am told, was excellent. Sir Bors’s elbow was, as anticipated, a reliable agent of distraction.”

Amaury’s mind, that newly-tilled field, began to sprout wild, connecting weeds. The Count’s whey-pale gaze, fixed on his wife’s body. Not grief. Audit. He was ensuring the performance was convincing. The sudden fever. The swift internment in the family crypt, no doubt. A crypt with a back door.

“The Count…” Amaury began.

“The Count is a man who understands that the greatest deficit is not of coin, but of time,” she said, finally lifting her gaze. “He is also a man who believes a living wife is a liability with opinions. A dead wife is a sentimental asset. A vanished wife, however, is a problem that becomes, with the proper documentation, someone else’s problem. He thinks I have fled to a convent with a lover, a story he will nurture with solemn disappointment. It tidies the board for him. No messy annulment. No political repercussions. Just a blank space where a countess used to be, which is his favourite kind of space.”

She ladled a portion of the steaming, fragrant liquid into a wooden bowl. It was not food. It was a distillation, a potion of memory and sharp herbs. She held it out to him.

“This is the elixir,” she said, and for the first time, a smile touched her mouth, a thin, crescent-shaped thing. “Not that swill. The story. The true one. You wanted a tale that wasn’t about deficits. Here it is. The deficit was my life. The surplus is my death. Sit and share it. You’ve earned a cup, I think. Your performance as the desperate, literate fool was masterful. You gave him exactly the distraction he needed to stop looking for me. He was so pleased with his own cleverness in ruining you, he never considered you were the backdrop against which I made my exit.”

Amaury took the bowl. The warmth seeped into his hands. He sat, not because he chose to, but because his legs would no longer hold the weight of the revelation. He had been a prop. A piece of stagecraft in someone else’s escape. The clean name he’d won was a mark on a playbill. The smell of turned earth was the scent of a trap he’d dug for himself, while thinking he was planting a garden.

He drank. The liquid tasted of bitter flowers and a sharper, more metallic truth.

“Why?” he asked, the word clouding in the steam between them.

“Love mortality,” she said, as if answering a question about the weather. “Not the mortality of love. The love of mortality. The passion for finitude. Guibert does not love me. He loved the idea of my family’s connections, which are now exhausted. He would have kept me as a piece of furniture, growing dusty in the solar, until I actually did die of boredom. I love… deadlines. Expirations. The closing of an account. To feel a limit, and to step over it—that is the only true vitality. I loved the limit of my own death. So I purchased it.”

She sipped from her own bowl. “He thinks it’s about another man. It is, in a way. It’s about the man I intend to become. A man with a new name, and no history, and the considerable sum of jewels I have been slowly converting to portable wealth for the past five years, sewn into the lining of my saddle. Which you have kindly brought me. Thank you for that.”

Amaury looked over his shoulder, towards where the horses were tethered. The ordinary world had not just been altered; it had been an illusion painted on a screen, and the screen had just been yanked away. The community he thought he might share his hard-won wisdom with was a fiction. The ones he’d left behind were players in a darker comedy. He had no elixir for them. He was the elixir—a bitter draught swallowed by a count to aid his digestion.

“And what am I to you?” he asked, his voice quiet. “The comic relief? The pawn to be sacrificed?”

“The witness,” she said, setting her bowl down with a soft tock. “A story needs a witness, or it is just a madwoman talking to the trees. You saw me dead. Now you see me alive. You hold the contradiction. That makes it real. And you are something else, now. You are not Amaury the Failed. You are the man who outwitted The Tally in his own cellar and walked away. That is a story with legs. It is a story I might have use for.”

“Use.” The word was a cold pebble in his gut.

“We are both stories now,” she said, her whey-pale eyes holding his. “Cut loose from our ledgers. The question is what we intend to author next. You can ride off into your clean dawn and try to plant your little wisdom in new soil. Or you can understand that the world is not a field. It is a forest. And in a forest, things do not grow in neat rows. They grow where the light falls, and they strangle one another for the privilege. I have light. I have resources. I have a destination in mind that requires a person who can turn a phrase into a key and a meaning into a weapon. You have just demonstrated a passing competence with both.”

She leaned forward, the fire carving shadows under her cheekbones. “Reconcile yourself, Amaury. Not with your old life. That is ash. Reconcile yourself with the fact that you have already chosen the new one. You chose it when you walked towards this fire. The man you were would have fled into the dark. The man you are came to see. So. See.”

He did. He saw the absolute, terrifying freedom in her face. It was not the freedom of the open road. It was the freedom of the burned bridge, the scuttled ship, the fabricated grave. It was a love of mortality so profound it had faked its own object. And he saw, with a lurch in his soul that felt like both sickness and hunger, that he wanted it. The clean name was nothing. The clean slate was everything. Even if the hand holding the chalk was that of a dead woman.

“What destination?” he asked.

“A port,” she said. “Where stories are currency, and identities are goods to be shipped. Where a man with a quick mind and a woman with a dead name can become… exporters. Of narratives. Of solutions. Of elegant, untraceable conclusions for people who find their own lives… overly persistent.”

She was proposing they become mercenaries of fate. Accountants of destiny, balancing the books of other people’s lives by closing them.

“You would trade in endings,” he said.

“I would trade in crafted endings,” she corrected. “Most people’s are so shoddy. Unplanned, sentimental, messy with regret. We could offer a premium service. A certain poetry. You have a gift for the framing metaphor. I have a gift for the plausible lie. Together, we could give people the death they deserve, if not the one they have earned.”

The hook was in him. It was not a hook of desire, but of dreadful, irresistible recognition. This was the new, improved ordinary world. Not a cottage, not a humble scribe’s post. A conspiratorial fire in the woods, with a partner who understood that the only thing more powerful than a sword or a tale was a tale that killed. He had defeated the antagonist of failure by telling a better story. She had defeated the antagonist of her life by becoming a ghost in a better story. It was a perverse, perfect logic.

He looked into the empty bowl in his hands. The elixir was gone. He had shared it with the only community that remained to him: a society of two, founded on a mutual understanding of deficits and the violent poetry of their correction.

“I have a horse,” he said finally, his voice a stranger’s in his own throat. “And an eating knife.”

“And a demonstrated capacity for moral compromise,” she added, standing and shaking out her cloak. “Do not undersell yourself. It is our chief asset. Now, fetch the horses. Dawn is a sentiment. We travel by night.”

They rode not as knight and lady, nor as master and servant, but as two shadows flowing along the deer paths, the forest absorbing the sound of their passage. Isolde—for he could not think of her as the Countess anymore—rode with a surety that spoke of prior reconnaissance. She was not fleeing. She was proceeding according to plan.

After hours of silence, broken only by the creak of leather and the sigh of the trees, she spoke again, her voice cutting through the dark.

“The love of mortality,” she said, as if continuing a conversation they’d been having. “It clarifies. It is the ultimate tally. Everything must balance to zero. Love, hate, wealth, breath. All accounts close. The art is in the closing entry. Guibert’s will be a long, slow, dusty column of petty figures, dwindling to a meaningless total. Mine will be a single, bold, fraudulent stroke of the pen. And yours?” She glanced at him, a pale smudge in the gloom. “Yours was a column of debts, until tonight. Now, it is a blank page. We will inscribe something worthy. Something with a finality that sings.”

Amaury said nothing. He was reconciling. With the cold, with the dark, with the monstrous, thrilling vacancy of the future. His initial lack—a place, a purpose, a name—had been resolved not by finding them, but by seeing them for the fictions they were. The only real thing was the movement forward, the crafting of the next line, the setting of the next scene. He was not reborn into a new man. He was edited down to his essential verbs: to go, to see, to do.
