They were dismissed with another silent gesture from the colourless clerk. As they walked back through the antechamber, past the martyred saint, Amaury felt a hollow triumph. They had done it. They had played the game. They had traded their painful, private artefacts for a shield of holy writ. But the parchment of their future, as Isolde had said, was now being inscribed by another hand. Their love story was becoming a pamphlet.

Outside, in the palace courtyard, the spring air felt shockingly free of incense and sawdust. Isolde let out a long, slow breath, her penitent’s posture dissolving into one of sheer fatigue.

“It worked,” Amaury said, not quite a question.

“It began to work,” she corrected. “We have bought a hearing, not a verdict. The document he gives us will be a cage of his own words. We must be prepared to live inside it.”

They walked through the town, towards the river. The familiar stinks of mud, fish, and humanity were a bracing tonic. As they crossed the market square, Amaury saw a familiar, hated figure: Fulk Mercet’s factor, a weasel-faced man named Joliffe, standing by the conduit, deep in conversation with a city guardsman. Joliffe saw them. His conversation stopped. His eyes, small and dark as currants, tracked them with a pure, professional interest. There was no anger in his look, only a calm, recalculating assessment. The news of their audience at the palace would already be winding its way through the town’s gossip-channels, a subtle shift in the political air pressure. Joliffe’s gaze was the barometer registering it.

When they reached the riverbank, they looked across at the Wharf Street scar. From here, it was a blackened tooth-gap. But at its centre, pale in the afternoon light, was the stubborn, intact foundation Amaury had laid. Their ‘miracle’.

“He will not stop,” Amaury said quietly.

“No,” Isolde agreed. “But his methods will now need… finesse. To act against a project under the Bishop’s consideration is to risk a spiritual censure. And Fulk’s power, while great, is not absolute. He needs the cooperation of the guilds, the tacit consent of the burghers. A public blessing from the Bishop makes us slightly more expensive to crush.”

“So we are a costly nuisance now, instead of a free one.”

“It is an improvement.” She managed a thin, weary smile. “We are no longer just a debt to be written off. We are a line in someone else’s ledger. That is a form of life.”

That night, in their cramped room at the inn, Amaury dreamed of mortar. Not the simple lime mix of his trade, but a glowing, golden substance, thick as honey. He was trying to set a stone—the cornerstone of their house—but the mortar would not grip. It slid, viscous and useless, over the stone, over his hands. He looked up and saw the Bishop and Fulk Mercet standing together on a scaffold high above him. They were not adversaries. They were foremen, consulting on the same vast, incomprehensible project. The Bishop held his mason’s compass; Fulk held a ledger. They looked down at Amaury’s struggles with identical expressions of mild, professional disappointment. The golden mortar began to cool, to harden, trapping his hands, cementing him to the stone he could not lift.

He woke with a start, his heart hammering against his ribs. Isolde slept beside him, her face smooth in the moonlight, temporarily escaped from the plots and documents. He watched the rise and fall of her breath, this woman who could turn their scorched history into a bargaining chip, who could sit straight-backed on a hard bench for hours and sell a lie so well it almost became truth. Their love had been a quiet, fierce flame in a hidden hearth. Now it was a commodity, a narrative asset. The fire had not consumed it, but it had transformed it into something else—something that could be weighed, valued, and traded in the cold markets of power.

He thought of the fused coins, soon to be locked in the Bishop’s treasury, labelled and displayed. Two separate things, melted into one indivisible mass. A symbol of their union, yes. But also a symbol of loss. You could never again spend them, break them apart, use them for their intended purpose. They were only themselves now, only a story. Their value was purely representational.

Three days later, they returned to the palace. The clerk presented them with a single sheet of vellum. The script was beautiful, a flowing, uncial hand. It told a story of a virtuous widow and a devout mason, joined in a chaste and holy affection. It spoke of a fire sent as a trial, and of the miraculous preservation of the foundation of their domicile, a sign of God’s favour upon their steadfast bond. It mentioned the Bishop’s wise and pastoral recognition of this sign. It was clean, bloodless, and utterly unrecognisable to Amaury. It was their life, embalmed and dressed in its finest azure gown, a wimple artfully arranged to conceal the greying truth.

“You will make your mark here,” the clerk said, pointing to the bottom.

Isolde took the quill first. She signed without a tremor. Amaury followed, his mason’s callous scraping against the feather. His mark felt like a signature on a lease for a property that no longer existed.

The Bishop himself appeared then, as if on cue. He carried a small aspergillum. With a few perfunctory motions, he flicked holy water over them where they stood. The droplets were cold on Amaury’s forehead.

“Benedicat vos Deus,” the Bishop intoned, his voice empty of any feeling save efficiency. “Your perseverance is noted. May your rebuilding be a testament to faith.”

It was done. They had their blessing. A weapon forged from parchment and plagiarism.

As they left the palace for the final time, a different clerk handed Isolde a sealed letter. “For your information,” he said blandly.

They broke the seal on the wharf, away from prying eyes. It was a short, anonymous note, written in a crude, blocky hand. It contained no threat, no demand. It was simply a piece of information, delivered with the chilling courtesy of a professional.

The Bishop’s master mason for the new chantry is one Bertrand Lacroix. He is married to the cousin of Fulk Mercet’s second wife. Stone is family. Always.

Isolde let the parchment fall. It fluttered down to land on the greasy surface of the river, where it was carried away, slowly saturating, sinking.

Their blessing was real. Their protection was parchment-thin. And the scaffold, it seemed, was owned by everyone and no one, a vast, interconnected structure where power married power in a silent, endless chantry of its own. They had built their legend, as she had said they must. But they had built it on land they did not own, using materials supplied by their enemy. The hymn had been written. Now they had to sing it, while watching for the hammers in the hands of the choir.

Chapter 16

CHAPTER 16

The river took the note, but the words remained, etched into the air between them like a mason’s mark on a foundation stone. They stood on the wharf, the administrative stink of the palace at their backs, the organic rot of the river before them. Amaury watched the parchment darken, sink, become nothing. It was a fitting end for a message that was both a gift and a shiv to the kidneys. A courtesy with a sharpened edge.

“Stone is family,” Isolde said, her voice flat. She was not looking at the water, but at the forest of masts and cranes across the river, the perpetual industry of a city that built upon its own rubble. “Of course it is. We should have anticipated it.”

“We anticipated greed,” Amaury corrected, his own voice rough from the dust of the road and the silence he had kept in the Bishop’s chambers. “We anticipated pride. We did not anticipate a wedding contract.”

It was the same thing, in the end. Just a different liturgy. The marriage of stone to power, of blood to contract, of Fulk Mercet’s vengeance to the Bishop’s ambition. Bertrand Lacroix. The name was a tool, a cold chisel. He would be the man on the scaffold, the hand holding the plumb line, the eye judging every block Amaury laid. And his loyalty would be to his wife’s cousin’s husband’s interests. A chain of obligation, each link forged in a chapel somewhere, stronger than iron.

Their walk back to the inn was a silent procession through a city that felt newly alien. The cries of hawkers, the rumble of carts, the clatter from open workshops—all of it seemed part of a vast, indifferent mechanism. They were a single grit of sand in its gears. Their blessing, that precious, hard-won scrap of vellum with its episcopal seal, felt like a promissory note written in ink that would fade in the sun. It granted them the right to build, and the right to be watched while they did so.

The inn’s common room was a cave of smoke and stewed mutton. They took a table in a corner, the weight of the journey and the audience settling on their shoulders like a yoke. Isolde ordered wine. When it came, she did not drink, but circled the rim of the clay cup with a fingertip.

“We must treat Lacroix as the weather,” she said finally. “A condition of the site. Like rain, or frost. We cannot argue with him. We cannot plead. We must simply work in spite of him, and plan for his interference.”

“He will have the plans,” Amaury said. “He will see the… liberties we have taken.”

“He will see a design approved by the Bishop’s own office. A design inspired by Saint Trophime. His job is to execute it, not to question its provenance. His question will be in the execution. A foundation dug too shallow here. A delivery of inferior stone there. A misinterpretation of an instruction.” She took a slow breath. “We must be the stone. We must be so precise, so unassailable in our own work, that his attempts to shift us merely chip against our faces.”

Amaury thought of stone. He thought of the quarry at Saint-Cloud, the smell of split rock, the patient, granular truth of it. Stone did not care about family. It cared about weight, and balance, and the slow, patient pressure of the earth. You could build a false legend upon it, dress it in stolen hymns, but the stone itself was neither true nor false. It simply was. And if you understood it, it would hold. Until it didn’t.

“We will need our own men,” he said. “Not just labourers. A core. A few masons, a few carvers. Men whose loyalty is to the craft, or to the coin, and not to Mercet’s web.”

“Find them,” Isolde said. It was not a request. It was the first move on a new board. “Use what remains of the purse. Be discreet, but be quick. We return to Saint-Cloud in three days. The ground must be broken before Lacroix can sow his own seeds in it.”

The search was a lesson in the economy of trust. Amaury haunted the taverns near the guildhalls, the smoky pits where men just off a site drank away the ache in their hands. He listened. He bought ale. He spoke little. He was looking for a particular type of silence—not the sullen quiet of the stupid or the broken, but the watchful reserve of a man who knows his own worth and has seen it discounted by masters more interested in speed than truth.

He found two.

The first was a carver named Thibaut, a man with a beard like a thicket of iron filings and fingers permanently stained with red ochre from marking stone. He had just finished a stint on a new town house for a wool merchant, a project he described with a sculptor’s contempt. “Puffery and paste,” he grunted into his tankard. “Leafy bunches where the corbels should be strong. Faces of angels that look like they’ve smelled the merchant’s accounts.” He had been paid, and paid well, but there was a sourness in him, a professional disgust. Amaury spoke of load, of line, of the virtue of a clean edge that spoke for itself. Thibaut’s eyes, small and bright as a boar’s, lost their glaze of ale and fixed on him. He asked no questions about patronage, about lineage. He asked about the stone. When Amaury described the Saint-Cloud limestone, its quality, its consistency, Thibaut gave a single, slow nod. “Good skin,” he said. “Takes a line. Holds a detail.” It was the highest praise. He was hired.

The second was a layer named Pons, younger, with the broad, scarred hands of a man who had been wrestling blocks since boyhood. He was out of work because his previous master, a contractor known for cutting corners, had attempted to use river sand in the mortar for a retaining wall. Pons had refused to mix it. The argument had been short, violent, and terminal for his employment. “Sand’s got salt,” he told Amaury, his jaw tight with the memory of the injustice. “Salt draws damp. Damp finds the crack. The wall falls. Then it’s not the master’s name they curse, it’s the mason’s.” He had a wife and a new child in a single room near the tanneries. His loyalty was to the integrity of the wall, because the wall was all that stood between his family and ruin. Coin was necessary, but his pride was a cheaper, more potent currency. Amaury offered both. Pons accepted.

It was a start. A core of two, around which other, more malleable labour could be gathered. Amaury reported the hires to Isolde that evening in the stable yard, where they stood under a sky the colour of a fresh bruise.

“A purist and a moralist,” she said, a faint, wintry smile touching her lips. “You have built us a foundation of principle. Let us hope it bears the weight of practical malice.”

“They understand stone,” Amaury said. “That is the principle. The rest is just politics.”

“Politics is the mortar,” Isolde replied. “It binds everything, and it is always mixed by someone else. We must simply hope our stone is too heavy for their mix to hold.”

The return to Saint-Cloud was a journey into a different season. The clinging warmth of the city gave way to the sharper, greener air of the countryside. The hawthorns were in full, furious bloom, their scent a sweet, desperate counterpoint to the ever-present smell of turned earth and woodsmoke. The village, as they rode into it, seemed to have drawn a collective breath and held it. Work on the ruined chantry had ceased entirely. The rubble field was quiet, haunted only by crows and the ghost of the fire. The people moved with a subdued air, their glances at Amaury and Isolde quick, furtive, laden with a new kind of calculation. The news had travelled ahead of them, carried by some rustic rumour-bird faster than any horse. They were no longer just the interlopers, the stubborn fools. They were the Bishop’s fools now. This conferred a fragile, dangerous respect.

Bertrand Lacroix arrived two days later.

He came not with a retinue, but with a single clerk and two quiet, observant apprentices. He was a man of middling years, with a carefully trimmed beard and the settled, prosperous bulk of a master craftsman who has long since traded his trowel for a ledger. His eyes were the colour of slate, and they missed nothing. He inspected the site with a polite, thorough detachment, his fingers tracing the blackened edges of the old foundation stones as if reading a text.

“A tragedy,” he said, his voice neutral. “Good ashlar, much of it. A shame.” He did not look at Amaury as he said it.

He called a meeting in the lee of the one standing wall, unrolling the plans Amaury had submitted to the Bishop’s office. The vellum crackled in the spring breeze.

“The design is… ambitious,” Lacroix said, his slate eyes moving from the drawing to the reality of the uneven ground. “The apse is deeper than the original. The foundations will need to be taken down to a firmer stratum. A costly endeavour. Time-consuming.”

“It is necessary for the proportions,” Amaury said, keeping his voice as neutral as Lacroix’s own.

“Proportions,” Lacroix echoed, as if the word were a foreign coin he was testing with his teeth. “Of course. The Bishop was most insistent on the… spiritual resonance of the design.” He looked directly at Amaury for the first time. “You have worked from models?”

“I have studied what has endured,” Amaury said.

A faint, non-committal hum was the only reply. Lacroix pointed to the plan with a clean, un-calloused finger. “The delivery schedule for the dressed stone from the quarry. I will oversee it. My cousin’s husband has an interest in the wagons. We will ensure efficiency. You will, of course, be responsible for checking each load as it arrives. For quality.”

It was a masterstroke. He had given Amaury both a leash and the responsibility for its tightening. Any flaw in the stone would be Amaury’s oversight. Any delay would be Amaury’s poor planning, hindering Lacroix’s efficient cousin’s husband.

“Understood,” Amaury said.

Lacroix nodded, as if a minor administrative detail had been settled. “We begin the excavation tomorrow. My apprentices will mark the lines. Your men,” he said, with a slight pause that made the word your sound like borrowed, “can begin the digging.”

He left then, taking his clerk and his quiet apprentices with him, back to the comfortable house in the village he had doubtless already secured. Amaury remained, staring at the plans pinned by stones at the corners. The design, his design, their stolen hymn, looked back at him. It was a beautiful phantom. And Lacroix had just begun the process of clothing it in compromised flesh.

The work began. It was a symphony of dirt and strain. Pons and Thibaut, joined by a dozen village men eager for wages, swung picks and heaved baskets of earth. The rhythm was set by Lacroix’s apprentices, two serious young men who spoke only to correct. The lines they had marked were exact, unyielding. The foundation trenches bit into the earth, revealing the charred, pathetic remains of the old footings. The smell was of damp soil, crushed roots, and the faint, persistent ghost of old smoke.

Isolde became a fixture on a stool under a waxed cloth awning, her account books open on a trestle, a sheaf of the Bishop’s vellum beside her. She was the keeper of the legend, recording every penny spent, every day of labour, in a hand that was both elegant and implacable. She was a silent counterweight to Lacroix’s occasional, drifting presence. He would appear, observe, make a note in his own book, and depart. His politeness was a form of violence.

A week in, the first cart of stone arrived from the quarry. It was good stone, cleanly cut. Amaury inspected each block with Thibaut. They found no fault. It was almost a disappointment. The tension of waiting for the blow was worse than the blow itself.

The true interference was subtler.

It began with the mortar. The sand delivered was from a pit near the river, not the inland source Amaury had specified. Pons pointed it out, his face grim. “It’s damp,” he said, letting a handful trickle through his fingers. “Feel it. It’ll never mix true.”

Amaury confronted the carter, a sullen man with Mercet’s livery on his tunic. “This is not the sand I ordered.”

The carter shrugged, a massive, indifferent roll of his shoulders. “This is what was loaded. I drive. I don’t dig.”

Lacroix, summoned, examined the sand with an expression of mild concern. “A regrettable error at the yard. I will have it corrected. But to send it back… the cost in time, in wagon hire…” He let the sentence hang, a practical man lamenting impracticality. “Perhaps, for the lower courses, where the damp is less of a concern…? A compromise, Master Amaury. Building is the art of compromise.”

It was a test. To refuse would be to make a stand on a principle Lacroix could frame as wasteful, arrogant. To accept would be to let the first flaw be mixed into their wall, a hidden weakness that would whisper to the damp for decades.

Amaury looked at the pile of damp sand. He looked at Pons’s set, angry face. He looked at Isolde, watching from her awning, her pen still.

“We will use it,” Amaury said, his voice hollow. “For the footings of the outer wall only. Pons will mix it drier, with more lime. Every other batch, every stone that will be seen or bear a direct load, gets the proper sand. Send the order today.”

It was a retreat, but a fighting one. He had ceded a trench, not the field. Lacroix’s nod was a magistrate’s verdict. So be it.

The days piled up like the stone. The foundation trenches deepened, became orderly graves waiting to be filled with masonry. The rhythm of the work was a kind of forgetting. In the strain of his muscles, the focus on a plumb line, the consultation with Thibaut over a tricky joint, Amaury could almost forget the web they were building within. This was craft. This was truth. The stone fit, or it did not. The wall was plumb, or it was not. There was a purity in it that the world of bishops and cousins and marriage contracts could not touch.

He found himself talking to Isolde in a new way, in the deep evenings when the site was quiet and the bats flitted against the twilight. They spoke of voussoirs and keystones, of the problem of the rounded apse and the square crossing. It was a language of necessity, a bridge built between them over the chasm of their arrangement. She listened, asked sharp questions, learned the vocabulary of his world. In return, he felt the shape of her mind—not the borrowed piety of the Countess, but the fierce, strategic intelligence of the survivor. She was building too, a structure of influence and perception within the village, tending to the legend of the pious widow with the same care he gave to a cornerstone.

One such evening, as they shared a simple meal of bread, cheese, and the last of the wine from the city, she spoke without looking at him.

“The wife of the miller. She brought a poultice today. For my hands.” Isolde held them up; they were reddened, stained with ink, but no longer the soft, idle hands of a noblewoman. “She said the village is talking. They say we are… determined. They say Lacroix is from the city, and his heart is not in the stone, but in the tally sheet. They make a distinction.”

“It is a small distinction,” Amaury said, tearing the bread. “But in a wall, a small distinction is where the crack begins.”

“Or where the loyalty sets,” she said. “It is a foundation of a kind. More malleable than yours, but no less real.”

He looked at her then, truly looked. The fading light softened the careful austerity of her face, the composed mask of the Countess. He saw the fatigue beneath it, the stubborn will, the mortal woman holding a phantom at bay through sheer force of paperwork and presence. She was fighting a war on two fronts: against the rot within their own project, and against the decay of the fiction that clothed her. The cost of it was etched in the faint new lines at the corners of her eyes, in the careful way she held her shoulders, as if against a constant, chilling wind.

He felt a sudden, unexpected surge of something that was not pity, and not merely professional respect. It was a recognition of a shared burden, a shared mortality. They were both ghosts building a monument, trying to make something real enough to haunt the world back. The feeling was as unsettling as it was profound. He looked away, back to the dark outlines of the trenches.

“We should get the first stones laid before the feast of Saint John,” he said, his voice gruffer than he intended. “The light is long. The men will work.”

“Yes,” Isolde said, and if she noticed the shift in his tone, she gave no sign. “We should.”

The first cornerstone was laid on a morning of high, scudding clouds. It was a ritual, demanded by Lacroix, who produced a minor canon from a neighbouring parish to bless the stone. The ceremony was brief, the holy water sparse. The canon mumbled the Latin, his eyes on the promised lunch. The village watched, a silent, ragged congregation.

Amaury and Pons lowered the great block of limestone, its bottom bed already buttered with the good, true mortar, into its waiting cradle of earth. It settled with a final, gritty sigh. Amaury checked it with the level. The bubble floated, serene and perfect, in the centre of its glass prison.

It was straight. It was true. It was the first irrevocable fact.

As the men began to cheer, half-heartedly, Amaury stepped back. His eyes met Isolde’s across the site. She gave the faintest nod. The hymn had begun. The first note was sung.

That afternoon, the second cart arrived. It carried the stones for the inner face of the apse wall. Thibaut was upon them before the cart had fully stopped. His practised eye scanned, his hands touched. He let out a sound, a soft, disgusted click of his tongue. He beckoned Amaury over.

“Look,” he grunted, pointing.

Amaury saw it. The stone was good, but the dressing was wrong. The faces were rough-picked, not smoothly finished. On a hidden course, it might not matter. On the inner face of the apse, which would hold the altar and catch the light from the high windows, it would look crude, mean. A subtle insult, a gradual coarsening of their beautiful phantom.

“This is not to specification,” Amaury said to the same sullen carter.

Another shrug. “From the quarry, master. As it came.”

Lacroix was there, materialising as if summoned by the discord. He examined the stone, his head tilted. “A heavier finish. It has a… rustic vigour. And it is quicker to produce. Perhaps the quarry master, in his zeal to meet our schedule, took a liberty.” He turned his slate eyes on Amaury. “To reject it would mean a delay of weeks. The foundation sits empty. The weather, as you know, is not a patient creditor.”

The trap was elegant. A choice between quality and progress, between the ideal and the possible. The art of compromise.

Amaury felt the eyes of the village upon him. He felt Pons’s rigid silence, Thibaut’s simmering fury. He felt Isolde’s gaze, heavy as stone from her awning. He ran his hand over the rough-picked face. It was not bad stone. It would hold. But it was not what he had designed. It was not the hymn. It was a grunt, where there should have been a chord.

“We will use it,” Amaury heard himself say. The words tasted of damp sand. “For this course only. The next delivery will be to specification, or it goes back, and the cost of delay is on the quarry’s account. Make that clear.”

Lacroix smiled. It was a small, dry thing, devoid of warmth. “I will make it abundantly clear.” He did not move. “You are learning, Master Amaury. Building is a dialogue with reality. One cannot always be a poet.”

He walked away. Amaury stood amidst the rising walls of his compromise, the rough stone waiting to be lifted, the good mortar waiting to bind it. The phantom was gaining weight, gaining substance. But with every stone that was not quite right, it was becoming something else. Something other than the dream he and the dead Countess had stolen. It was becoming a building, yes. But whose?

That night, in the cramped room above the tavern that served as their joint chamber and scriptorium, the silence was a thick, cold mortar. Isolde was at the small table, her head bent over the accounts. Amaury stood at the narrow window, looking out at the dark bulk of the site, a deeper black against the starry sky.

“He is winning,” he said, not turning. “Not in a battle. In a dilution. He is making our vision… ordinary.”

The scratch of her pen stopped. “Ordinary things endure,” she said, but the usual steel in her voice was tempered with a new weariness. “Cathedrals are built by ordinary men, making ordinary compromises, one after the other, until the result is extraordinary in spite of them.”

“This will not be a cathedral. It will be a competent chantry, built with slightly inferior materials, to a slightly compromised design, by a man whose heart was not in it.” He finally turned. “That is what they will say. That is the truth we are building.”

Isolde looked up. The single candle painted hollows under her cheekbones, made her eyes pools of shadow. “The truth is that we are still building. While we build, we are alive. While we build, the Countess Isolde is alive. The moment we stop, for any reason, we are dead, and she is a corpse in a borrowed gown. The difference between a competent chantry and a ruin is the difference between a breathing woman and a skeleton. It is all the difference in the world.”

Her words hung in the close air. They were not about stone, or design. They were about the most fundamental compromise of all: the acceptance of a diminished reality over the absolute finality of death. It was the bargain of the living. It was the mortar of existence.

Amaury saw it then, with a clarity that was like a chisel blow. Their partnership was not just a fraud, or a strategy. It was a shared act of defiance against mortality itself. He was building to escape the grave of his own obscurity. She was building to escape the grave, full stop. In the face of that, what did a rough-picked stone matter?

He walked to the table. He did not sit. He looked down at the vellum, at her strong, clear hand recording the cost of the inferior stone, the cost of the damp sand. The ledger of their survival.

“The next delivery,” he said, his voice low. “If it is wrong, we send it back. Delay or no delay. We draw the line at the apse. That is the heart of it. If we compromise the heart, we might as well stop.”

She held his gaze for a long moment, then gave a single, slow nod. “The heart,” she agreed.

It was a pact. A line drawn not in the earth, but in the fragile territory of their resolve.

Three days later, the cart arrived. It carried the first of the arch stones for the apse window, the most delicate, most visible part of the curve. Thibaut and Amaury approached together. The stone was well-dressed, the arrises sharp. But as they turned one of the crucial voissoirs, they saw it. A flaw. A hairline crack, a fossilized weakness running through the heart of the block. It would hold, perhaps for years. Perhaps for a lifetime. But it was a flaw. A secret fault.

The sullen carter did not shrug this time. He just waited.

Lacroix was not there. He was in the village, meeting with the reeve about the hiring of local boys to fetch and carry.

Amaury looked at the stone. He looked at the empty curve of the foundation, waiting. He looked at Thibaut, whose face was stone itself. He thought of the line. The heart.

“Take it back,” Amaury said, his voice carrying across the quiet site. “It is flawed. We will not lay it.”

The carter’s eyes widened, just slightly. This was not in his script. “Master Lacroix said—”

“Take it back,” Amaury repeated, each word a block laid. “Tell the quarry master the next load must be perfect, or the contract with the Bishop’s office will be reviewed. Tell anyone who needs to know.”

There was a long pause. Then the carter spat into the dirt, nodded, and gestured to his mate. They began, with slow, theatrical reluctance, to heave the flawed stone back onto the cart.

Amaury turned. Pons was watching, a hard, approving glint in his eye. The village men had stopped their work, tools in hand, following the drama. And Isolde, from her awning, was watching too. She did not smile. But she inclined her head, once, deeply. It was a salute.

The victory was tiny, costly, and absolute. They would wait. The work would stall. Lacroix would be furious. The pressure would tighten like a vice.

But the heart, for today, would remain uncompromised. They had drawn a line in mortar, and for now, it held. The phantom had demanded its due, and they had paid, in the only currency they had: time, and the mounting interest of their enemy’s patience. The hymn was still theirs to sing, but the next verse would be sung in a higher, more dangerous key. The silence that settled over the site as the cart rumbled away was not the silence of peace. It was the silence of a drawn breath, held before a shout.

Chapter 17

CHAPTER 17

The silence of the drawn breath lasted three days. It was a brittle, watchful thing, stretched thin over the building site like the skin on cooled milk. The cart had not returned. No new stone arrived to fill the waiting curve of the foundation. The village men, under Pons’s gimlet eye, turned to the meticulous, slow work of trimming and squaring the blocks they already had, the tock-tock of their mallets a measured, anxious heartbeat. They were marking time, and everyone knew it. Time was a currency they were spending recklessly, and the treasury of Lacroix’s patience, they were certain, was running dry.

Amaury moved through the suspended days like a man walking a ledge. The victory over the flawed stone had been a spike of pure, clean fire in his chest. Isolde’s silent salute had been a draught of cold, clear water. But the fire had banked to embers, the water had dried on his tongue, and the ledge was growing narrower. He felt Lacroix’s absence as a pressure, a gathering thunderhead on the horizon. The master builder was not a man to accept a line drawn in his mortar. He was a man who erased lines.

Isolde, under her awning, was a statue of waiting. She no longer sketched the phantom. She drew instead the details of the site: the calloused hands of the masons, the particular sag of a rope, the way light fell through the wooden ribs of the unfinished apse. She was documenting the quiet war. Her presence was no longer a curiosity; it was a fact, as established as the foundation itself. The men no longer glanced her way with unease. They glanced at her for a sign, a reading of the weather. She gave none, her face a calm mirror reflecting their own tension back at them.

On the fourth morning, Lacroix returned.

He came not with a roar, but on a sigh. He rode into the compound on his neat mare, his robes dusty, his face the colour of old parchment. He did not look at the gap in the foundation. He did not look at Amaury, who stood by the tracing floor, a lump of chalk drying in his hand. He dismounted, handed the reins to a boy, and walked directly to the awning.

“My lady,” he said, his voice scraped raw from the road. “A word, if you would spare it for a humble servant of the Bishop.”

His humility was a blade, finely honed. Isolde set her charcoal down. “You are returned, Master Lacroix. The reeve’s business was concluded?”

“The reeve,” Lacroix said, with a smile that did not touch the chill in his eyes, “is a man who understands the flow of goods, and men, and favour. A practical man. We reached an understanding.” He stepped closer, into the shade of the awning. His scent, of horse and sour sweat and something metallic, like ink, pushed against the smell of her paper and charcoal. “I understand there was some… difficulty with a delivery in my absence.”

“The stone was flawed,” Isolde said. Her voice was flat, a stone itself.

“Flawed.” Lacroix tasted the word. “A subjective term, in the hands of the inexperienced. The quarry master is insulted. He speaks of broken faith. He is a man with other clients, other great works. The Bishop’s favour is vast, but it does not flow infinitely. It is a river that can be diverted by the stubbornness of a… stone.”

He let the implication hang. The stone was not the stone. The stone was Amaury. The stone was her.

“The heart cannot be flawed,” Isolde said, repeating Amaury’s doctrine as if it were scripture. “You have said this yourself. It is the first principle.”

“Principles are the luxury of those who build with other men’s money and other men’s time,” Lacroix murmured, so low only she could hear. “We build with the Bishop’s money. His time is measured in the lifetimes of his ambitions, not in the seasons of a quarry. A flaw can be hidden. A delay cannot.”

He finally turned his head, his gaze finding Amaury across the compound. It was not a look of anger. It was a look of calculation, of reassessment. The phantom had not just demanded its due; it had created a lieutenant.

“The carter will return tomorrow,” Lacroix announced, his voice lifting to carry. “With a new stone. It will be laid. The work will proceed. We have lost enough of the Bishop’s daylight with this… fastidiousness.”

The drawn breath was released. Not in a shout, but in a low, collective exhalation from the men. The verdict was in. The line had been erased. Amaury felt the chalk in his hand crumble to dust.

The new stone arrived at noon the next day. It was, to any eye but the most trained, identical to the first. Grey, veined, massive. The same carter and his mate unloaded it with the same sullen efficiency, but their eyes darted now, rabbit-quick, between Lacroix, who watched from the door of his lodge, and Amaury, who stood rooted by the tracing floor.

Amaury approached as the oxen were led away. He walked a slow circle around the block. The grain was true. The colour consistent. He knelt, running his palms over the surface. Cool, rough, unyielding. He could see no fissure, no hidden weakness. It was, by every measure he possessed, perfect.

And yet.

It was a different stone. The first block, the flawed one, had been a problem he could see, a enemy he could name. This was a silent replacement. A correction. Its very perfection was a rebuke. To lay it would be to concede. To accept Lacroix’s rewritten law: that the appearance of the heart was all that mattered, not its truth.

Pons came to stand beside him, his shadow falling over the stone. “It’s clean,” the old mason grunted.

“Yes.”

“We cannot refuse it.”

“No.”

Pons spat a glob of phlegm onto the dust, just missing the stone’s edge. “Then the fight changes. It is not about the stone anymore. It is about the mortar.”

Amaury looked up at him. Pons’s face was a landscape of old grudges and older wisdom. “The mortar?”

“The heart is not just the stone, boy. It is the stone set. It is the marriage. A perfect stone laid with grit in the bed, or with slurry mixed thin, is a flawed heart. It will weep. It will crack. It will fail.” He gave a grim, gap-toothed smile. “Lacroix commands the stone. He commands the men. But the mortar? That is alchemy. That is in the wrists, and the eye, and the weather of the day. That,” he said, nodding toward the mixing troughs, “is still ours.”

A spark, tiny but fierce, struck in the gloom of Amaury’s spirit. The hymn had not ended. It had shifted to a minor key, a more subtle harmony. He stood, his knees popping. “We lay it after Sext. The sun will be past the yardarm; the shadow will be right for the truing.”

Pons’s nod was a slow, deep thing. “Aye. We’ll lay it in the shadow.”

The preparation was a sacrament. Amaury mixed the mortar himself, with sand from the pit beyond the river, lime from the kiln that had burned for three days straight, and water drawn fresh from the well. He worked it with a hoe, folding and turning, until the consistency was thick as butter and smooth as cream. The smell was sharp, ancient, the smell of buried seas and fire.

When the bell for Sext faded, the shadow of the western wall stretched a long, cool finger across the foundation trench. The perfect stone hung in its ropes, suspended over the gap. The men manned the winches, their muscles corded. Lacroix emerged from his lodge to observe, a ledger tucked under his arm, a living monument to the tally.

Amaury stood in the trench, in the shadow. He wore a simple tunic, smeared with dust and lime. In his hands was a wooden hawk, loaded with the first bed of mortar. He looked up. Isolde had left her awning. She stood at the edge of the trench, her hands clasped before her, a sentinel in blue.

“Gently!” Pons barked. The stone began its descent.

It was a dance of terrible slowness. The block swayed, a pendulum of immense weight. Amaury’s world narrowed to the space beneath it: the cleaned rock of the foundation, the waiting mortar on his hawk. He could hear the grunt of the men, the groan of the ropes, the dry whisper of Lacroix turning a page.

The stone kissed the mortar bed with a soft, wet sigh. Amaury dropped to his knees, ignoring the cold seep of the earth. With a trowel, he began to work the oozing mortar into the joint, packing it deep, ensuring no void, no pocket of air, would remain to haunt them. The stuff was cold and alive under his tool. This was the marriage. This was where the phantom lived, not in the stone, but in the union.

He worked with a furious, silent concentration. The shadow deepened. The men eased the ropes, letting the stone’s full weight settle. Amaury packed the final scoop, smoothed the seam until it was a hairline, a promise. He sat back on his heels.

The stone was laid. The heart, in its visible, perfect form, was in place.

Lacroix closed his ledger with a snap that echoed in the quiet. “It is done,” he said. The words were not a celebration. They were a period at the end of a sentence. He turned and went back inside.

The men began to coil the ropes, their talk a low murmur. The tension of the day bled away into the ordinary fatigue of labour. But Amaury remained in the trench, staring at the joint. Pons climbed down beside him, handed him a skin of water. Amaury drank, the water tasting of leather and lime.

“It is a good bed,” Pons said, squatting to peer at the work.

“It is,” Amaury said. But his eyes were on the shadow, now creeping up the side of the new stone. It was no longer just a shadow. It was a stain. The perfect stone, laid in shadow, would forever bear the memory of this moment, of the compromise that had set it there. Its perfection was a lie, and the mortar that held it, for all its purity, was the glue of that lie.

He climbed out of the trench. Isolde was still there. She met his eyes, and in her grey gaze he saw she understood completely. The victory had been stolen, replaced with a colder, more durable defeat. They had kept the flaw out of the heart, but they had let the shadow into its making.

“It will hold,” he said to her, his voice hoarse.

“Yes,” she said. “But will it sing?”

He had no answer. The phantom, he realized, did not just demand perfection. It demanded truth. And they had just buried a lie at the heart of their hymn.

That night, a different fever visited the site.

It began with Thibaut, the sullen carter’s mate. He had taken his wage and drunk it in the village, returning to the labourers’ lean-to long after dark, his breath a fog of cheap wine. By matins, he was rolling on his pallet, bathed in a sweat that smelled not of labour but of sickness, a sweet-rotten odour that cut through the smells of hay and men. He babbled of cold stones and hot shadows.

By prime, two other men who had handled the new stone—men who had guided it with their hands into its final rest—were shivering, their teeth chattering like dice in a cup. Their skin, when Pons roughly pulled back a sleeve, was hot and dry, marbled with a faint, rosy rash.

Plague.

The word went through the compound not as a shout, but as a hiss, the sound of fear sucking air between its teeth. Men drew back from the lean-to as if from a leper. Work ceased. The tock-tock of mallets was replaced by the sound of muttered prayers and the crackle of a fire being built high, to burn the bedding.

Lacroix emerged from his lodge, a cloth held over his nose and mouth. His eyes, above the linen, were not fearful, but furious. Sickness was a thief of time far more profligate than any fastidious clerk.

“The village barber-surgeon,” he commanded a trembling servant. “Fetch him. And isolate the sick. No one leaves the compound.”

Amaury stood at the edge of the panic, watching. The sickness had not touched him, nor Pons, nor any who had worked only with the mortar. It had chosen the men who had laid hands on the perfect, shadow-kissed stone. A coincidence, a trick of bad air or fouled water. It had to be.

But as he looked at the groaning men, at the terror in the eyes of their comrades, he felt the cold logic of the phantom. It was a different kind of flaw. A flaw in flesh. The stone had rejected them. Or the shadow within it had leaked out, a miasma of compromised intention, and found purchase in their mortal humours.

Isolde came to him. She did not flinch from the sight or the smell. Her face was pale, but set. “This is not the fever that took my mother,” she said. “That was a burning thing. This is… a cold fire.”

“It is a distraction,” Amaury said, though he did not believe it. “Lacroix will use it. He will say the site is cursed, that our delays have invited ill humours.”

“Perhaps they have,” she said, her gaze drifting to the foundation, where the new stone sat, innocent and grey in the morning light. “Not by delay, but by the acceptance of a lie. The phantom is not a gentle muse. It is a jealous god. It punishes impurity.”

The barber-surgeon arrived, a pinched man with bloodstained cuffs and the resigned air of a man who mostly shaved beards and lanced boils. He examined the men, held a polished stone to their throats, sniffed their breath, and pronounced it a pestilential fever, caught from the night air or from the malice of a witch. His prescription was bleeding, a draught of strong wine with powdered wormwood, and prayer.

As the man set out his rusty fleams, Amaury turned away. He walked to the foundation trench. He stared down at the heart-stone. In the stark morning light, it looked ordinary, inert. A thing of the earth. But he remembered the cold sweat of its surface under his palms. He remembered the shadow.

Love mortality.

The phrase surfaced in his mind, unbidden. To love the mortal was to love the flawed, the impermanent, the dying. The phantom demanded an immortal perfection in stone. But the men who served it were mortal. They sweated, they lusted, they feared, they sickened. They were flawed stone. To build for God was to bridge that impossible gap: to use dying hands to create an undying thing. But if the pursuit of perfection killed the men… what then was the hymn for?

He heard a step behind him. It was Lacroix, the cloth still masking his lower face, giving him the look of a bandit or a grave-robber.

“This is a test,” Lacroix said, his voice muffled. “Sent to try our resolve. The work must not stop. You and the old man, and those still hale, will continue with the coursing. The sick will be quarantined. We will burn juniper to cleanse the air.”

“We are masons, not physicians,” Amaury said, still looking at the stone.

“You are servants of a will greater than your own,” Lacroix corrected, his eyes cold above the linen. “The stone does not care if you are sick. The plan does not care. The Bishop does not care. Only the finished work matters. The men are clay. They are replaceable. The heart,” he said, nodding toward the foundation, “is not.”

There it was. The final, brutal arithmetic. Lacroix did not see a phantom. He saw a diagram. He did not see a hymn; he saw a tally of blocks laid, of time spent, of obstacles removed. The mortality of the men was just another column in his ledger, a cost to be minimized.

Amaury felt a hollow open inside him, a void colder than the foundation trench. He had thought the fight was about stone and mortar. It was not. It was about souls. Lacroix would build his perfect, hearted church even if it required a foundation of human bones. He would do it efficiently, without malice, because in his theology, the end was all. The men were not brothers-in-hymn; they were units of labour.

“We will work,” Amaury said, the words ash in his mouth. “But we will also tend to our own.”

Lacroix’s eyes crinkled, the only sign of a smile beneath the cloth. “A noble sentiment. See that it does not slow the work. The Bishop’s visit is now confirmed. He will be here in six weeks, to see the walls at the springing of the vaults. The heart will be buried, the work will be rising. It will be a testament to faith. To order.”

He walked away, leaving Amaury alone with the stone and the sounds of sickness drifting from the lean-to.

The drawn breath was over. The shout had begun. It was not a shout of rage, but of suffering. And Amaury knew, with a certainty that settled in his bones like a chill, that the phantom’s next demand would be in a currency far more precious than time. It would ask for a sacrifice not of principle, but of flesh. The line they had drawn, and had seen erased, would now be drawn in the very substance of their mortality. And he would have to choose which side of that line his soul, and Isolde’s, would stand upon. The hymn was becoming a dirge, and the shadow at the heart of the church was beginning to drink the light.

Chapter 18

CHAPTER 18

The sickness was a patient craftsman. It did not rush its work. It settled into the lean-to with the permanence of a new tenant, its tools a racking cough, a sweat that smelled of spoiled hay, and a fever that turned a man’s eyes the colour of tarnished pewter. It worked not on stone, but on breath. It measured its progress not in courses laid, but in silences where once there had been the grunt of effort, the rasp of saws.

Amaury had thought the line was one of principle. He had been a fool. Principles were for the well, for men with breath to spare for abstractions. Here, the line was drawn in the dirt floor of the infirmary, between the pallets of the living and the shrouded shapes laid in the cool dark of the unfinished crypt. It was a border that shifted each dawn, its geography rewritten by the night’s quiet departures.

He moved through it now, a phantom of fatigue. The hollowness Lacroix had opened in him had become a vessel, filled to the brim with the thick, sweet-sour air of decay and the sounds of men trying not to drown in their own lungs. He carried water, wiped brows, and when the craftsman finished its work, he helped Brother Reynard, whose hands now shook perpetually, wrap the results in coarse linen.

Isolde was his compass in this new, interior country. She worked without speaking, her own face drawn tight over its fine bones, a wimple of rough hemp covering her hair. She had become a study in economy: the precise fold of a cloth, the exact angle to lift a man’s head for a sip of willow-bark tea, the minimal pressure of a cool hand on a burning forehead. Her love, once a vast and open country, had been surveyed, mapped, and reduced to a series of essential, tactical gestures. It was no less potent for its efficiency. It was, Amaury saw, a love that had accepted mortality as a business partner. It did not rage against the dying of the light; it simply lit a smaller, more focused candle, and shielded it from the wind.

“He is asking for you,” she said, her voice low. She did not need to name him. There was only one ‘he’ now.

Lacroix had not entered the infirmary. The line, for him, was absolute. He conducted his inspections from the threshold of the mason’s lodge, a square of clean, unmuddied stone, his masked face turned toward the ceaseless activity at the walls. The work was rising. The shout of suffering from the camp was met, measure for measure, by the shout of chisels on stone. The foundation was complete, the heart-stone buried and blessed in a brief, perfunctory ceremony from which the sick had been screened by a canvas drape. The walls of the nave were now chest-high on a tall man. The geometry of salvation was asserting itself, indifferent to the biology festering beside it.

Amaury found him there, a ledger open on a drafting table, a rule in his hand. He looked up, and his eyes, above the linen, were the clear, untroubled blue of a summer sky seen from a great height.

“The mortar mix on the north wall is too rich in lime,” Lacroix said, by way of greeting. “It will craze before it cures. See it is corrected.”

Amaury stared at him. The words were stones dropped into the hollow inside him, producing no echo. “Gautier died last night. Alain is bleeding from his ears. The sickness is in the town. The baker’s wife is gone.”

Lacroix placed the rule down carefully, aligning it with the edge of the table. “Then we shall need a new baker. And more sand for the mortar. The Bishop’s visit is in five weeks and five days. The vaults must be sprung. The phantom’s price, it seems, was not so high. A few men. A delay measured in days, not weeks. We are ahead of the ledger.”

It was the calm that did it. The serene, numerical assessment. The hollowness in Amaury overflowed, not with heat, but with a cold, clarifying fluid.

“You see them as a cost,” Amaury said, his voice flat. “A column in your book. You are wrong. They are not a cost. They are the currency. The phantom is not trading for their lives. It is spending them. And you are the mint.”

For the first time, Lacroix’s stillness seemed deliberate, a held pose. The liquid eyes did not change, but the skin around them tightened, minutely. “You are fatigued. The miasma from the sick-pit clouds your reason.”

“My reason is the only clear thing here,” Amaury said, taking a step forward. He smelled of the infirmary, of death and thyme, and he saw Lacroix’s nostrils flare behind the mask. “The hymn. You said it was a call for a heart. But a heart needs a body. A church needs a congregation. You are building a perfect, hearted shell for ghosts. You are constructing a tomb, not a testament. The phantom knows it. It is not hindering you. It is fuelling you. Every man who dies from this… this efficiency… adds another block to the wall. Their silence becomes part of the acoustics.”

Lacroix looked at him for a long moment. Then he picked up his rule again, tapping it lightly against his palm. “A poet’s fancy. The Bishop requires a church. I am building it. The method is irrelevant. The men are mortal. The Church is eternal. Which would you have me serve?”

“God is not a ledger-keeper,” Amaury spat.

“Are you certain?” Lacroix asked, the question gentle, almost curious. “He demands sacrifice. He balances sin against grace. He tallies souls. My only heresy, perhaps, is in my clarity.” He turned back to his plans. “The mortar. See to it.”

It was the shattering. Not with a cry, but with a whisper of vellum. The false victory Amaury had built in the previous weeks—the belief that they could tend their own, that a line of care could exist within Lacroix’s diagram—collapsed into dust. There was no battle between the work and the sickness. They were in symbiosis. The church grew taller on the sustenance of their dying.

He walked back to the infirmary, the world rendered in the stark lines of Lacroix’s vision. The groans of the sick were the friction of the machine. The shrouded bodies were its waste product. And he and Isolde were not healers; they were its tender, smoothing the passage of raw material from one state to the next, making the process more efficient, less disruptive to the primary function.

Isolde was waiting, a bowl of water in her hands. She read his face as she read a fevered man’s—the set of the jaw, the distance in the eyes. “What did he say?”

“He said we are ahead of the ledger,” Amaury replied, his voice hollow.

She absorbed this. She did not flinch. She placed the bowl down, slowly, precisely. “Then we are not fighting the sickness. We are feeding it.”

He nodded, the motion heavy. “The phantom’s next demand. It will be for flesh. Not just any flesh. A specific sacrifice. To consecrate its… its investment.”

Her gaze went to the pallet where young Luc, the carver of angels, lay burning and muttering about wings. Then it travelled to the open doorway, to the relentless climb of the walls, grey against the grey sky. “It will want a life that matters to the work. One that would be a true subtraction from Lacroix’s column.”

“Or one that would bind the work to it forever,” Amaury murmured. The thought formed, cold and complete. “A cornerstone. Not of stone. Of spirit.”

Their eyes met. The understanding was instantaneous, a shared chill. The phantom had been playing a longer game than any of them had imagined. It did not want to stop the church. It wanted to own it. To be woven into its very essence. And for that, it needed a soul not just taken, but given. A willing sacrifice, or one made with such love and anguish that its resonance would stain the stones for eternity.

“It will come for one of the core,” Isolde whispered. “Gérard. Reynard. You.”

“Or you,” Amaury said, the words tearing his throat.

Silence stretched between them, filled with the sounds of the dying and the distant, rhythmic percussion of the build. The love they had, which had become a tactical, sustaining thing, now faced its final audit. To save the work, or what remained of its soul, would require spending the most precious currency they possessed. Each other.

It was Brother Reynard who brought the summons. He shuffled in, looking older than the foundation stone, his habit stained. He did not look at them. “The master mason,” he croaked. “He requires you both. At the east end. Immediately.”

The tone was wrong. Reynard’s fear was usually a hot, sputtering thing. This was cold, congealed.

They went, the dread a third companion walking between them. The east end of the nascent church was where the altar would stand, where the morning light would first strike. Now, it was a forest of scaffolding and stacked ashlars. A group of masons stood in a loose, uneasy circle. Lacroix was at the centre. And at his feet lay Gérard.

The master carpenter was on his back, his face a mask of shock and pain. His left leg was twisted beneath him at an impossible angle, the stout wool of his hose dark and wet. A massive, shaped keystone block, meant for the first arch of the apse, lay beside him, one corner crusted with red.

“He was directing the placement,” Lacroix said, his voice devoid of inflection. “The hoist rope frayed. The block fell. It missed his head. It did not miss his leg.”

Gérard’s eyes were open, swimming with pain. He tried to speak, only a wet gasp emerging.

“The bone is shattered,” Lacroix continued, clinical. “It is a pulp to the knee. The bleeding is… profound. Brother Reynard’s arts will not suffice. It will fester. He will be dead in three days, in great agony.” He looked up, his gaze settling on Amaury and Isolde. “Or, the leg can be removed. Now. Cleanly. It may save him.”

A collective breath was drawn in by the men. Amputation was a death sentence more often than not. But a slow one, or a fast one?

Gérard understood. A wild, animal panic filled his eyes. He shook his head, a frantic jerking motion.

“He does not consent,” Isolde said, her voice cutting through the murmur.

“His consent is secondary,” Lacroix replied. “He is a vital asset. His knowledge of the centering for the vaults is irreplaceable. Losing him would set us back a month. We do not have a month.” He turned his whey-pale eyes to Isolde. “You have a healer’s hand. A steady nerve. You will do it.”

The world narrowed to the space between Lacroix’s words and Isolde’s face. Amaury saw the blood drain from her lips, saw the tremor she suppressed in the hand she held at her side. He saw the calculation in Lacroix’s gaze—this was the test. This was the line, redrawn in Gérard’s flesh. Would she serve the work, or the man? Would she become the instrument of the ledger, performing a brutal calculus to save a column of time?

“No,” Amaury said, stepping forward.

“You lack the skill,” Lacroix dismissed him, his attention locked on Isolde. “It must be quick. Clean. The saw must be hot. You understand necessity, my lady. You have shown it these past weeks. This is no different. It is a pruning. To save the tree.”

Isolde looked at Gérard, at the terror in his eyes. She looked at the monstrous block, at the dark, spreading stain on the ground. She looked at Lacroix, a creature of pure, unblinking purpose. And Amaury saw the moment her own purpose crystallized. It was not acceptance. It was recognition. This was the phantom’s demand. Not for Gérard’s life, but for hers. For her to become the sacrifice, to surrender her healer’s soul to the machine, to let her love be perverted into a tool of efficiency. It would bind her to this place, to this church, as surely as any ghost. Her anguish would be the mortar for its highest vault.

“I will not,” she said, the words clear, bell-like in the hushed air.

Lacroix blinked, slowly. “Then he dies. And the work falters. And the Bishop sees failure. And your phantom wins.”

“The phantom wins if I do it,” she said. “You are its instrument, Master Lacroix, not its opponent. You are delivering me to it, gift-wrapped in necessity.” She knelt beside Gérard, ignoring the blood, and took his calloused hand. “I will ease his pain. I will stay with him. I will not be your butcher.”

A strange light entered Lacroix’s eyes. Not anger. Something akin to respect, and profound disappointment. “Sentiment. The final, intractable flaw in the material.” He turned to the masons. “Take him to the infirmary. Let him die there. Assign Michel to study his drawings. We have five weeks and four days.”

As the men moved, lifting Gérard gently, his cries of pain sharp in the air, Lacroix stepped close to Amaury. His voice was a whisper, for him alone. “You see? This is the mortality love chooses. It chooses the single, dying sparrow over the soaring cathedral. It is a beautiful, fatal flaw. And it is why the work will always be done by those who understand the greater mathematics.”

He walked away, towards the unblemished north wall, his rule already in his hand.

Isolde stayed with Gérard through the afternoon and into the deepening twilight. Amaury brought her water, which she did not drink, and a cloth, which she used to wipe the carpenter’s brow. She gave him sips of a dark, syrupy poppy tincture, enough to blur the edges of the agony, not enough to stop it. The carpenter’s breath grew ragged, his big body struggling against the damage within.

Amaury watched her. Her love was no longer tactical. It was absolute, and it was doomed. It was a small, bright flame held against a vast, consuming dark. It would not save Gérard. It would only bear witness. And in bearing witness, it would burn itself into the memory of every man who saw it, a more potent consecration than any bishop’s blessing.

Gérard died just before compline. It was not peaceful. It was a final, shuddering battle against the ruin of his flesh, which he lost. His last breath was a sigh that seemed to carry all the sawdust and planning of his life out of him.

Isolde closed his eyes. She did not cry. Her face was a monument to spent compassion.

Outside, the rhythmic sounds of work had ceased. The men were gathered, silent, in the twilight. They had heard. They knew. The false community of the hymn was truly dead. In its place was a new knowledge: they were expendable not just in principle, but in practice, and the only mercy available would refuse the knife.

Lacroix emerged from the lodge. He surveyed the silent men, the stilled worksite. He walked to the east end, to the spot where the blood had darkened the earth. He took a small, sharp mason’s hammer from his belt, and a newly-cut, square ashlar waiting to be laid. In full view of them all, he knelt. With precise, deliberate blows, he chipped a small, neat notch from the corner of the stone.

He stood, holding the stone aloft. “This,” he announced, his voice carrying in the still air, “is the first stone of the apse. It bears the mark of our labour. Of our sacrifice. Gérard’s life is in this stone. His memory will be in this wall. We will build it for him. We will build it with him.”

He handed the stone to the nearest mason. “Set it. Now.”

The man took it, his face pale. The gesture was obscene in its brilliance. Lacroix had taken their grief, their horror, and had transmuted it. He had made the death part of the architecture. Gérard was no longer a tragedy; he was a foundational anecdote. The phantom’s currency had been accepted, minted, and was now being laid into the sacred geometry.

The work began again, the sounds hollow in the gathering dark. The shout of suffering had been incorporated into the hymn.

Isolde walked out of the infirmary, past the men, past Lacroix, towards the river. Amaury followed. She stood on the bank, staring into the black, swift water.

“He was right,” she said, her voice thin. “I chose the sparrow. And the sparrow died. And the cathedral grows anyway.”

“You chose your soul,” Amaury said. “He has sold his.”

“Has he?” She turned to him, her eyes reflecting the last grey light. “What is a soul, Amaury, but a kind of heart? And he is building a perfect heart. He is just using different materials. Our love. Our mortality. Our refusal.” She wrapped her arms around herself. “The phantom’s demand… it wasn’t for me to cut. It was for me to refuse. To create the… the tension. The friction. The perfect, hearted machine needs a sacrifice it cannot consume. It needs a love that says ‘no.’ That is the final ingredient. That is what gives the ghost its hold. My ‘no’ will be in every stone laid from this night forward.”

The profundity of it staggered him. Lacroix saw the refusal as a flaw. Isolde saw it as the key. The phantom was not building on death. It was building on witnessed, unanswered suffering. On the space between the knife and the mercy. That was the shadow at the heart. That was the darkness drinking the light.

“Then we must give it an answer,” Amaury said, the words forming from a deep, cold well of resolve. “Not refusal. Not consent. Something else.”

She looked at him, waiting.

“The phantom wants a sacrifice woven into the fabric. Lacroix is providing them. We cannot stop the building. But we can… re-consecrate it. We can change the story in the stones.”

“How?”

He looked back at the site, at the dark silhouette of the walls against the starless sky, at the pinpricks of torchlight where men worked through the night, laying the stone that bore Gérard’s mark. “We give it a different heart. Not one of stolen mortality. Not one of efficient suffering.” He took her cold hands in his. “We give it ours. Not by dying. By living. By building something into it that Lacroix cannot see, that the phantom cannot use. A secret. A true hymn. One of life, not a dirge.”

Her fingers tightened on his. “A secret in the heart of his perfect church.”

“A flaw in the material,” Amaury whispered. “But not of sentiment. Of hope.”

The hook was set. The work would rise, a juggernaut of order and death. But within its walls, in the very fabric of its sanctity, they would now begin a different construction. A hidden architecture. It would require theft, deception, a risk greater than any refusal. For if Lacroix’s church was a testament to the mathematics of salvation, theirs would be a gamble on the algebra of grace. And the first step would be to steal a piece of the heart, and replace it with something living, before the Bishop arrived to sanctify the tomb.

Chapter 19

CHAPTER 19

The stone was a lie.

Amaury knew this as he ran his thumb along its cold, dressed edge. It was a perfect block of pale Caen limestone, hauled from the quarries at ruinous expense, cut and squared by masons who sang hymns as they worked. It would form part of the inner skin of the apse, the holy curve that would cradle the altar. On its visible face, the banker-mason had incised a tiny, precise cross—a mark of consecration. On its hidden bed, the side that would marry eternally with mortar and the stone below, Amaury had, an hour before dawn, scratched a different sigil. It was not a cross. It was the rough outline of a leaf from the white mulberry that grew in the castle’s lower bailey, a tree that had, according to kitchen gossip, fruited the year Isolde was born.

The lie was not in the leaf. The lie was in the assumption of inertness. Lacroix, the Bishop, the phantom in the ground—they all believed stone was a terminal thing. A conclusion. A suitable vessel for a final, captured moment. Amaury, his fingers stained with dust, understood the stone was a beginning. It was a seed. Their secret was not an act of vandalism, but of insemination.

The challenge was one of adulteration. How do you poison a well that is meant to contain only purity? You do not pour in venom. You drop in a single, living spore of something else. You trust the water to carry it.

“The Bishop’s party has passed the eastern gate,” a voice murmured at his shoulder. It was Thibault, the quiet mason from the Limousin whose daughter had survived the sweating sickness because Amaury, breaking quarantine, had brought her mother yarrow and stubborn hope. Thibault’s loyalty was not bought; it was woven, a subtle thread in the growing, hidden tapestry. “Twenty horsemen. The wagon with the relics is drawn by white oxen. Lacroix meets them at the site.”

Amaury gave a single, slow nod. The air was different today. It had the brittle, polished quality of a blade unsheathed for ceremony. The usual cacophony of the site—the shout of quarrymen, the grind of the great treadmill crane, the chime of iron on stone—was muted, replaced by a low, reverent hum. The workers moved with a stiff, conscious grace, like actors in a sanctified play. They were building the stage for a miracle, or for a burial. Amaury was no longer certain there was a difference.

He looked across the skeletal nave. The arcades were rising, the ribs of a great stone beast. At the eastern end, where the foundation trench was deepest, the air shimmered with a cold that had nothing to do with the spring morning. That was where Gérard had died, his life measured out not in prayers, but in the diminishing space between a falling stone and the unyielding earth. The phantom’s gallery. Its hunger was a palpable texture now, a greasy film on the back of the tongue. It did not rage. It waited. It was a connoisseur, and today it was to be served a feast.

His eyes found Isolde. She stood apart, near the stonemasons’ lodge, a figure in grey wool against the pale chaos. She was watching the eastern road. Her posture was that of a sentinel, but her hands, clasped before her, were restless, the fingers worrying at a simple ring of twisted silver. He had given it to her two nights past, a thing forged not by a jeweller but by the castle blacksmith from a melted-down spoon. A token of their conspiracy. A conductor for their silent, desperate hymn. Seeing it on her hand, a dull gleam against the grey, sent a current of fierce, terrifying warmth through his chest.

The procession wound into view. First the cross-bearer, then the choirboys in scarlet, their voices thin and sharp as icicles. Then the Bishop himself, a mountain of purple and damask, his mitre a gilded shell atop a face that was all shrewd, desiccated planes. And beside him, a half-pace behind in perfect deference, walked Lacroix. The master builder wore a new houppelande of dark blue, the cut severe, the fabric swallowing the light. He was smiling. It was a small, technical expression, a blueprint of satisfaction.

The Bishop halted at the edge of the foundation, where the first course of ashlar met the raw, dark soil. He raised a hand. Silence fell, complete and swallowing.

“Locus iste a Deo factus est,” the Bishop intoned, his voice a dry rustle of parchment. “This place was made by God.”

Amaury felt the words sink into the ground. They did not bounce. They were absorbed, like blood into a sponge. The phantom was listening.

The ceremony of consecration was a long, fragrant torture. Incense uncoiled in thick, sweet ropes, battling the scents of damp earth and cold mortar. Psalms echoed off the unfinished walls, finding odd corners and hollows where they seemed to repeat, softer, in a different key. The Bishop blessed the four corners of the world, sprinkled holy water that glittered like flung diamonds in the sun. He processed along the lines of the future walls, a slow, ponderous navigation of an invisible ship.

And through it all, Lacroix’s eyes were everywhere. They were calipers. They were levels. They measured the bow of a chorister’s head, the angle at which a monk held a candle, the slight unevenness in the terrain where Amaury stood. They were compiling a ledger of the sacred, ensuring its arithmetic was correct.

Finally, the reliquary was brought forward. A small casket of oak banded with iron. The Bishop received the key from Lacroix—a deliberate, staged transfer—and unlocked it. From within, he drew out a bone, yellowed and porous, wrapped in silk.

“A fragment of the tibia of Saint Eligius,” the Bishop proclaimed. “Patron of metalworkers, of craftsmen. He who shaped precious things for earthly kings, and in turn shaped his soul for the King of Heaven.”

A perfect choice. Not a martyr’s dramatic remnant, but a worker’s bone. A sanctification of labour. Of this labour. Lacroix’s theology of the chisel and the T-square made flesh, or rather, made relic.

The cavity was ready in the foundation stone of the high altar—a square, dark mouth in the pristine limestone. The Bishop lowered the silk-wrapped fragment into the stone’s gut. A mason stepped forward with a trowel of mortar. This was the moment of sealing. The moment of interment.

Lacroix’s gaze swept the crowd and found Amaury. It was a glance of pure, unadulterated possession. See, it said. See the perfection of the mechanism. The saint’s bone enters the stone. The stone enters the wall. The wall defines the sacred. The sacred justifies the wall. The circle is closed. There is no crack.

The mason smoothed the mortar. The relic vanished from the world.

A collective breath was released. The Bishop began a final prayer.

It was then that Isolde moved.

She did not walk towards the ceremony. She drifted sideways, like a leaf caught in a draft from an unseen door. She moved to the northern arcade, where the new stones were still bare, awaiting their cladding. She stopped before a specific pillar, one that Amaury had marked for her two days prior. Its base was already set, its foundation stone hidden below the turf. But its core was hollow, a temporary void for scaffolding.

From the folds of her grey gown, she drew a small, cloth-wrapped bundle. Her movements were not furtive, but deliberate, slow, as if she were performing a liturgy of her own. She knelt, her skirts pooling on the damp ground. She reached into the hollow heart of the pillar and placed the bundle within, pushing it deep into the shadows where the mortar would soon flow.

Amaury knew what it contained. A lock of her hair, woven with a strand of his. A petal from the first primrose she had found that spring, pressed flat. A tiny, crudely carved wooden bird, a thing Gérard had whittled for a child in the village, found among his pitiful effects. And a single, smooth river stone, warm from being carried in a pocket for a week. Not a relic of a saint. A relic of the living. A catalogue of fragile, persistent things.

She rose, her face pale but serene. She dusted her knees, a practical, human gesture amidst the grand theatre of sanctity. She had planted their seed.

No one saw. The entire assembly was fixated on the sealed altar stone, on the Bishop’s raised hands, on the promised sanctity. Only Amaury saw. And, he realized with a jolt of ice down his spine, Lacroix.

The master builder was not looking at the Bishop anymore. He was looking at Isolde. His head was tilted, the way a master mason examines a stone with a hairline fracture, assessing whether it can bear the load or must be discarded. His technical smile was gone. In its place was a pure, clinical curiosity. He had not seen the act, but he had seen the posture, the intention, the deviation from the planned emotional script. He had seen a human variable introduce itself into his equation.

The ceremony concluded. The Bishop was led away to refreshments. The sanctified site began to exhale, the workers shifting, murmurs returning. Lacroix did not immediately follow the Bishop. He walked, with his precise, efficient stride, directly to Isolde.

Amaury’s blood became a silent roar in his ears. He began to move, but Thibault’s hand, firm on his arm, held him back. “Not now,” the mason breathed. “You will only paint the target brighter.”

Lacroix stopped before her. He did not speak at first. He simply looked at her, his eyes taking inventory: the silver ring, the dust on her hem, the particular stillness of her face.

“My lady Isolde,” he said finally. His voice was polite, a tool kept sharp. “You seemed… moved by the ceremony.”

“It was a powerful rite,” she replied. Her voice did not waver. It was a clear, cool stream. “To bury a piece of a saint. To make death the cornerstone.”

“Not death,” Lacroix corrected gently, as if tutoring a clever but misguided child. “Transcendence. The bone is not a corpse. It is a token of a life that escaped corruption. It is a promise that the material world can be a vessel for the immaterial. A blueprint, if you will, for the resurrection of the body.”

“I see,” she said. “So the church is built on a promise.”

“On a guarantee,” Lacroix said. “The mathematics of grace are absolute. The relic is the first axiom. From it, all else follows.” He paused, his gaze drifting to the pillar beside her, then back to her face. “Some seek to build on softer foundations. Sentiment. Memory. The fleeting impress of a feeling on the air. It is a common error. A understandable one. But it produces only… shrines. Not cathedrals.”

He was speaking about her. About Gérard. About the hidden bundle in the stone. He knew. Not the specifics, but the category of the crime. The crime of introducing the personal into the perfect.

“A shrine can be a true thing,” Isolde said.

“Oh, undoubtedly,” Lacroix conceded, with a slight dip of his head. “But it is a private truth. It cannot bear the weight of a community’s hope, of a kingdom’s doctrine. It cracks under the load. My concern,” he said, taking a half-step closer, his voice dropping to a confidential register that was more threat than intimacy, “is that in seeking to create a small, personal shrine within a cathedral, one risks introducing a point of failure. A hidden flaw. And in a structure of this ambition, a single flaw does not remain local. It propagates. It undermines the whole.” He smiled again, the technical expression returning. “I would hate for your… personal devotions… to become the cause of a greater collapse.”

The threat hung in the air, more palpable than the incense. He was not threatening her life. He was threatening her meaning. He would let her plant her seeds, and then he would point to the cracking wall and name her the blight.

“I have faith in the builder,” Isolde said, meeting his eyes. “To incorporate all things into his design.”

Lacroix’s smile froze for a fraction of a second. Then it warmed, becoming something genuinely horrifying in its approximation of paternal approval. “A pious sentiment. Let us hope the builder’s vision is equal to the faith placed in him.”

He bowed, a crisp, efficient motion, and turned to follow the Bishop.

Amaury reached her side as the crowd dispersed. He said nothing. He simply stood beside her, their shoulders not touching, but the space between them charged.

“He knows,” she whispered, watching Lacroix’s receding back.

“He knows the genus of our act,” Amaury corrected, his voice low. “Not the species. He sees a weed. He does not see the root, or the flower. We must be the stone that receives his scrutiny and shows him only conformity.”

“And the phantom?” she asked. “Did it take the saint’s bone?”

Amaury looked toward the altar foundation. The air above it was still. No shimmer. No unnatural cold. It had a sated, quiescent quality. “It took it,” he said. “It drank the guarantee. It is digesting certainty.” He turned to her. “Our offering is different. It is not a guarantee. It is a question. A possibility. We must hope the hunger is complex enough to taste the difference.”

The work resumed with a new intensity. The consecration had acted as a spur. The site was now officially holy ground, and every block laid was an act of piety. The rhythm of the crane, the chant of the masons setting stones, took on a zealous, pounding tempo.
