The church was rising faster, as if the relic in its heart was exerting a gravitational pull on the stone.

Amaury and Thibault worked their secret liturgy in the gaps. A river stone set beneath the threshold of the south transept door. A feather from a wren trapped in the mason’s lodge, wrapped in a scrap of linen with the word light inked in berry juice, placed in a core wall. Each was a negation of the final. Each was a breath held in the stone.

But the phantom was not idle. Its satisfaction with the relic was not contentment; it was an awakening of a more refined appetite. Two days after the consecration, a labourer from the quarry, a man known for his strength and his silence, was found at dawn seated against the rising wall of the nave. He was dead. His eyes were open, fixed on the eastern end where the altar would be. His face was calm, but his hands were clenched into fists so tight the nails had broken the skin of his palms. There was no mark on him. No sign of struggle. The physician, summoned from the town, muttered about a seizure of the heart, a fate that befalls even the strong.

Amaury examined the man’s position. He was not lying down. He was seated, as if he had paused to rest and simply never moved again. His gaze was a direct line to the sealed relic. It was a votive offering. The phantom had accepted the saint’s bone, and now it was collecting the interest. A life, not taken in violent refusal like Gérard’s, but quietly, reverently extracted. A tithe.

Lacroix ordered the body removed before most of the workers arrived. He spoke to the quarrymen, his voice calm, assuring them their comrade had died in a state of grace, within the consecrated bounds, and that this was a mercy. He promised a mass. He paid the widow a solatium from the building fund. It was efficient. It was tidy. It was another stone laid in the edifice of acceptable loss.

That night, Amaury found Isolde in the castle chapel, not praying, but staring at the small, dusty altar.

“It is learning from him,” she said, without turning. “It is becoming more… elegant. Gérard’s death was a protest. This was an endorsement. It is starting to build according to Lacroix’s plans.”

“Then we must build faster,” Amaury said.

Their opportunity came from an unexpected quarter. The Bishop, pleased with the progress and the aura of sanctity, had gifted Lacroix a small, exquisite casket of Lebanese cedar, intended to hold the finer drafting tools. The casket was lined with raw, undyed silk. Lacroix, in a rare moment of what might have been vanity, or perhaps merely a desire to integrate the gift fully into his system, ordered that the silk be used. Not for tools. It was to be cut into strips and mixed into the mortar for the central pillar of the crossing—the physical and symbolic heart of the church, the point where nave and transepts crossed.

It was a masterstroke. The fabric of a holy gift, rendered into fibres, would bind the heart-stone. The material would literally hold the geometry together.

When Amaury heard the order, he felt the hook of fate tug deep. This was it. The centre. Not the altar’s foundation, which held a dead saint, but the living, breathing intersection. If their hidden hymn was to have a chorus, it must be here.

He volunteered to prepare the mortar batch. Thibault would assist. The silk was delivered to the mixing trough—a bolt of fabric the colour of cream, finer than anything most of the men had ever touched. The order was precise: one strip, no larger than a finger’s length, per mixing.

As Thibault brought water and lime, Amaury stood over the silk. His heart was a frantic bird in his chest. This was not a hidden stone or a dark cavity. This was integration at the molecular level. To adulterate this was to risk everything. Lacroix would inspect this mortar. The phantom would feed on it.

He drew his knife. He cut the prescribed strips, his movements exact. Then, from inside his tunic, he drew out the only thing he had that belonged, inextricably, to a living love. It was the strip of linen she had used to bind the cut on his hand the night they had first spoken of refusal, weeks ago. It had long since been washed, but a faint, rust-brown shadow of his blood remained, a ghost of a wound healed. He had kept it, not for sentiment, but as a witness. He laid it beside the holy silk. The contrast was brutal. One was pure, virgin, a gift. The other was stained, personal, a record of repair.

With a single, swift motion, he cut a strip from his linen, identical in size to the others. He folded it into a tiny, tight square. He took one of the Bishop’s silk strips and did the same. He dropped both into the dry mix of lime and sand.

Thibault poured in the water. They began to turn it, over and over, with heavy hoes. The fibres tangled with the grit, the holy and the mended twisting together, becoming indistinguishable. The mortar was a pale, thick paste, holding its secrets.

They brought the trough to the crossing. The master mason overseeing the heart-stone—a vast, drum-shaped block—nodded his approval. The mortar was applied. The great stone was lowered, settling with a final, grinding sigh that seemed to shake the very earth.

It was done. The heart of the church contained, now, a double helix: the Bishop’s sanctified gift, and the proof of Amaury’s healed flesh. A covenant and a scar, mixed into one.

That evening, as the sun bled out behind the scaffolding, Lacroix made his inspection. He walked the site alone, his hands clasped behind his back. He paused at the crossing. He knelt. He ran his fingers over the fresh mortar joint, his touch as delicate as a physician’s. He stayed there for a long time, his head bowed, as if listening.

Amaury watched from the shadows of the masons’ lodge, his mouth dry. Had he been too bold? Had the stain of his blood, however faint, whispered a betrayal to Lacroix’s exquisite senses?

Lacroix stood. He did not look angry. He looked… puzzled. He stared at the joint, then at his fingers, as if they had told him something his mind could not yet parse. He turned slowly, his gaze sweeping the darkening site. It passed over the lodge, over the shadows where Amaury stood, frozen. It did not stop. But it did not dismiss, either. It was the gaze of a man who has heard a single, wrong note in a vast and perfect harmony. He could not find the source. But he knew the song was no longer flawless.

He walked away, back towards his chambers, his step for the first time lacking its absolute, metronomic certainty.

Amaury let out a shuddering breath. The flaw was in the material. Not a crack. A complexity. Lacroix had sensed it. The phantom, bound to the mortar, would taste it. The question was: would it reject it as poison, or would it, in its hunger for meaning, be forced to digest a new and baffling ingredient—the taste of a love that had chosen to mend, rather than to sacrifice?

The hook was set not just in the stone, but in the builder’s certainty. The hidden architecture was now part of the load-bearing structure. And the next stone to be laid would be the keystone of the chancel arch, the stone that locked the altar’s view of heaven into place. Lacroix would be watching. The phantom would be feeding. And Amaury, holding Isolde’s silver-ringed hand in the dark, would have to decide what final, living truth they could possibly steal to place in that final, locking embrace.

Chapter 20

CHAPTER 20

The keystone was a wedge of honey-coloured limestone, a dense, sober tooth waiting to be fitted into the jaw of the arch. It sat on a bed of straw in the masons’ lodge, a patient, weighty secret. To Amaury, it looked less like a stone and more like a verdict, yet to be pronounced.

He had not slept. The memory of Lacroix’s puzzled, searching gaze was a cold stone in his own gut. The man’s certainty had been a kind of foundation, terrible and immovable; its tremor was more frightening than any rage. Amaury had spent the night in the crypt, not working, but sitting with his back against the tomb of the nameless knight, Isolde’s silver ring a cool band against his chest. He was waiting for a sign of rejection—a fever in the mortar, a weeping crack, the phantom’s rage manifest as a chill that killed the lichen on the walls. Nothing came. The silence was not peaceful. It was the silence of a palate considering a strange new wine, trying to decide if the odd, bright note was genius or corruption.

At dawn, the site steward, a man whose face was a roadmap of old grievances, found him. “The Master Builder wants it set today. Before noon. He’ll do the blessing himself.”

This was unprecedented. Lacroix observed rites, he did not perform them. His presence was the blessing. For him to take the trowel, to speak the words… it was an act of reassertion. A purification. Amaury felt the hook in his own flesh twist.

“I’ll prepare the bed,” Amaury said, his voice rough.

“He’s already preparing it,” the steward said, with a faint, malicious satisfaction. “He’s up there now. With the other mortar.”

Amaury’s blood became a slurry of ice. He climbed the spiral stair to the choir, his legs leaden. The space was empty of workers, the morning light slicing through the eastern windows in thick, dusty bars. At the summit of the chancel arch, on the wooden platform that served the apex, Lacroix stood alone. He had a small, pristine hawk in his hand. Beside him sat a new tub of mortar. Its surface was unblemished, a perfect grey lake.

Lacroix heard his approach and did not turn. “The mix from yesterday was flawed,” he said, his voice carrying down in the clear, still air. “A taint in the sand, perhaps. Or the lime was restless. It spoke of… incompletion.”

Amaury reached the platform, the wood creaking under him. He was close enough to see the fine grain of Lacroix’s knuckles, white around the hawk’s handle. “It seemed sound to me, Master.”

“Your senses are competent, Amaury. They are not exquisite.” Lacroix finally looked at him. His eyes were not puzzled now. They were sharp, analytical, like a surgeon’s probe. “There was a resonance. A dissonance. Like a string slightly out of tune. The harmony of this place is mathematical. It is spiritual. It is also material. The mortar is the breath between the stones. It must be neutral. It must carry, not comment.” He gestured to the new tub. “This is pure. It will silence any… lingering echoes.”

Panic, clean and bright, shot through Amaury’s veins. The phantom would taste nothing in that sterile mix. The hook would be discovered, a bare, meaningless thing. Lacroix would scrape out the joint Amaury had poisoned with his blood and love, and replace it with a deaf mortar. The hidden architecture would collapse before it was ever loaded.

“The keystone is the lock,” Amaury said, the words coming out too fast. “Should its mortar not have… character? A finality? The old mix may have been speaking, not of flaw, but of readiness.”

Lacroix’s head tilted a precise degree. It was the motion of a scribe finding an interesting error in a manuscript. “Readiness? You anthropomorphise the material. A dangerous sentimentality. Stone wants only to obey its nature and the geometry imposed upon it. Mortar wants only to bind and be forgotten. Any other desire is a corruption.” He lifted a trowel, its edge catching the light. “We will use the new mix. You will assist. You will watch the purity of the act.”

It was a sentence, not an invitation. Amaury was to be an audience to his own undoing, his own erasure. He gave a stiff nod, his mind racing over a cliff-edge. As Lacroix turned to inspect the bed of the arch once more, Amaury’s gaze fell to the new tub of mortar. Pure. Silent. Dead.

The procession of the keystone was a sombre affair. Eight masons, their faces solemn, carried it on a stout litter from the lodge, up the nave, and into the choir. It was not a joyous rite, but a funeral for doubt. Lacroix led them, a high priest in a plain linen shirt, his hands clasped behind his back. The workers gathered below, a silent congregation.

The keystone was hauled up to the platform with ropes and a small crane, a slow, grunting ascent. It hung in the air between Amaury and Lacroix, turning slowly, its finely-dressed faces gleaming. The space left for it in the arch was a dark, hungry mouth.

“The mortar,” Lacroix said.

Amaury bent to the new tub. He dipped the large trowel into the grey mix. It was cool, uniform, and utterly inert. It had no memory, no hunger. It was the perfect servant. He laid a heavy bed of it onto the stone sill of the arch’s left side, then the right. The action felt like a betrayal of the highest order.

Lacroix took the trowel from him. With a fastidiousness that was almost violent, he scraped away Amaury’s application. He mixed the mortar in the tub once more, as if chasing out even the ghost of a bubble. Then, with his own hands, he laid a fresh, thin, perfect bed. He did the same on the other side. His movements were a liturgy of exclusion.

“Now,” Lacroix said, stepping back. “Guide it home.”

The masons on the ropes eased their burden. The great wedge of stone descended. Amaury, positioned on the left, and another mason on the right, placed their hands on the cool limestone, guiding its final inches. The world narrowed to the shrinking gap between the keystone and the waiting mortar. Amaury could smell the clean, mineral scent of the new mix. It smelled of nothing.

The stone touched. It was a soft, profound kiss. A sigh seemed to go through the very scaffolding. Lacroix leaned in, his eye a caliper, checking the alignment. It was perfect. The keystone settled into its beds, the mortar squeezing out in a thin, grey smile.

Lacroix took up a smaller pointing trowel. He would now seal the joints, completing the act. This was the moment. The phantom, if it fed at all today, would taste only this bland, formal offering. It would learn nothing. It would not be forced to digest Amaury’s truth.

As Lacroix’s trowel swept up a final curl of mortar, Amaury acted. His movement was not a lunge, but a stumble, a craftsman’s loss of balance on the narrow platform. He knocked against the tub of pure mortar. It was a heavy, clumsy thing. It toppled.

Lacroix spun, a hiss of irritation escaping him. But the spill was not the disaster. The tub, falling, struck the edge of the platform and cracked, vomiting its grey contents. But it also struck the older, half-empty tub from the previous day—the tub of the flawed, the resonant, the speaking mortar. That tub rocked, teetered, and then spilled backwards, its contents—the mortar Amaury had charged with his blood and Isolde’s memory—slurping out in a thick, slow wave.

It poured directly onto the top of the newly-set keystone.

For a heartbeat, there was only the sound of the mortar slapping against stone. Amaury stared, horrified at his own success. Lacroix stared, frozen.

The grey, meaningful slurry oozed over the apex of the arch’s lock, seeping into the narrow space between the keystone and the voûsoirs, dripping down the faces in long, dirty tears. It was a desecration of the pure, precise act. It was also an inoculation.

Lacroix’s face went through a series of subtle, terrifying transformations: shock, then fury, then a cold, calculating containment. He looked from the ruined mortar dripping down his perfect stone, to Amaury still sprawled on the platform, to the broken tubs.

“Clumsy,” Lacroix said, the word a shard of glass. But his eyes were not on Amaury’s clumsiness. They were on the mortar. The old mortar. He watched it seep, as if observing a poison entering a wound. “This… residue. It must be removed. Every trace.”

He snatched a rag and knelt by the keystone, his back to Amaury. He began to scrape and wipe with a frantic, focused intensity. He was not cleaning a mess; he was performing an exorcism. He scraped at the dripping mortar, but in doing so, he only worked it deeper into the microscopic pores of the limestone, into the hairline seam where the keystone met its pure, silent bed.

Amaury got to his knees, muttering apologies. He fetched water, more rags. Together, in a terrible pantomime of repair, they scrubbed at the stone. But the stain of the meaningful mortar was not just physical. It was present. It had touched the lock. It had been introduced.

After an hour, the keystone was clean to the eye. The pure joints below were sealed, flawless. The arch stood complete, a bridge of stone between the earthly choir and the proposed heaven of the vault. But the apex, the very crown, had been anointed with a different sacrament.

Lacroix stood back, breathing heavily. He examined his hands, then the stone, then his hands again. The puzzlement was back, deepened, fermented into a quiet dread. “It is done,” he said, but it sounded like a question.

He left the platform without another word, descending the ladder with the stiff care of an old man. The workers below, sensing a storm had passed but left a changed atmospheric pressure, slowly returned to their tasks. The choir filled with the hesitant sounds of labour.

Amaury remained above, alone with the arch. He placed his palm flat on the keystone, now warm from the sun and their frantic scrubbing. He closed his eyes. He listened.

At first, there was nothing. Only the distant chip of chisels, the call of a mason below. Then, a vibration. Not in the stone, but in the silence behind his ears. A low, resonant hum, like a bell too large to be heard, only felt in the teeth and the bones. It was not the hungry, whispering vacuum of the phantom’s feeding. It was different. Fuller. It had a texture.

The phantom had tasted the pure mortar, the silent offering. And then, immediately, it had been given the other. The flawed mix. The mix that spoke of a love that chose to mend. The contrast was not a rejection. It was a shock. A confrontation.

The harmony Lacroix worshipped was indeed mathematical, spiritual, material. But Amaury had introduced a fourth term: the moral. A choice. A refusal. The phantom, an entity born of absolute sacrifice, was now digesting the taste of a love that had refused to sacrifice its beloved. It was being forced to metabolise a paradox.

The hum in Amaury’s skull resolved, for a fleeting second, into something almost like a chord. A major triad, sweet and shocking in this place of grey austerity, followed instantly by a dissonant, grinding minor second. Agreement and argument, happening at once. The phantom was arguing with itself.

He took his hand from the stone. The sensation faded, leaving a profound emptiness in its wake. He had done it. He had hooked the entity not with a greater sacrifice, but with a philosophical dilemma. He had made its hunger a crisis of conscience.

The victory was ash in his mouth. For what was a crisis of conscience in a starving, disembodied will, if not the prelude to a fit?

He descended. The rest of the day passed in a blur of normalcy that felt like a lie. Lacroix did not reappear. Word came he had retired to his chambers, pleading a megrim. Amaury worked, pointing other joints, his body moving by rote. His mind was in the keystone, listening to a silent, spiritual digestion.

At twilight, he went to the crypt. He needed to tell Isolde. He needed to feel the solidity of her ring, the finality of her tomb. He lit a single candle and sat against the knight’s effigy.

He had been there only minutes when he heard the step. Not Lacroix’s measured tread. This was lighter, quicker. Amaury blew out the candle, melting into the shadow of a pillar.

Brother Alban, the librarian, slipped into the crypt. He carried no light. He moved with a nervous, bird-like certainty to the Countess’s tomb. He did not pray. He ran his hands over the lid, his fingers searching the carved stone lilies. Then, he pressed. There was a click, a soft, stone-on-stone sigh. A small, hidden compartment at the base of the tomb slid open.

Alban reached in and withdrew a book. Not a psalter. A small, leather-bound journal, its cover worn to a deep umber. He clutched it to his chest, his head bowed for a moment in what looked like grief, or guilt. Then he closed the compartment and hurried away, his form swallowed by the dark of the stair.

Amaury’s blood thumped in his ears. Isolde’s journal. The librarian had it. He had stolen it, or was its guardian. It was not lost. It was hidden. And Alban, the man who knew the cathedral’s every written secret, had just retrieved it in the dead of night, after the keystone was set.

The hook was in the stone. The phantom was feeding on a confounding truth. And now, the key to Isolde’s own truth—her side of the story, her voice, her why—was in the hands of a frightened monk. Amaury knew, with a certainty that chilled him more than Lacroix’s puzzlement, that these two events were not separate. The journal’s reappearance was the phantom’s first response. It was regurgitating a piece of its own history, forcing it into the light, just as it was being forced to digest a new and terrible idea.

The architecture was no longer hidden. It was interacting. And the next move would not be his, or Lacroix’s. It would be the phantom’s. Amaury had wanted to teach it. Now, he would have to survive its education.

Chapter 21

CHAPTER 21

The journal was a small, dry weight in the dark, a second heart beating against Amaury’s ribs. He waited in the crypt’s ink until the echo of Alban’s fleeing steps had been absorbed entirely by the stone, until the only sound was the drip of distant water and the thrum of his own blood. The phantom was quiet. It had fed on the keystone’s truth, and now it had offered a scrap of its own in return. A transaction. A terrible, intimate commerce.

He did not follow Alban. The librarian’s path would be a maze of known shadows, of choir stalls and scriptorium desks. Amaury’s path was the architecture itself, and it had just revealed a new, hidden joint. He approached Isolde’s tomb.

In the absolute dark, his fingers became his eyes. They traced the cold, familiar contours of the carved lilies on the side of the sarcophagus, the smooth channels of their stems, the hard points of their petals. He found nothing. He pressed, as Alban had, at the centre of each bloom, at the junctions, at the leaves. Stone. Implacable stone.

He knelt, his knees cracking against the flags. The base of the tomb was a plain plinth, the join between it and the main body a sharp, clean line. He ran his fingertips along it, feeling for a seam, a catch, a breath of difference. On the side facing the knight’s effigy, his nail caught. A hair’s-breadth gap, not where the plinth met the tomb, but within the plinth itself. He pressed, not down, but in, at a specific point where the grain of the marble disguised a slight depression.

The same soft, stone-on-stone sigh. A section of the plinth, no larger than a prayer book, slid inward an inch. He hooked his fingers into the gap and pulled. The compartment was shallow, lined with dry, dusty velvet. Empty, now, of everything but the ghost of the object it had been built to conceal.

Amaury sat back on his heels. This was not a later addition. The craftsmanship was seamless, contemporary with the tomb itself. Isolde, or someone who loved her, had commissioned her final rest with a secret. A place to keep a truth too fragile for the light of day, or the finality of the grave. He thought of her hands, folded over her jewelled psalter in the great hall. A performance. The real scripture of her last days had been tucked away here, a silent, papery heartbeat waiting beneath the stone lilies.

The phantom had known. It had always known. It had digested the fact of the tomb, the weight of her body, the scent of her decay. And it had digested the secret of the compartment, the pressure of the hidden book. This was its memory. Its gut-feeling. And now, provoked, it had guided Alban’s nervous fingers to the trigger. It was not thinking. It was reacting, like a stomach convulsing around a poison.

Amaury stood, his limbs stiff with cold and revelation. The journal was not a clue left for him. It was a piece of the phantom, forcibly disgorged. To read it would be to read the phantom’s own digested history. A dangerous meal.

He left the crypt, not for the cloister, but for the cathedral’s north transept. A small, iron-bound door led to the spiral stair that climbed the library tower. He had no plan to confront Alban, not yet. The monk would be terrified, guilty, a bird with a stolen seed in its beak. He needed to see the place where the journal had lived, to understand the ecology of this secret. The air in the stairwell was thick and still, smelling of old paper, dust, and the faint, sweet tang of decaying leather.

The library was a long, low gallery under the tower’s leaden roof, its windows mere slits in the massive walls. It was less a room than a canyon of knowledge, the shelves towering into the gloom, the spaces between them choked with the shadows of centuries. The only light came from a single, shuttered lantern on the great reading desk, its glow a small, defiant campfire in a wilderness of words.

Brother Alban was not at the desk. Amaury moved silently between the shelves, his shoulder brushing against the rough vellum of chained folios. He could feel the phantom here, too, but differently. It was not the active, gnawing presence of the crypt or the choir. Here, it was a patient, listening silence. It had digested these books, not their contents, but their physicality—the smell of ink, the creak of bindings, the slow, granular decay of paper. It was a library of sensations, not stories.

He found Alban’s cell at the far end of the gallery, a niche barely larger than a tomb, curtained off with a worn tapestry depicting Saint Jerome and his lion. The tapestry was pulled aside. Within, on a plain straw pallet, Alban sat. He was not reading the journal. He was holding it, his hands wrapped around it as if to warm it, or to stop it from speaking. His face, in the thin light from the lantern outside, was the colour of old parchment, his eyes wide and unseeing.

Amaury did not enter. He stood at the edge of the tapestry’s shadow and spoke, his voice low, blending with the rustle of mice in the walls.

“She entrusted it to you.”

Alban did not startle. He simply turned his head, slowly, as if the movement cost him a great weight. His eyes focused on Amaury with a terrible clarity.

“You saw.”

“I saw you take what was hidden. I saw the tomb give it up. Did she ask you to keep it? Or to destroy it?”

Alban’s throat worked. He looked down at the journal. “She gave it to me the night before the fever took her. She said… she said it was the only true thing she would leave behind. That the rest was costume. She told me where to hide it. To keep it safe from… from the household. From the Count.” He swallowed. “She did not say for how long.”

“And you have kept its secret all these years.”

“I have prayed for guidance.” Alban’s voice was a dry leaf of a sound. “To burn it would be to murder her voice. To keep it is to hold a ghost. To give it to the Count would be a betrayal. So I have done nothing. I have let it sleep. Until tonight.”

“What woke you tonight, Brother?”

Alban’s gaze drifted past Amaury, towards the heart of the cathedral. “It called. Not with a voice. A… pressure. In the stones. In the air. A need. I knew, as I know my own name, that it was time. That the hiding was over.” He looked back at Amaury, a flicker of accusation in his eyes. “You did this. You and the other one, the mason. You stirred the waters. You made it hungry.”

Amaury stepped into the cell. He did not reach for the journal. He sat on the wooden stool by the pallet, making himself small, unthreatening. “It is not hunger. It is indigestion. We have fed it a truth it cannot stomach. It is retching up the past in response. This,” he nodded at the book, “is the first heave. You felt its distress. You are… attuned.”

“I am its librarian,” Alban whispered. “Its keeper of the dead words. I feel the sigh of the shelves when the air grows damp. I hear the groan of the tower in a high wind. This place… speaks to me. And tonight, it screamed in silence.” He thrust the journal towards Amaury. “Take it. You have awakened the need. You must satisfy it. Read her truth. Let it be digested. Perhaps then it will be quiet.”

Amaury took the book. The leather was smooth, almost buttery from years of handling. It felt alive. “And what of your vow to her?”

Alban closed his eyes. “My vow was to keep it from those who would use it for malice. You… you are not of this world. You are a phantom-hunter. Your malice is for the thing in the stones, not for her. Read it. And then, for the love of God, let her rest.”

Amaury did not return to his alcove. He took the journal to the one place in the cathedral that felt, if not neutral, then acceptingly dead: the chantry chapel of the Reynard family, a neglected side-apse where the plaster saints had lost their faces to damp. Here, by the guttering light of a stolen candle, he opened Isolde’s last testament.

The handwriting was not the formal, practised script of a noblewoman’s correspondence. It was swift, sloping, often blurred, as if written in haste or under strong emotion. It was not a chronicle. It was a conversation with herself, a desperate mapping of a shrinking world.

20th April. The physician has been again. He speaks of humours, of black bile, of a melancholy of the womb. He is a kind man, and a fool. My sickness is not in my blood. It is in this room. It is in the sound of Guibert’s tally-stick clicking against his teeth as he reviews the rents. It is the smell of the rushes, which they change too seldom. It is the weight of the wimple, which is my crown and my shroud. He prescribes tansy and abstinence from red meat. I prescribe air. I am denied both.

3rd May. Saw the mason Lacroix in the lower courtyard today, arguing with the master carpenter over the tracery for the new rose window. Such passion over stone and light. He gestured with his hands, carving the air into shapes of such beauty I felt my breath catch. Guibert would see only the cost of the glass. Lacroix sees the sun’s victory over lead. I am a countess. I am not permitted to speak to such a man. So I watched from the gallery, and for a moment, the sickness lifted. I was not a prisoner of damp stone, but a spectator to creation.

15th May. A terrible row tonight. The steward caught a kitchen boy stealing sugared almonds. Guibert had the boy’s hand placed on the chopping block in the yard. He took the meat-cleaver himself. His aim was poor. It took three blows. The sound… it was not like butcher’s work. It was a wet, splitting of something young. I vomited into my lap. Guibert was displeased. He said my weakness encouraged thievery. He said a ruler must be a sharp lesson. I looked at his face, so calm, so certain. I did not see a man. I saw a mechanism. A tally-stick that counts in blood.

The entries were not daily. They were eruptions. Amaury read of her silent observations, her sharp, poetic condemnations of the court’s petty cruelties, her fleeting moments of stolen joy in a shaft of sunlight through glass, in the scent of rain on the herb garden. And he read of Lacroix.

1st June. He was in the cloister, studying the join of the new vaulting. I was walking with Alban, discussing a translation of Augustine. Our paths crossed. He bowed. I nodded. Our eyes met. In his, I saw the man who argues with the sun. In mine, he told me later, he saw a woman listening to the stones. We did not speak. We did not need to. Alban, dear, oblivious Alban, prattled on about textual corruptions. The world had narrowed to a point of silent recognition in a sun-dappled walkway. The sickness fled. For an hour, I was well.

10th June. A note, slipped into the psalter Alban left for me. A single line. ‘The keystone accepts the weight, and makes it grace.’ I know what it means. He sees the weight I bear. He tells me I can transform it. I have read the line a hundred times. It is my prayer.

25th June. We have found a way. The building is our conspirator. A gap in the scaffolding behind the apse. A place where the eye of the household cannot go. We meet there. We do not speak of love. That word is too small, too chewed-over by troubadours. We speak of weight and balance, of light and shadow. He touches my face. His hands are rough with stone. They are the most honest things I have ever known. With him, I am not a countess. I am Isolde. I am the woman who laughs at the wrong moments, who prefers stonemason’s drawings to tapestries, who is, at last, alive. It is a treason of the highest order. It is the only truth I have ever committed.

Amaury’s candle burned low. The phantom was silent around him, but he felt it listening, a vast, attentive presence in the walls. He was feeding it, word by word. He was letting it taste the memory of its own creation from the inside.

12th July. I am with child. There is no doubt. The timing… it could be Guibert’s. The world would assume it is. But I know. I know in the way the air tastes, in the way my blood sings. It is his. A life carved from secret joy, not dutiful loathing. I have not told him. I am afraid. Not for myself. For him. Guibert’s justice is a blunt, final thing.

20th July. I told him today. In our secret place. He did not speak. He took my hands, those great, stone-working hands, and he wept. Then he smiled. It was like watching the sun break through the worst gloom. He said we would flee. He had contacts in Burgundy. We would go before my condition showed. We would live as simple people. He would build houses, I would keep a garden. We would be people. It was a mad, beautiful dream. We planned for the new moon. When the building site is darkest.

The next entry was dated three days later. The handwriting was different—shaky, the ink blotted.

23rd July. Guibert knows. I do not know how. He has not confronted me. He looks at me over the dinner table with the mild curiosity of a man examining a flawed ledger entry. He has posted extra guards on the walls. He has called Lacroix to his chamber tomorrow to discuss ‘urgent modifications’ to the tower foundations. A cold terror has settled in my bones. It is not a fear of death. It is a fear of his correctness. He will not rage. He will rectify.

There was a blank space on the page. Then, a final entry, written in a frantic, sprawling hand, the letters stumbling over each other.

He is gone. Sent to the quarries at dawn on a pretext to select marble. An armed escort. He will not return. Guibert told me, smiling his thin smile, as he broke his fast. ‘A talented man, but restless. The mountain air will focus his mind.’ I am alone. The sickness is back, but it is not melancholy. It is a living thing. A cold fire in my veins. Guibert has asked the physician to attend me this evening. He is concerned for my ‘feverish humours.’ I am not a fool. I have given Alban this book. If I am gone, it is not a fever. It is a tally. The final entry. Forgive me, my love. I could not transform the weight. It has crushed me.

The journal ended.

Amaury closed the book. The chantry chapel was freezing. The candle flame guttered, drowning in its own wax. He understood now. The phantom’s love was not for the cathedral. It was for the secret. For the stolen moments in the scaffolding, for the whispered plans, for the life that was cut short. Its mortality was Isolde’s. It had digested her hope, her terror, her final, cold understanding. And it had digested Lacroix’s grief, which must have been a torrent when he returned to find her sealed in stone, the official story a bland lie about a sudden fever.

The keystone. Lacroix’s final, furious act. Not a tribute to divine glory. A grave marker. A testament to a murder disguised as a misfortune. He had carved their child, the unseen, unconceived future, into the heart of the stone. He had fed the phantom the truth of its own creation: it was built on a foundation of love and loss, yes, but also of a specific, calculated crime. Amaury had just confirmed the victim’s testimony.

The phantom now knew, truly knew, what it was. It was not a spirit of place. It was the embodied grief of a wronged man, fused with the final, desperate consciousness of a murdered woman, baked into the stones they had both loved. It was a tomb for two.

And Amaury had just read the epitaph aloud to the tomb itself.

A sound pulled him from the awful clarity. Not from the journal, or the walls. From the cathedral proper. A deep, resonant thrum, like the lowest string of a great viol being plucked. It vibrated up through the flagstones, through the stool, into his spine.

Then came the scream.

It was not human. It was the sound of stone under intolerable stress—a high, grinding shriek that tore through the night silence. It was followed by a crash that shook dust from the chapel roof. Not the crash of collapse. The crash of something being rearranged.

Amaury was on his feet, the journal shoved inside his tunic. He blew out the candle and ran into the nave.

The great space was moonlit, the silver light falling through the clerestory windows in cold, geometric shafts. Nothing seemed amiss. Then he looked towards the apse, towards the new chapel.

The scaffolding there, a skeletal forest of poles and platforms, was trembling. As he watched, a massive timber, thicker than a man’s thigh, twisted free of its lashings with a sound of snapping rope. It did not fall. It moved, laterally, through the air, as if shoved by a giant’s hand. It swung and struck the newly finished wall of the chapel with a blow that echoed like a cathedral bell.

The phantom was not screaming. It was working.

It was using the materials at hand—the wood, the stone, the iron—the way Lacroix had used them. But not to build. To dismantle. To express.

Another timber ripped itself loose. Then a basket of mortar flew from a platform, exploding against the pillars in a cloud of grey dust. Stones, carefully shaped for the new arcading, began to tumble from their piles, not randomly, but in a deliberate, terrifying cascade, forming a rough, jagged mound in the centre of the apse floor.

Amaury saw Lacroix then. The mason stood at the edge of the chaos, his figure silhouetted against the moonlit ruin. He was not fleeing. He was staring, his head tilted, his whole body rigid. Not with fear. With recognition.

He took a step towards the tumbling stones, his hand outstretched.

“Stop!” Amaury’s shout was swallowed by the grinding roar.

Lacroix either did not hear or did not care. He walked into the storm of flying debris. A timber swung towards his head. It stopped, mid-arc, quivering in the air an inch from his temple. The hail of stones parted around him like a river around a rock. The phantom knew its maker. It would not harm him. It was showing him.

Lacroix reached the jagged mound. He knelt. He placed his hands on the rough-hewn stones. He bowed his head.

The chaos ceased. The last stone settled. The dangling timber lowered gently to the floor. The silence that followed was absolute, a physical pressure on the ears.

Amaury approached slowly. The mound was not random. It was a rough, brutalist cairn. A tomb. And at its head, placed with terrible care, was a single, small, perfectly shaped ashlar—a keystone, but for a arch that would never be built.

Lacroix was weeping, soundlessly, his shoulders shaking. He looked up at Amaury, his face a mask of dust and agony.

“She knew,” he rasped. “She knew what he would do.”

“And he did it,” Amaury said softly. “The fever was a lie. You carved the truth into the stone. And the cathedral… they… have known it all along. Not as a story. As a taste. A feeling. And now we have given them the words.”

Lacroix stroked the crude cairn. “This is its grief. Its rage. It cannot hurt Guibert. He is dust. So it hurts his work. Its own body.” He looked at Amaury, his eyes gleaming in the dark. “It will bring it down. Stone by stone. It will unravel our lie, and its own existence with it.”

Amaury looked at the savage cairn, at the silent, waiting scaffolding. The phantom had made its next move. It had begun to deconstruct the world that was its tomb. It was not trying to communicate anymore. It was trying to destroy the evidence.

And in the profound, listening silence that now filled the cathedral, Amaury heard a new sound. Not from the stones. From the cloister. A door slamming. Hurried footsteps. Voices, raised in alarm and confusion. The crash had woken the household.

The evidence was no longer just in the stones. It was in Amaury’s tunic, pressed against his heart. Isolde’s journal. The victim’s own testimony. And the Count’s men, the descendants of the man who kept a tally in blood, were now running towards the apse.

The phantom had regurgitated the truth. Now the living would have to swallow it.

Chapter 22

CHAPTER 22

The footsteps were a thunder in the cloister, a clumsy, living counterpoint to the cathedral’s held breath. Amaury’s hand went to his chest, to the stiff parchment and brittle leather sewn into the lining of his tunic. Isolde’s journal was a second heart, a diseased and frantic one, beating against his ribs. The phantom had finished speaking. It had left its confession in a cairn of rubble and a keystone laid like a headstone. Now it was the turn of the living to lie, or to break upon the truth.

Lacroix did not move from the cairn. His tears had cut clean tracks through the stone dust on his face, two rivulets of raw feeling in a landscape of devastation. He looked less like a man and more like a feature of the new, savage architecture—a gargoyle carved for grief.

“They will see this,” Amaury said, his voice low and urgent. “They will ask what we have done.”

“We?” Lacroix’s laugh was a dry crack. “We are auditors of collapse. We witnessed. We recorded.” He laid his palm flat on the rough ashlar headstone. “He is the author. She is the testament. We are merely the poor scribes.”

The voices were at the door to the south transept now. Torchlight, a garish, hungry orange, began to lick at the edges of the great pillars, staining the grey stone the colour of a fresh bruise. The dark of the apse, where they stood, was still profound, a pool of shadow the light had not yet dared to enter. The cairn was a hulking silhouette, a new altar to a different god.

Amaury made a decision. It was not courage. It was a colder, more practical thing, the instinct of a clerk who knows a damning entry must be blotted out before the steward arrives. He stepped away from Lacroix, towards the approaching light, placing his body between the cairn and the door.

The first man through was Brother Porter, his habit hitched up around his knees, his face a moon of panic. Behind him came two of the Count’s household men-at-arms—not the polished knights of the high table, but the workaday brutes who guarded storehouses and collected rents. Their mail was dull, their faces bore the permanent squint of men who expect trouble from shadows. They carried cresset lamps, and the smoke from them joined the lingering dust in the air.

“By all the saints!” Porter’s cry was half prayer, half shriek. His lamp swung wildly, throwing monstrous, leaping shapes up the walls. “The vault! The vault has fallen!”

His light found Amaury first, then swept past him to the mound. He stopped as if struck. The two men-at-arms shouldered past him, their hands going to the hilts of their short swords. They took in the scene: the master mason kneeling by a heap of destruction, the architect’s agent standing guard before it. Their expressions hardened from alarm to a grim, suspicious comprehension. This was not an act of God. God did not build cairns.

One of them, a thick-necked man with a nose that had been broken and poorly set, spoke. His voice was a gravelly ledger of accusations. “You. What mischief is this?”

“No mischief,” Amaury said. He kept his tone flat, administrative. “A structural failure. We were inspecting the piers when it gave way. A section of the vaulting stone collapsed. We are fortunate to be alive.”

The man’s eyes, small and dark as currants, did not believe him. They darted from Amaury’s dust-covered clothes to Lacroix’s devotional posture. “Inspecting. At this hour.”

“The cathedral does not keep a clock,” Amaury replied. “The stones speak when they choose. We listen.”

The other guard, younger, with the fuzz of a boy still on his chin but a cruel set to his mouth, took a step towards the cairn. “What’s that, then? You build a little shrine to your clumsiness?”

Lacroix’s head snapped up. In the shifting light, his dust-masked face was skull-like. “It is a marker. So the next man knows where the weakness is. Where the heart of the rot lies.” His voice was too raw, too full of a meaning that vibrated just beneath the words.

The older guard heard it. His gaze fixed on the keystone, placed with such terrible intentionality. “Move aside, mason.”

Lacroix did not move. He became a stone himself.

Amaury stepped forward, intercepting the guard’s path. “The pile is unstable. Disturb it, and you may bring the rest down upon us. Is that your wish? To explain to your Count why you brought half his apse down on your own heads?”

He was playing the only card he had: their fear of Guibert’s tally. The Count would not care for mysteries, only for results. A collapsed vault was a problem. A collapsed vault caused by his own men’s blundering was a debit against their lives.

The older guard hesitated, his accounting mind weighing the risk. The younger one was less subtle. He shoved past Amaury, his hand reaching for Lacroix’s shoulder. “I said, move, you whoreson—”

He never finished. As his fingers brushed the wool of Lacroix’s tunic, the cairn shifted.

It was not a loud sound. A single stone, about the size of a man’s fist, detached itself from the upper slope and rolled down the pile. It came to rest against the younger guard’s boot. In the absolute silence that followed the cessation of the chaos, the click of it was as sharp as a breaking bone.

Every man froze. Porter whimpered, a high, thin sound.

Then, from deep within the mound, a sigh escaped. It was the sound of settling, yes, but also of release. A long, dusty exhalation that seemed to carry a note of cold satisfaction on its breath.

The younger guard snatched his foot back as if burned. All the colour drained from his face, leaving the cruel mouth loose and stupid. He looked from the stone to Lacroix, whose eyes held a dark, knowing light.

“It is not stable,” Amaury repeated softly, each word a nail in the moment. “The spirit of the place is… agitated.”

He used the word deliberately. Spirit. Not structure, not masonry. In the flickering lamplight, in the wake of that sentient sigh, it was the only word that fit.

The older guard found his voice, though it had lost its gravelly authority and was now strained. “Agitated. Aye.” He backed up a step, pulling his younger companion with him. “Brother Porter. Fetch the sacristan. And the seneschal. Now.”

Porter needed no second bidding. He fled, his sandals slapping a frantic retreat on the flagstones.

The two guards did not sheath their swords, but they made no further move towards the cairn. They stood their ground, a wary cordon, their eyes wide and white in the lamplight. They were used to threats that bled. This was different. This was a ledger written in stone, and the debt was centuries old.

The wait was an exquisite tension. The cathedral, having expressed its fury, was now a silent conspirator. Amaury could feel it listening, judging the quality of their fear. He stood beside Lacroix, not touching him, a united front before the crude tomb. The journal burned against his skin. He understood now why Lacroix had carved his truth into hidden stones. A spoken word could be denied. A written testimony could be burned. But a feeling, a taste of wrongness baked into the very mortar of a place—that was a ghost no fire could exorcise.

The seneschal arrived first, a tall, lean man named Morel who carried the Count’s authority like a thin, sharp blade. He was dressed in a fur-trimmed bed-robe, his hairless scalp gleaming with oil and annoyance. Behind him came the sacristan, old Brother Clement, clutching a silver pectoral cross as if it were a raft.

Morel took in the scene with one sweeping, contemptuous glance. His eyes skipped over the human drama and fixed on the damage. “Explain this,” he said, his voice cutting the air like the tally-knife he wore at his belt.

The older guard stammered out a version: the crash, their arrival, the two men, the unstable pile, the… noise.

Morel’s pale eyes settled on Amaury. “You caused this.”

“We witnessed it,” Amaury corrected. “The fabric failed. The mason marked the site for repair. Your men attempted to interfere, against our warning, and provoked a further settlement.” He gestured to the single stone at the guard’s feet. “You see the result.”

“I see a heap of the Count’s good limestone,” Morel snapped. “And I see two men who have no business here in the dead of night.” He turned his blade of a gaze on Lacroix. “You. Mason. Is this your work?”

Lacroix rose slowly to his feet. He was a head shorter than the seneschal, but he seemed to draw the darkness around him like a mantle. “It is the cathedral’s work,” he said, his voice hollow. “It is correcting a flaw.”

“A flaw.” “A lie,” Lacroix said, and the word hung in the air, simple and devastating.

Morel’s nostrils flared. He took a step forward. “You are raving. The night air and the dust have addled your wits.”

“Have they?” Lacroix’s smile was a terrible thing. “Then why does your Count’s hall smell of cloves and regret? Why does the tally of his line skip a generation? Why does the stone weep where the first Countess lies in her painted box?”

Brother Clement made a sign of the cross. The younger guard took another step back. Morel’s face did not change, but a tiny muscle began to twitch beneath his left eye. Amaury felt a cold thrill. Lacroix was not just speaking truth. He was speaking the cathedral’s truth, the phantom’s curated, sensory indictment. He was making them taste it.

“You speak in riddles,” Morel said, but his voice had lost its edge. It was the tone of a man who has heard a rumour he dared not believe confirmed in the public square.

“The only riddle is why you still stand in this building,” Lacroix pressed, a mad, holy fervour in his eyes. “It does not want you. It wants to be rid of you. It is unmaking itself to be rid of the memory of you. Can you not feel it? It is not a house of God. It is a gut full of shame.”

“Enough!” Morel’s shout was a crack of control. He pointed a long, bony finger at Lacroix. “You are under arrest. For destruction of the Count’s property. For sorcerous talk. Take him.”

The guards hesitated. They looked at the cairn, at the single, accusing stone.

“Take him!” Morel screamed, spittle flying.

They moved, reluctantly, grabbing Lacroix by his arms. He did not resist. He let them manhandle him, his eyes never leaving Morel’s face. “Arrest the stone, then,” he murmured. “Arrest the memory. See how well your cells hold them.”

As they dragged him away, Lacroix’s gaze found Amaury’s. It was not a plea. It was a transfer of custody. The journal. The truth. It is yours now.

Morel turned to Amaury. “And you. Architect’s man. You will come to the hall and give a full account. A material account. Of stresses and loads. Leave poetry and madness to the condemned.”

Amaury bowed his head, the picture of contrite reason. “Of course, my lord seneschal.”

He followed Morel out of the apse, leaving Brother Clement alone before the cairn, praying in a frantic whisper to a God who, in this place, seemed to have turned His face away. The old monk’s words were a feeble counterpoint to the profound, listening silence that remained, a silence that now felt like a held breath before a scream.

The high hall was a different world. The rushes were fresh, the hearth embers glowed a sullen red, and the lingering scent of the evening’s meal—grease, garlic, stale wine—lay over everything like a shroud. It was the smell of the living, of mundane power. It felt obscenely normal.

Count Guibert was not present. The hour was too late, or the matter too vulgar, for The Tally. Morel presided from the dais, in the Count’s high-backed chair. A scribe sat at a small table to his side, a quill poised over parchment. The performance was to be recorded.

Amaury stood before the dais. The two guards flanked him. Lacroix had been taken below, to the cells beneath the keep where the air was wet and the walls wept constant, non-supernatural tears.

“Your name,” Morel began, the formal inquiry a weapon.

“Amaury of Clermont, agent of Master Robert de Luzarches, architect of the cathedral.”

“Your business here tonight.”

“To accompany the master mason, Julien Lacroix, in a final survey of the apse piers. There were… concerns about settlement. We wished to observe the structure in its quietest state, free from the vibrations of the workday.”

“And these concerns were justified, it seems.”

“Tragically, yes. The central pier on the southern arcade showed a catastrophic weakness. The core must be flawed. It gave way as we observed it.”

Morel steepled his fingers. “A flaw. A lie. A memory. The mason used many words. Which is it?”

Amaury met his gaze. “To a mason, my lord, a flaw in the stone is a lie. It promises strength but contains failure. The memory is in the cathedral’s ledger. This pier has borne a hidden strain for generations. It chose tonight to remember it.”

He was walking a knife-edge, using Lacroix’s language but sanding off its holy madness, turning it into a metaphor a practical man might dismiss as artisan’s fancy.

Morel was not a practical man. He was a courtier. He understood metaphor as a vehicle for poison. “A curious philosophy. And this… cairn. This marker. Why build it? Why not simply call for help?”

“In the moment, with dust choking the air and stones still falling, it was an act of propitiation. A mason’s instinct. To acknowledge the failure, to contain it symbolically, lest it spread.” Amaury paused. “Your men’s intervention nearly caused a second collapse. The mound is a warning as much as a marker.”

The older guard shifted his weight but said nothing.

The scribe’s quill scratched, translating chaos into neat, black lines.

“And the mason’s ravings?” Morel’s voice was silk over ice. “The late Countess? The tally of the line? Cloves and regret?”

Amaury allowed a look of professional pity to cross his face. “Julien Lacroix is a genius with stone. He feels its pains as his own. The shock of the collapse, the dust… it has unhinged him. He speaks of the cathedral as if it were a person. A common enough affliction among those who spend their lives in conversation with inert matter. They forget it is inert.”

“Do you believe it? That the stone is… agitated?”

The question hung in the smoky air. It was the trap. To agree was to share in the madness. To dismiss it too lightly was to ignore the evidence of the sigh, the sentient stone.

“I believe,” Amaury said carefully, “that a building of this scale has a character. It can feel heavy, or light, or troubled. It is a reflection of the minds that built it and the faith that fills it. Tonight, it feels wounded. Lacroix, in his distress, gives that wound a voice. A history.”

Morel studied him for a long moment. The twitch under his eye had returned. “A pretty answer. Empty, but pretty.” He leaned forward. “Where is the mason’s tool satchel? His drawings?”

“Lost,” Amaury said without hesitation. “Buried in the rubble.”

“And you have nothing on your person? No notes? No… testimonies?”

Amaury felt the journal, a stiff, accusing rectangle against his heart. He spread his hands, showing them empty. “I am an observer, my lord. My testimony is here.” He tapped his temple. “And it will be in my report to Master Robert.”

The threat was implicit. Robert de Luzarches was a name with weight, a man who corresponded with bishops and kings. His agent was not a common workman to be bullied.

Morel understood. He leaned back, the carved griffins on the chair’s back seeming to frame his skull. “Your report will wait. You will remain in the guest quarters. You will not approach the apse. You will speak to no one of this. The matter is under the Count’s investigation.”

It was house arrest, politely phrased.

“And the mason?” Amaury asked.

“The mason is a vandal and a raver. He will be tried for the destruction. His fate is the Count’s to decide.” Morel’s smile was thin and lifeless. “Perhaps the stone will speak in his defence.”

The audience was over. A guard was assigned to escort Amaury back to the guest tower. As he turned to leave, his eyes swept the hall. They passed over the high table, the Count’s empty chair, the space where a silver chalice might once have been nudged. They caught on the dark mouth of the staircase that led to the solar. The staircase that had, once, carried a chair down to the hall, bearing a guest of honour in an azure gown.

The smell was gone, scoured away by time and rushes. But the memory of it, the idea of it, was now a living thing inside him. It was no longer a story. It was a taste.

The guest chamber was a cell of comfort. A narrow bed, a washstand, a single slit window overlooking the inner ward. The guard took up position outside the door. The lock clicked, a sound of finality.

Amaury stood in the centre of the room, the silence pounding in his ears. He was a container for a secret too large to hold. He went to the window. The moon had set. The ward was a pit of blackness, but in the distance, the bulk of the cathedral was a deeper cut against the star-flecked sky. No light showed from within. It was a sleeping beast, digesting its meal of truth and rubble.

He needed to read it. Now. While the phantom’s rage was still a fresh echo in his bones, while Lacroix’s translation of its language was still clear.

Moving to the bed, he sat with his back to the door. With fingers that trembled only slightly, he unlaced the neck of his tunic. He reached inside, past the sweat-damp linen of his shirt, and found the hard, irregular shape. He had sewn the journal into the lining himself, using a sailmaker’s stitch, tight and small. Now he took his eating knife from his belt and carefully slit the threads.

The journal emerged, wrapped in a scrap of oiled cloth. It was small, the leather cover stained and supple from years of handling. He unfolded it with the reverence of a priest unveiling a relic.

The script was a woman’s, elegant and firm, but with a hurried, desperate energy that made the letters dance and spike. The Latin was fluent, interspersed with bursts of the vernacular, as if some feelings could only be borne in the tongue of her cradle.

The early entries were mundane. Records of household management, notes on fabrics for vestments, a recipe for a salve against winter chapping. Then, subtle shifts. A mention of Guibert’s absence at the Easter feast. A note that the chaplain had preached on the virtue of a wife’s silence.

He turned the pages, the quiet snick of parchment loud in the room. He found the heart of it midway through.

…he calls it his tally. A game, he says. A way to measure the health of his demesne. He has a book, bound in boar’s hide, and in it he writes not the yields of wheat or the head of swine, but of lives. A mark for a child born in a tenant’s hut that will one day work his fields. A mark struck through for a man who died in a ditch, his labour lost. A mark for a marriage he arranged, for the children it will produce. He tots them up at year’s end, and he smiles if the black ink outweighs the red. He says it is the only true scripture: the book of gain and loss. I said it was a sin, to reduce God’s children to figures. He laughed. He said, “My dear, you are the finest figure in the ledger. A credit of alliance, of beauty, of potential issue.” He touched my cheek. His fingers were cold. I felt like a prize heifer.

Amaury’s breath caught. Here was the genesis of The Tally, not as a nickname given by tax collectors, but as a soul-deep pathology. The Countess saw it. She tasted its blasphemy.

The entries grew darker, more confined. She wrote of the walls of the castle, which began to feel not like protection, but like the pages of that boar-hide book, pressing in on her.

…the fever took little Mathieu today. The priest came. Guibert was in his counting-room. I heard the scratch of his quill through the door. After, I went to him. I was raw with grief. I said, “Your son is dead.” He did not look up from his book. He said, “A debit. A substantial one. We must redouble our efforts.” He meant me. He looked at my belly as if assessing a field for planting. I vomited right there on his floor. He rang for a servant to clean it, and made a note in his margin.

Nausea, real and present, rose in Amaury’s throat. This was not just cruelty. It was a fundamental disassembly of love, of kinship, into columned figures. The phantom’s rage was not merely at murder. It was at this—this unmaking of the human heart into arithmetic.

He turned the page. The writing became frantic, the letters sprawling.

He knows I have seen his true book. Not the one of rents, but the other. The one he keeps in the iron chest. I found the key. I looked. Saints forgive me, I looked. It is not lives. It is… transactions. Favours bought with pain. Secrets kept with silver or threat. Alliances sealed with the blood of others. My father’s name is there. The price he paid for my marriage was not in land. It was in silence. Over a death. A man who spoke too much of a mill burned, a family inside. I am a figure in that column, too. A cost incurred. I live in a ledger of sin. The castle is not built on rock. It is built on these entries, these black numbers. They are the mortar. I can smell it. It smells of cloves and cheap incense, the smell of the embalmer he keeps on retainer, for the accidents of his accounting…

Amaury had to stop. He closed his eyes. The connection was complete. The phantom’s sensory assault—the cloying smell of preservation, the taste of regret—was not the memory of her death. It was the memory of her understanding. The moment she comprehended the full, grotesque architecture of her world. Her marriage, her home, her very life, were entries in a ledger of moral ruin.

The final entry was a single paragraph, written in a hand so stressed it was barely legible.

The fever is a lie. He told me himself. He stood at the foot of my bed and said it was a necessary correction. An imbalance in the household accounts. I have born no living heir. I have become a whisperer against his work. I am a persistent debit. He has ordered a new, profitable alliance. To clear the ledger, the old entry must be voided. He said it is nothing personal. It is only good management. The physician will come. The draught will taste of honey and bitter almond. He will make the mark in his book. And the world will be balanced. For him. I am to be a red line. A settled account. God, let there be no balance. Let there be a riot of loss. Let the sums never add up. Let it all… unravel.

There was no more.

Amaury sat in the cold dawn light seeping through the slit window, the journal heavy in his hands. He understood everything now. The phantom was not Isolde’s ghost. It was her curse. Her final, furious prayer against the tyranny of balance. Let it all unravel.

And the cathedral, Guibert’s pride, the stone embodiment of his legacy and his piety, was the perfect vehicle. It was his ultimate entry in the ledger—a massive credit to his name, his soul. To make it unravel itself was the most exquisite vengeance. Not to destroy it from without, but to expose the hidden flaw, the lie in its heart, and let its own weight do the work.

Lacroix was right. It was not trying to communicate. It was trying to destroy the evidence. But the evidence was not just in the stones. It was here, in this book. The victim’s testimony.

A soft sound made him look up. It was not from the door. It was from the window.

A single, fat drop of moisture had gathered on the inner stone sill. It trembled, reflecting the grey light, and then fell, leaving a dark star on the dry wood below.

Amaury looked at it. He looked at the journal.

The phantom had given him the words. The living were now arrayed to silence them. Lacroix was in a cell, condemned. He was under guard, discredited. The journal was a secret that could be burned.

But the stone was still weeping.

The hook was not in the door, or in the threat of the trial. It was in that single, silent drop. The unraveling had not stopped with the cairn. The cathedral was just beginning to settle its account. And Amaury, holding the ledger of its grievance, was now the only one who could read the final sum.

He had to get out. He had to get to Lacroix. And he had to decide, before the first true light of day, whether to bury the truth forever, or to place it, like a keystone, into the arch of the coming storm.

Chapter 23

CHAPTER 23

The drop was a period. A full stop, in the middle of a sentence that had not yet been spoken.

Amaury stared at the dark star it had left on the wood of the sill. It was not water. It was colder, thicker, a distillation of the damp that lived in the stone’s marrow. The cathedral’s sweat. Its slow, weeping blood. He looked from it to the journal in his hands, its leather cover cool and supplicant against his palms. The Countess’s final prayer was a dry rustle between his fingers, a sound like old leaves in a crypt. Let it all unravel.

He understood the mechanism now, the terrible, elegant justice of it. Guibert had built his monument on a ledger of loss, a column of subtractions that began with a wife’s stifled soul. The phantom was not haunting the place. It was auditing it. It was the final reckoning, the sum that would not balance, made flesh in mortar and stone. To silence Lacroix, to burn this book, would be to grant the Count a stay of execution. It would be to agree that some debts could be written off, some victims left uncounted in the grand, cold total of history.

Amaury had spent a lifetime in the shadow of totals. The number of prayers said, the weight of sins confessed, the measure of grace dispensed. He had believed in the balance. Now, holding Isolde’s testament, he saw that true balance was a violence. It demanded a corpse for a crown, a silenced woman for a soaring vault. The phantom offered a different arithmetic: a riot of loss. An unravelling.

He moved. The decision was not a thought, but a turning of the tide within him, cold and irreversible. He tucked the journal inside his robe, against his chest, where it lay like a second, frantic heart. The guard outside his door was a lumpish silhouette against the torchlight in the corridor, a man chosen for bulk over wit. Amaury did not try the door. He went to the window.

The slit was narrow, a stone mouth grimacing at the dawn. The outer wall fell away, a sheer plunge to the cobbles of the inner ward forty feet below. But to the left, a scant arm’s length away, the corner of the keep met the slightly lower roof of the garrison hall. It was a gap a cat might think twice about. The roof was steep, slick with night damp and moss.

Amaury did not calculate the distance. He did not pray. He thought of Lacroix in his cell, of the man’s fierce, inconvenient love for truth—a love that was, in its way, as absolute and doomed as Isolde’s for her music. He thought of the stone, weeping in the dark. He pushed his head and shoulders through the slit, the rough stone scraping his back, and reached.

The cold morning air bit at his fingers. His shoulder screamed a protest lodged deep in the bone. For a suspended moment, he was nowhere, a man stretched between a cell and a fall. Then his grasping hand found the gutter of the garrison roof, a lip of lead, and he hauled, a desperate, graceless contortion that left him sprawled on the sloping slate, breathless, the journal digging into his ribs.

He lay still, listening. Below, the castle was a beast stirring in its sleep: the clank of a pail from the well, the murmur of the kitchen yard. No shout of alarm. He was a grey smudge on a grey roof, of no interest to the waking world.

The route to the lower bailey was a slow, treacherous pilgrimage across rooftops and down crumbling exterior stairs, through the reeking, narrow alley between the tannery and the brewery. He moved like the phantom he pursued—a shadow against the stone, a whisper of cloth, a shape that did not belong. The sky was the colour of a bruise, yellow and purple at its edges, bleeding into a sickly grey. It was the light that falls before a verdict is read.

The old gatehouse that served as the prison was a tooth of black flint sunk in the softer gum of the outer wall. Its door was oak, banded with iron, but its guard was a boy, no more than sixteen, huddled on a stool with a woollen cloak pulled tight around his ears. He was not guarding against escape from within; he was keeping the morning chill from the prisoner. Amaury walked up to him as if he had every right.

“The Bishop’s business,” he said, his voice the dry rasp of authority. “I am to hear the mason’s final confession before the trial.”

The boy blinked, his face pale and pinched. “The Captain said no one—”

“The Captain answers to the Count. The Count answers to God. I answer to the Bishop. Open the door.”

The weight of this chain of command, a theology of power the boy understood in his marrow, made him fumble with the keys. The lock groaned. The door swung inward on a darkness that smelled of stale straw and cold piss.

Lacroix was in the lowest cell, a space not meant for long occupancy. It was a hole where men were stored prior to hanging. He was not sitting on the straw. He was standing at the back wall, his fingers tracing the mortar between the stones, his head cocked as if listening to a faint, distant melody. He did not turn as Amaury entered.

“They are singing Prime in the chapel,” Lacroix said, his voice quiet. “Do you hear it? All those flat, dutiful voices. They sing to keep the silence out. They have no idea the silence is the only thing listening.”

Amaury barred the door from the inside. The boy would not dare interrupt a confession. “I have it.”

Now Lacroix turned. In the thin light from the grille above the door, he looked like one of his own gargoyles, his face all hollows and sharp edges, his eyes two chips of flint. “Have what?”

“The reason. The why.” Amaury drew the journal from his robe. It seemed to suck the meagre light from the air. “It was the Countess. Isolde. She is the flaw in the foundation.”

He spoke in a low, urgent rush. The feast, the unspoken competition with the roast pig. The ledger. The music box. The final, furious prayer. He watched as Lacroix’s professional curiosity—the mason assessing a structural stress—gave way to a darker, more personal understanding. The man’s shoulders slumped, not in defeat, but in a kind of awful reverence.

“A curse,” Lacroix murmured. “Not a spell. A wish. The last, desperate entry in her own ledger.” He looked up at Amaury. “You see it, don’t you? The perfection. He buried her joy in a box. So her vengeance buries his pride in stone. It is the same principle. Balance.”

“It is not balance,” Amaury said, the words sharp as a splinter. “It is justice. The kind heaven is too polite to administer.”

Lacroix’s flinty eyes gleamed. “And you have brought me her testimony. Why? To comfort me before the noose? To show me the beauty of the mechanism that has killed my men and will kill me?”

“To finish the audit,” Amaury said.

He laid it out, the plan forming as he spoke, cold and clear as the drop on the sill. The trial was a foregone conclusion. Lacroix would be found guilty of malice, of heresy, of undermining the Count’s divine work. He would hang, and the journal would burn, and the cathedral’s slow suicide would continue, unseen, until one day a keystone sighed and a million tons of sanctimony followed it into the dust. Guibert would get his martyr, but not the one he intended. The phantom would win, but its victory would be mute, anonymous, a footnote in a chronicle of collapse.

“Or,” Amaury said, leaning close, “we give the phantom a voice.”

Lacroix was still. “The trial.”

“You are a mason. You speak of forces, of loads, of tensions. So speak. Tell them what you hear in the stone. Not heresy. Observation. And I…” Amaury touched the journal. “I will provide the testimony. The motive. We will not accuse the Count of murder. We will accuse his cathedral of bearing witness. We will read the victim’s statement into the record of the court. We will make the silence speak.”

“They will never let you.”

“They will have to. The Bishop’s confessor, presenting evidence of a spiritual malaise affecting the foundation? They will hear it. They may damn it, but they will hear it. The truth will be a seed in the ear of every lord and cleric present. It will be a crack. Not in the stone. In the story.”

Lacroix studied him for a long time. The distant singing had stopped. The silence in the cell was profound.

“You would do this?” Lacroix asked finally. “You would burn your world to the ground for a principle you learned from a ghost?”

Amaury thought of the ledger of his own life. The neat columns of obedience, of silence, of accepted totals. He thought of the cold, monastic love he had borne for a God of balance, and the warmer, more terrible love he now felt for a truth that unbalanced everything. It was a love that accepted mortality—not just of the body, but of power, of legacy, of stone itself.

“It is already burning,” Amaury said. “I am merely choosing to stand by the light.”

A fist hammered on the door. “Father! The Captain comes!”

Lacroix gave a short, sharp nod. It was not an agreement between friends. It was a pact between two condemned men, a merging of their separate sentences. “Then we give them a trial to remember.”

The great hall had been transformed from a place of feasting to a theatre of law. The high table was now a judicial bench, draped in grim scarlet. Count Guibert sat at its centre, a ledger open before him—a different one, of course, but the principle was the same. To his right sat the Bishop’s representative, a canon with a face like a dried apple, and to his left, the seneschal, whose duty was to look grave and impartial. The air was thick with the smell of rushes, damp wool, and a keen, bloodless anticipation.

The hall was packed. Every knight, every burgher of note, every minor cleric had squeezed onto the benches. A hanging was one thing. The ritual destruction of the man who had dared fault God’s house—that was a spectacle. Amaury stood at the edge of the crowd, his hood drawn up, the journal a leaden weight against his chest. He felt the phantom’s presence here, not as a chill, but as a pressure, a listening attention. The stones of this hall were older, quieter. They were waiting.

Lacroix was brought in. They had not beaten him, but they had stripped him of his mason’s leathers, dressing him in a rough, grey sackcloth tunic that made him look like a penitent, or a corpse already in its shroud. His hands were bound before him. He walked with his head high, his eyes scanning the vaults of the hall as if checking the joints.

The charges were read: Malicious Sorcery. Corruption of Sacred Works. Heretical Design. The words were sonorous, empty vessels waiting to be filled with the crowd’s fear. The Count’s advocate, a man with a voice like a saw cutting wet wood, laid out the case. The collapses. The strange sounds. The mason’s own suspicious words. He painted Lacroix as a man jealous of the Count’s piety, a servant of dark forces who had woven weakness into the very fabric of the holy edifice.

Through it all, Guibert’s face was a mask of pained righteousness. He made notes in his ledger. The Tally, balancing the accounts of men’s souls.

When it was Lacroix’s turn to speak, a hush fell. They expected a plea, a grovel, perhaps a wild confession wrung from a guilty conscience.

Lacroix cleared his throat. “My lords,” he began, his voice not loud, but carrying the flat, clear tone of a man calling measurements across a yard. “You speak of corruption. Of a flaw. You are correct. There is a fatal flaw in the cathedral.”

A murmur rippled through the hall. Guibert’s pen paused.

“But it is not one of my making,” Lacroix continued. “I am a mason. I understand weight and counter-weight, thrust and repose. A stone bears down. An arch channels that weight out and down to its piers. It is a conversation of forces. In your cathedral, the conversation has stopped. The weight is no longer flowing. It is pooling. It is gathering in the central crossing, below the new tower.”

“Sorcerous nonsense!” the advocate barked.

“It is masonry,” Lacroix shot back, a flicker of his old fire igniting. “Send any master from Paris, from Chartres, to measure the settle of the piers. They will tell you. The foundation is sound, the stone is good. But the load… the load is wrong. It is as if a new, terrible weight has been added to the structure. A weight no blueprint accounted for.”

Guibert spoke, his whey-like eyes fixed on Lacroix. “And what, mason, is the nature of this phantom weight? What does your… expertise… tell you it is?”

Lacroix met his gaze. “Grief, my lord.”

The word dropped into the hall like a stone into a well. There was a baffled silence.

“Grief,” Lacroix repeated. “A specific, concentrated, and furious grief. It has mass. It has intention. It settles in the heart of a building raised not to the glory of God, but to the glory of a man’s ledger. It is the weight of a soul that was entered on the wrong side of the page.”

The Canon leaned forward. “You speak in blasphemous metaphors, mason. Souls have no weight.”

“Do they not?” Lacroix’s voice dropped, becoming almost conversational. “Then why do we feel crushed by guilt? Why does shame bow the shoulders? Why does a great sorrow feel like a millstone on the chest? We are creatures of earth and spirit, my lord canon. The two are not so easily parted. And sometimes, a spirit with a grievance forgets to let go of the earth. It becomes a tenant. A dissatisfied one.”

Guibert’s face had gone very still. Amaury saw it—a flicker in the pale eyes, not of fear, but of recognition. The Count knew the language of accounts. He understood the concept of a debt that assumed physical form.

The advocate recovered first. “This is precisely the heresy we speak of! Animating stone with pagan spirits! You condemn yourself with your own words!”

“I describe a symptom,” Lacroix said, his voice rising now, ringing off the timber roof. “The cause is not my spell. The cause is the original sin of the foundation. You built on a wrong. And the wrong is remembering itself.”

It was the opening. Amaury stepped forward, pushing back his hood.

“He speaks the truth.”

All eyes turned to him. The Canon squinted. “Father Amaury? You are meant to be in your chambers.”

“I was,” Amaury said, walking to the open space before the high table. He felt the gaze of the crowd, a physical pressure. He felt Guibert’s stare, cold and sharp as an icicle. “I was contemplating the nature of testimony. And of evidence.” He drew the journal from his robe. The azure silk binding was dull in the hall’s light, but it was unmistakably a woman’s book. A noblewoman’s.

A stillness, different from the quiet before, seized the room. This was a new, unknown quantity.
