“What is that?” Guibert asked. His voice was soft, deadly.

“A spiritual ledger, my lord,” Amaury said. “Kept by the late Countess Isolde. It was found sealed in a cavity within the music gallery of the cathedral. The cavity was meant to hold a relic. It held this instead.”

The Canon looked alarmed. “A private devotional text? This is not—”

“It is the relic,” Amaury interrupted, his own voice gaining a strange, resonant certainty. “It is the heart of the matter. The mason speaks of a weight of grief. This book is its source. It is the last testament of a soul whose music was silenced, whose joy was itemized and found wanting against the grand total of a legacy. She prayed, in its final pages, not for salvation, but for justice. For the unravelling of the cold arithmetic that condemned her to silence.”

Guibert stood up. The legs of his chair shrieked on the flagstones. “You will not read from that.”

“I am the Bishop’s confessor,” Amaury said, meeting his eyes. “It is my duty to discern spiritual maladies. This book reveals a profound sickness at the inception of this holy project. A sickness that has, I believe, manifested in the physical distress of the structure. The phantom is not a demon. It is a symptom. To hang the mason is to treat a fever by breaking the thermometer. The disease remains.”

“This is an outrage!” the advocate spluttered. “My lord, this is a defamation of your holy wife’s memory! A manipulation by a condemned heretic and a renegade priest!”

But the seed was planted. Amaury saw it in the faces of the lesser lords, the burghers. They understood property. They understood inheritance. They understood the bitterness of wives. A holy curse was one thing—a frightening, distant concept. A noblewoman’s bitter diary causing walls to crack was something else. It was a story. A delicious, terrible story.

Guibert saw it too. His jaw worked. He was a man who controlled through ledgers, through controlled increments of force and favour. This was a riot. An unravelling. He could have Amaury seized, the book snatched and burned. But the words were already out. The idea was in the air, mixing with the rushes and the smoke. To destroy the book now would be to confirm its power.

“The book,” Guibert said, each word a chip of ice, “will be examined by the Canon. Privately. For its theological soundness. The mason’s heresy is plain for all to hear. He has confessed to believing stone can be haunted by human sentiment. That is a rejection of God’s order. The sentence is clear.”

He was trying to reassert the narrative. Crime. Heresy. Sentence. A clean line.

Lacroix laughed. It was a short, harsh sound, like stone splitting. “Examine it? You mean burn it. Just as you burned her music. You are a consistent man, my lord. But you cannot burn the weight. It is already in the stones. It is listening to you now, trying to make the sum come right. You should let Amaury read the last page. The prayer. It is for you.”

Every eye swung back to Guibert. The challenge was direct, intimate. A duel fought with a dead woman’s words.

For a long moment, no one moved. The Count’s pale eyes were locked on the journal in Amaury’s hands. Amaury felt the phantom’s attention focus to a point, a cold, silent spotlight on this tableau. This was the moment the curse had been waiting for—not the collapse, but the confrontation. The bringing of the hidden ledger into the light.

Guibert opened his mouth to pronounce the sentence, to end it.

And the castle shook.

It was not a sound first. It was a deep, subsonic groan that rose through the soles of the feet, up the bones of the legs, into the belly. The flagstones trembled. Dust sifted in fine veils from the joints of the great roof timbers. A cup on the high table toppled, its ringing clatter absurdly loud in the sudden, petrified silence.

Then the sound came: a deep, grinding crack, muffled by distance but immense in its implication, rolling across the bailey from the direction of the town. It was the sound of something massive giving way.

All decorum shattered. Men surged to their feet, rushing to the high windows that overlooked the town. Amaury did not move. He looked at Lacroix. The mason’s eyes were closed, his head bowed as if in prayer, or in receipt of a terrible message.

Guibert reached the window first. He stared out, his back rigid. Amaury saw the rigidity seep from his shoulders, replaced by a slow, dreadful slump. The Count did not cry out. He simply placed one hand against the stone mullion, as if to steady himself.

Amaury walked to a different window. He looked out.

The new tower of the cathedral was gone.

Not collapsed. Not fallen in a heap. It had… unravelled. The elegant, soaring finger of stone had disassembled itself. It was a cascade of rubble, a sloping hill of dust and broken masonry sheathing the jagged stump of the crossing. But the collapse was oddly contained. The choir, the nave, the transepts—they still stood, as if the tower had carefully excised itself from the body of the church. A calculated subtraction. Dust billowed in a great, slow-moving cloud, swallowing the nearby houses, but through it, Amaury could see the hole where the tower had been, a raw wound open to the bruise-coloured sky.

The phantom had not just settled its account. It had published the final sum for all to see.

In the hall, chaos bubbled—shouts, prayers, the clatter of arms as guards were called. But at the centre of it, three men stood in a pocket of stillness. Guibert, his face ashen, his hand still on the window, his life’s work reduced to a column of dust. Lacroix, his eyes now open, fixed on the Count with a look of pure, unanswerable pity. And Amaury, the journal held tight, the words within now echoing in the thunder of falling stone.

The hook was not in the escape, or the trial, or even the collapse. It was in the silence that followed the roar. It was in the way Guibert finally turned from the window, his whey-like eyes finding Amaury’s. There was no fury there now. No calculation. Only a vast, hollow confusion. The ledger had been struck by a force that did not understand columns. It only understood erasure.

The Count looked at the book. He looked at the mason. He looked at the priest.

And he said, very quietly, a question that was also a surrender: “What does it want now?”

Amaury knew. The unravelling was not complete. The tower was just the interest paid. The principal remained. The journal was the demand note. And they were all—Count, mason, priest—co-signatories on the debt.

Outside, the first screams from the town began to filter through the dust, a chorus for the new, silent world.

Chapter 24

CHAPTER 24

The dust was beginning to compete with the incense.

This was the central, unspoken tension in the shattered nave. Father Lacroix, a spindle of a man whose face resembled parchment stretched over a vow, held the smouldering thurible aloft, its chain trembling in his grip. As he did, the grey plume from its bowl met the great, settling cloud of powdered stone, the two substances engaging in a slow, liturgical dance. The priest did not flinch at the grit that coated the wine in the chalice on the makeshift altar. His gaze, dark and depthless as a well, remained fixed on the fourth point of their compass, lying on a trestle table they had dragged from the hall.

The living Count Guibert.

He was, by any objective measure, having a worse morning than anyone else in the ruin. Three hours since the tower’s fall, he had been installed in a chair of state, then removed from it when he could not stop his hands from plucking at the air as if tallying invisible columns. They had laid him upon the table like a transaction awaiting final notation. He was dressed in his finest fur-trimbed robe, his hands now folded over his chest, a cloak artfully arranged to conceal the worst of the trembling. The physician, a harried man from the town who smelled of garlic and dread, had done his best. But a deep, subterranean chill had settled into the bones of the church, a sharp, mineral silence that carried the scent of wet plaster, old fear, and now, beneath the frankincense, the sweet, coppery hint of mortality not yet realised.

Amaury watched the Count from the shadow of a surviving transept arch. He held the mason’s journal not like a book, but like a weight—the counterbalance to the tonnage of absence where the tower had been. The screams from the town had subsided into a low, animal keening, the sound of a community whose arithmetic had been permanently scrambled. The phantom had not just published the final sum. It had burned the ledger and salted the earth where the counting-house stood.

Lacroix completed his circuit, the clink-clink of the thurible chain the only metronome in the cavernous quiet. He stopped before the table. “He does not see us,” the priest said, his voice stripped of its usual melodic certainty, leaving only the raw wood of statement.

“He sees the numbers,” Amaury said, his own voice gravelly with dust. “They just don’t add to anything anymore.”

Guibert’s whey-like eyes were open, fixed on the vaults where the tower’s base had once punched through. His lips moved, soundless. Interest. Principal. Mortmain. Tithe.

“What does it want now?” Amaury murmured, repeating the Count’s surrender. He looked down at the journal. The answer was in the final, frantic sketches, in the margins of the last page. Not a design for a tower, but a diagram for its dissolution. A careful, loving illustration of collapse. The mason had not gone mad. He had achieved a terrible, final clarity. The phantom did not want a monument. It wanted a confession. And the confession was written in falling stone.

“It wants the story,” Lacroix said, as if reading the thought on the air. He set the thurible down. Its metal bowl hissed against a damp flagstone. “It wants the true name of the debt. Not the sum. The source.”

The source. Amaury’s thumb found the page. The sketch of the tower’s foundation, and beneath it, the rough, angry cross-hatching the mason had drawn over and over, like a man trying to black out a memory. But in one corner, a detail he had missed before: a tiny, meticulous drawing of a box. A reliquary. Not saintly. The proportions were all wrong. Too long. Too narrow.

A cold that had nothing to do with the church’s air seeped into Amaury’s marrow. He remembered the ossuary in the crypt, the neat stacks of forgotten citizens. He remembered the Countess Isolde, propped at the high table, her psalter closed over hands that would never turn another page. A public display. A private theatre of possession.

“The foundation isn’t just stone,” he said, the words leaving him like a breath held too long. “He built his peace on a specific sin. Not a general greed. A particular love. A particular mortality.”

Lacroix went very still. His eyes found Guibert’s twitching form. “The Countess. Her fever was… convenient. The town murmured. But the Tally’s books showed a generous donation to the abbey for masses for her soul. The ledger balanced.”

“But love doesn’t enter the ledger,” Amaury said. “And neither does theft.”

The hook was not in the collapse, or the phantom’s rage, or the Count’s unraveling mind. It was in the quiet, dreadful symmetry. Chapter One: a corpse at a feast, a debt of honour unspoken. Chapter Twenty-Four: a living corpse in a ruin, a debt of love unpaid. The spiral had completed its turn, but it had descended into a darker cellar.

“The mason knew,” Amaury continued, the journal feeling suddenly hot in his hands. “He must have been hired for the tower, yes. But he discovered something during the digging. Something Guibert had buried in the foundation trench. Not for stability. For silence.”

Lacroix crossed himself, a swift, jerky motion. “A blasphemy.”

“A signature.” Amaury looked towards the ragged hole in the ceiling. The sky was the colour of a fresh bruise, bleeding into dawn. “The phantom isn’t the mason. The phantom is the witness. The one interred without a name, without a psalm, without a stone. The one whose love was turned into a column’s foot. The mason saw. He drew it. And then he was… incorporated. His journal is the witness’s testimony, written in a language of lines and stress.”

The priest’s face crumpled, not in fear, but in a profound, weary recognition. “De profundis clamavi. Out of the depths, I have cried. The cry is not from the soul. It is from the soil.”

Amaury moved then, walking past the trembling Count, past the priest, towards the crossing, where the great mound of rubble sloped up into the light. He began to climb. The stones shifted and groaned beneath his boots, a language of settling grief. Lacroix, after a moment, followed, his robes gathering dust like a shroud.

They climbed not to escape, but to descend. The tower’s fall had created a new geography. At its peak, where the spire had kissed the sky, was now a treacherous slope leading down into the crypt of the crossing itself—a space that should have been dark, sealed, holy. Daylight, raw and accusatory, fell into it for the first time in a century.

At the edge of the light, Amaury stopped.

There, in the centre of the violated crypt, the rubble had fallen in a perfect, ironic circle, as if the collapsing stone had bowed in reverence. And in the centre of that circle, the earth had been churned up, not by the fall, but by something pushing from below.

A long, narrow box of rotted wood, split open like a seedpod.

It was empty.

But beside it, placed with a care that was more terrifying than any violence, was a small, water-shaped stone. A river rock. The kind a child might pocket for its smoothness. The kind a lover might give as a token, for it cost nothing, and therefore meant everything.

Lacroix sank to his knees, not in prayer, but in defeat. “He took her,” the priest whispered. “Not just her life. Her rest. He laid the first stone of his glory upon her… her quietus. A dowry of silence.”

The phantom’s demand was now clear. It did not want the tower rebuilt. It did not want Guibert’s death. Those were transactions, and it was done with ledgers.

It wanted the story told. It wanted the true name spoken in the light. It wanted the love, however forbidden, however tragic, however mortal, to be entered into the only record that mattered: the memory of the living. The principal of the debt was not gold, or stone, or even a life. It was the truth.

Amaury looked from the empty box to the river stone to the journal in his hand. The final sketch. The long, narrow box, cross-hatched into oblivion. The mason had tried to hide it, even from himself. But he had drawn it. He had borne witness.

They climbed down in silence. The Count’s mumbling had stopped. He was sitting up on the trestle table, his head cocked as if listening to a distant song. His eyes, when they found Amaury’s, were clear. Cleared by the final, absolute subtraction.

“You found her,” Guibert said. It was not a question.

“She was never lost,” Amaury replied. “You knew exactly where she was.”

A smile, thin and terrible as a crack in ice, touched the Count’s lips. “She was the only thing that ever added to nothing. The only sum that made no sense. A deficit of the heart. I could not let it stand. I built the tower to… to correct the error. To make her a part of something grand. To give her meaning.”

“You gave her a tombstone a hundred feet tall,” Lacroix said, his voice hollow. “And you called it piety.”

“What does it want now?” Guibert asked again, but the tone was different. He was asking for the final entry.

Amaury walked to him and placed the smooth river stone in his palm. He closed the Count’s cold fingers around it. “It wants you to finish the story. Out loud. In the square. With the dust of your tower on your tongue.”

The Count looked at the stone. A single, clean tear cut a path through the grime on his cheek. It was the first honest thing he had produced in decades. He nodded.

The hook was not in the confession to come, or the town’s judgement, or the phantom’s final peace. It was in the walk from the ruined church to the marketplace. It was in the three of them—the ruined lord, the faith-shaken priest, the mason who read ghosts—forming a grim procession. It was in the way the people fell silent as they emerged, not with fury, but with a weary, bone-deep curiosity. The tower was gone. The Tally was broken. A new, terrifying ledger was about to be opened, one whose currency was not coin, but consequence.

Guibert climbed the steps of the market cross, the river stone clutched to his breast. He opened his mouth. The first word was not a number. It was a name.

And as the name—a simple, forgotten name of a woman who had loved and been loved—hung in the thick, dust-laden air, Amaury felt the journal in his hand grow still. The final, silent tension bled away. The account, at last, was settling.

But as the Count’s halting, unbearable story unfolded, a story of a love that dared not speak its name and a death that paved a path to heaven, Amaury looked past the crowd, towards the river gate.

A figure stood there, vague in the lingering haze. Not a phantom of stone and rage, but a suggestion of a form, a quietude of posture. It watched for a moment, as the mortal love was finally spoken into mortality.

Then it turned, and walked, not into the river, but into the soft, grey light of the morning, dissolving like a sigh of release.

The debt was paid. The principal of the heart, returned.

In the silence that followed the Count’s last, broken syllable, Amaury looked down at the open journal. The ink of the final sketch seemed to blur, to soften, as if the page itself was letting go. The cross-hatching over the long box faded, and beneath it, he could now see what the mason, in his guilt, had tried to obscure: not an angry scrawl, but a delicate, careful tracing of two interlocking rings. A union. A vow. Written not in stone, or ink, but in the negative space between lines.

The hook for the silence after the story was this: the phantom had its truth. The town had its reckoning. But Amaury held the proof. And proof, in a world learning to live without towers, was a dangerous, weighty thing. He closed the book. The sound was the softest of finalities.

He looked at Lacroix, who stared at the empty space by the river gate, his faith now a different, more complicated architecture. He looked at Guibert, hollowed out, empty, finally balanced.

And he knew, with a certainty that chilled him more than any ghost, that some accounts are so vast, their settlement leaves a void. And nature, like a ledger-keeper, abhors a void.

Something else would come to fill it.
